Amm and Khass — General and Specific

Definition of Command (Amr) and Its Specificity to the Imperative Form
Defines 'amr' and argues it is specific to the imperative form with Hanafi proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi definition of 'amr' (command), explain why it is specific to the imperative form, and evaluate the dispute over whether the Prophet's actions are obligating.

Core Idea
The command (amr) is specifically the imperative form 'if'al'; the Prophet's actions are not commands and require separate evidence for obligation.

In this passage from Al-Shashi's Usul, we encounter a fundamental Hanafi position: the term 'amr' (command) is defined strictly as the imperative formula. This has far-reaching implications, especially regarding the legal weight of the Prophet's actions. We will explore the definition, the arguments for specificity, and the counter-arguments from other schools.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of amr
The passage defines 'command' as a saying of a speaker to another using the imperative form 'if'al', signifying a demand for obligation. This is the lexical and technical Hanafi definition.
The definition excludes prohibitions, self-commands, and mere statements of obligation.
2
Distinguish between the reality and the intended meaning
The author refutes the claim that 'amr' is literally only the imperative form, because Allah's pre-eternal speech includes command without form. Rather, the intended meaning is obligation (wujub) upon the servant, which can be established without the formula.
Example: faith is obligatory even for those who never received revelation.
3
Identify the exclusions from the definition
The definition explicitly excludes the Prophet's actions, self-commands, prohibitions, and statements like 'you must do such and such' (which are khabar, not amr).
This is a key point of ikhtilaf with other schools.
4
Analyze the dispute about Prophetic actions
Some Shafi'is and Malikis argue that the Prophet's actions are obligating either because they are commands or share the ruling of commands. The Hanafi response cites the hadith of the sandals: the Prophet disapproved of people imitating his action, proving that actions alone do not obligate.
Hanafis require evidence of constancy (muwadaba) and absence of specificity for an action to be obligating.
5
Apply the distinction between rational and legal obligation
Legal obligations (shar'iyyat) only become apparent through the imperative form, whereas rational obligations (like belief in God) do not require a verbal command. This shows that the imperative is not the only source of obligation, but it is the primary indicator for legal rulings.
This affects how later scholars derive rulings from non-imperative texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البحث الأول في كتاب الله فصل في الأمر وفي الشرع تصرف إلزام الفعل على الغير على سبيل الاستعلاء وذكر بعض الأئمة أن المراد بالأمر يختص بهذه الصيغة واستحال أن يكون معناه أن حقيقة الأمر يختص بهذه الصيغة فإن الله تعالى متكلّم في الأزل عندنا وكلامه أمر ونهي وإخبار واستخبار واستحال وجود هذه الصيغة في الأزل واستحال أيضا أن يكون معناه أن المراد بالأمر للآمر يختص بهذه الصيغة فإن المراد للشارع بالأمر وجوب الفعل على العبد وهو معنى الابتلاء عندنا وقد ثبت الوجوب بدون هذه الصيغة أليس أنه وجب الإيمان على من لم تبلغه الدعوة بدون ورود السمع شيء وهو المأمور به ولا بد من مكلف ليجب عليه وهو المأمور أو بالأمر لا يجب شيء على الأمر بل على المأمور وهذا الأمر لوجوب فعل على العبد وفعله لا بد أن يقع في زمان وهو المأمور فيه قول القائل لغيره أي من الخاص الأمر يعني مسمى الأمر لا لفظه لأنه يصدق عليه أنه لفظ وضع لمعنى معلوم وهو الطلب على الوجوب والقول مصدر يراد به المقول لأن الأمر من أقسام الألفاظ وهو جنس يشتمل كل لفظ وبقي فيه النهي فخرج بقوله افعل وهي صيغة طلب الفعل مشهورة ومعروفة قيد به ليخرج به ليفعل فإنه لا يقال له أمر بلا قيد بل يقال له أمر الغائب بقيد الإضافة واحترز بقوله قول القائل عن فعل النبي عليه السلام فإنه لا يسمى أمرا عندنا وبقوله الغيره عن الأمر لنفسه نحو قوله تعالى وَلْتَحْمِلْ خَطَايَاكُمْ وبقوله افعل عن النهي وعن قول من هو مفترض الطاعة أو وجب عليك أن تفعل كذا فإنه ليس بأمر بل هو إخبار عن الإيجاب خلافا لبعض أصحاب الشافعي فإنهم يقولون إن فعل النبي عليه السلام أيضا موجب إما لأنه أمر وكل أمر للوجوب وإما لأنه مشارك للأمر القولي في حكم الوجوب كذا في الحصول وقد ثبت الوجوب في الفروع الشرعية الموقوفة على الشرع أيضا بدون هذه الصيغة كما في قوله تعالى كُتِبَ عَلَيْكُمُ الصِّيَامُ وقوله وَكُتِبَ عَلَيْكُمُ الْقِتَالُ وقوله الجهاد ماض قال أبو حنيفة رحمه الله لو لم يبعث الله تعالى رسولا لوجب على العقلاء معرفته بعقولهم فيحمل ذلك على أن المراد بالأمر يختص بهذه الصيغة في حق العبد في الشرعيات حتى لا يكون فعل الرسول الله بمنزلة قوله افعلوا ولا يلزم اعتقاد الوجوب به والمتابعة في أفعاله إنما تجب عند المواظبة وانتفاء دليل الاختصاص ثم قال في الشرعيات يعني أن الوجوب علينا في التكليفات التي وجبت بالشرع فقط غير التي وجبت بالعقل كالإيمان بالله تعالى وصفاته لا يظهر لنا إلا بصيغة الأمر وإن كان الإيجاب من الله تعالى في الأزل لا يتوقف على صيغة الأمر ومعنى الاختصاص يظهر في أن فعل الرسول الله لا يكون موجبا عندنا خلافا لبعض أصحاب الشافعي ومالك رحمهما لما روي أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال صلوا كما رأيتموني أصلي وهذا تصريح بالمتابعة في فعله ولنا أنه عليه السلام خلع نعليه في الصلاة فخلع الناس نعالهم فقال منكرا عليهم ما لكم خلعتم نعالكم فقالوا رأيناك خلعت فقال أتاني جبريل عليه السلام آنفا وأخبرني أن في نعليك أذى الحديث فلو كان المتابعة في فعله واجبا عليهم لما أنكر عليهم وأما قوله صلى الله عليه وسلم صلوا كما رأيتموني أصلي فالمتابعة قيد بلفظ الأمر لا بالفعل كذا في الفصول
The first research on the Book of Allah. Chapter on the command. In Shari'ah, it is a disposal imposing an action upon another in a manner of supremacy. Some imams mentioned that the intended meaning of 'command' is specific to this formula (if'al). It is impossible that its meaning is that the reality of command is specific to this formula, because Allah is a speaker in pre-eternity according to us, and His speech is command, prohibition, information, and inquiry, and it is impossible for this formula to exist in pre-eternity. It is also impossible that its meaning is that the intended meaning of command for the commander is specific to this formula. Indeed, the intended meaning of the Lawgiver by command is the obligation of the action upon the servant, and that is the meaning of trial according to us. Obligation has been established without this formula; is it not that faith was obligatory upon one whom the call did not reach without the arrival of revelation? Something is the commanded thing, and there must be a legally responsible person for it to be obligatory upon him, that is the commanded person. By the command, nothing is obligatory upon the commander but upon the commanded. This command is for the obligation of an action upon the servant, and its occurrence must take place in a time, which is the 'time of commanding'. The saying of a speaker to another: (he said) 'specific' means the named of command, not its wording, because it is true of it that it is a word placed for a known meaning, which is a demand for obligation. 'Saying' is an infinitive by which the said is intended, because command is among the divisions of words. It is a genus that includes every word, and prohibition remains excluded. So by his saying 'if'al' it is excluded; it is the well-known formula of demanding an action. It is restricted by it to exclude 'liyaf'al', for it is not called a command without a restriction; rather it is called 'command of the absent' with the restriction of addition. He guarded by his saying 'the speaker' against the action of the Prophet (peace be upon him), for it is not called a command according to us. And by his saying 'to another' against the command for oneself, like His saying 'And you will bear your sins'. And by his saying 'if'al' against prohibition and against the saying of one whose obedience is obligatory or 'you must do such and such', for it is not a command but an information about obligation, contrary to some Shafi'i followers, for they say that the action of the Prophet (peace be upon him) is also obligating, either because it is a command and every command is for obligation, or because it shares with the verbal command in the ruling of obligation. This is in al-Husul. And obligation has been established in the subsidiary legal rulings that depend on the Law also without this formula, as in His saying 'Fasting is prescribed for you' and His saying 'Fighting is prescribed for you' and his saying 'Jihad continues'. Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him) said: If Allah had not sent a messenger, it would be obligatory upon the rational to know Him by their intellects. So that is interpreted as that the intended meaning of command is specific to this formula in the right of the servant in the legal matters, so that the action of the Messenger of Allah is not equivalent to his saying 'do', and belief in obligation through it is not necessary, and following his actions is only obligatory when there is constancy and absence of evidence of specificity. Then he said: 'In the legal matters' means that the obligation upon us in the obligations that are only established by the Law, not those established by reason such as belief in Allah and His attributes, does not appear to us except through the formula of command, even though the imposition of obligation from Allah in pre-eternity does not depend on the formula of command. And the meaning of specificity appears in that the action of the Messenger of Allah is not obligating according to us, contrary to some Shafi'i and Maliki followers, due to what is narrated that the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Pray as you have seen me praying.' This is an explicit statement of following his action. And for us is that he (peace be upon him) took off his sandals in prayer, so the people took off their sandals. He said to them disapprovingly: 'Why did you take off your sandals?' They said: 'We saw you take off.' He said: 'Gabriel came to me just now and informed me that there was impurity in your sandals' – the hadith. So if following his action had been obligatory upon them, he would not have disapproved of them. As for his saying: 'Pray as you have seen me praying,' following is tied to the verbal command, not the action. This is in al-Fusul.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage is a definition and analysis of the term 'amr' (command) in Islamic jurisprudence, specifically within the Hanafi school. It begins by stating the Shari'ah meaning of command: an imposition of an action upon another in a superior manner. Some imams claimed that command is exclusively tied to the imperative formula 'if'al' (do). However, the author argues that this cannot be literally true because Allah's speech in pre-eternity includes command without the formula. Instead, the intended meaning of command from the Lawgiver is obligation of the action upon the servant, which is the essence of trial. Obligation can be established without the formula, as in the case of faith being obligatory for one who never received the call. The passage then defines 'command' as the saying of a speaker to another, using the word 'if'al', excluding the action of the Prophet (which is not called a command in the Hanafi school), self-commands, prohibitions, and merely informative statements of obligation. It then addresses the view of some Shafi'is who consider the Prophet's actions as obligating, either because they are commands or share the ruling of commands. The author refutes this by citing a hadith where the Prophet disapproved of people imitating his action of removing sandals in prayer, indicating that his actions are not automatically obligatory unless accompanied by a verbal command or evidence of continuity and lack of specificity. The passage concludes by distinguishing obligations derived from reason (like belief in God) from those derived from law, asserting that the formula of command is necessary only for the latter. This discussion highlights a key usul difference: for Hanafis, the Prophet's actions do not inherently impose obligation; they require additional indicators.

Key Terms
الأمر (al-amr): The command: a saying of a speaker to another using the imperative form 'if'al', signifying a demand for obligation.
الصيغة (al-sigha): The formula: specifically the imperative form 'if'al' (do) used to express a command.
الوجوب (al-wujub): Obligation: the legal requirement that an action be performed, established either by reason or by divine law.
المتابعة (al-mutaba'a): Following: imitating the Prophet's actions. It is obligatory only when there is constancy (muwadaba) and absence of evidence of specificity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view presented in this passage, why is the Prophet's action of removing his sandals in prayer not considered a command that obligates imitation?
A) Because the Prophet explicitly forbade following his actions.
B) Because the Prophet disapproved of the people imitating him, showing that his action alone is not a command.
C) Because the action was specific to the Prophet and not meant for others.
D) Because the Prophet later revoked that action.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, 'amr' is strictly the imperative form 'if'al'; actions alone, even the Prophet's, do not create obligation without additional evidence.

The Absolute Command: Dispute Over Its Implication
Examining the scholarly disagreement on the meaning of absolute command

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on the absolute command and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument using the concept of suspension (tawaqquf).

Core Idea
The absolute command indicates obligation by default; suspension is rejected by the Hanafi school.

This lesson examines a fundamental usul dispute: what does a command without context imply? The Hanafi school holds that it indicates obligation (wujub) unless there is evidence otherwise, while Ibn al-Sharih and others argue for suspension (tawaqquf) due to multiple possible meanings. We will analyze the proofs on both sides and see why the Hanafi position is preferred.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Articulate the Hanafi ruling: the absolute command indicates obligation unless a contrary evidence (dalil) emerges. The reasoning is that abandoning a command is disobedience (ma'siya) and compliance is obedience (ta'a).
2
Identify the opposing view and its proponent
Ibn al-Sharih (a Shafi'i scholar) holds that the absolute command requires suspension (tawaqquf) because it is a lexical homonym used for many literal meanings.
Al-Ghazali also holds suspension but focuses on the word's coinage.
3
Examine the opponent's proof
The command is used in many senses: recommendation, rebuke, incapacitation, etc. Each usage is literal by agreement, so without context, the meaning is ambiguous. Probability necessitates suspension.
Examples from Quran are cited in the source.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The majority of scholars hold that the command has a specific meaning. The multiple usages are either figurative or require context, but the default (asl) is obligation. The burden of proof is on those claiming otherwise.
The Hanafi proof is based on the consequence of disobedience.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
In fiqh application, Hanafis presume obligation from any absolute command until proven otherwise (e.g., fasting is obligatory). Suspensionists withhold judgment until further evidence clarifies the intent.
This affects how rulings are derived from primary texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الأمر المطلق: اختلف الناس في الأمر المطلق أي: المجرد عن القرينة الدالة على اللزوم وعدم اللزوم نحو: قوله تعالى: ﴿وَإِذَا قُرِئَ الْقُرْآنُ فَاسْتَمِعُوا لَهُ وَأَنْصِتُوا لَعَلَّكُمْ تُرْحَمُونَ﴾، وقوله تعالى: ﴿وَلا تَقْرَبَا هَذه الشَّجَرَةَ فَتَكُونَا مِنَ الظَّالمين﴾، والصحيح من المذهب: أن الوجوبُ إلا إذا قام الدليل على خلافه؛ لأن ترك الأمر معصية كما أن الائتمار طاعة. = المراد، وهي موجبه الأمر أي وجوب الفعل كنكاح تسع، وكوجوب التهجد والضحى ثم المراد بالمواظبة من غير ترك، وإلا فالمواظبة المطلقة دليل على السنة، أما المواظبة من غير ترك دليل على الوجوب بصيغة فهو واجب استدلالي بطريق أنه لو لم يكن واجباً لتركه مرة تعليماً للجواز، ولما لم يترك قط في حياته علم أنه كان واجباً أمره بصيغة الوجوب كذا في "المعدن". اختلف الناس إلخ فذهب ابن الشريح من أصحاب الشافعي إلى أن موجبه التوقف؛ لأنه يستعمل في معان كثيرة بعضها حقيقة اتفاقاً، فعند الإطلاق يكون محتملاً لمعان كثيرة والاحتمال يوجب التوقف إلى أن يبين الندب كقوله تعالى: ﴿فَكَاتِبُوهُمْ﴾، والتوبيخ كقوله تعالى: ﴿اعْمَلُوا مَا شِئْتُمْ﴾، والتعجيز كقوله تعالى: ﴿فَأْتُوا بِسُورَةٍ مِّنْ مِثْلِهِ﴾، والإرشاد كقوله تعالى: ﴿وَاسْتَشْهِدُوا﴾، والامتنان كقوله تعالى: ﴿كُلُوا مِمَّا رَزَقَكُمُ اللهُ﴾، والإكرام نحو: ﴿ادْخُلُوهَا بِسَلامٍ آمِنِينَ﴾، والتأديب نحو: "كل مما يليك"، والتخيير نحو: ﴿كُونُوا قِرَدَةً خَاسِئِين﴾، والإهانة نحو: ﴿وذُقْ إِنَّكَ أَنتَ الْعَزِيزُ الْكَرِيمُ﴾، والدعا نحو: ﴿ربنا اغفر لي﴾، والاحتقار نحو: ﴿أَلْقُوا مَا أَنْتُمْ مُلْقُونَ﴾، وغير ذلك، ثم اعلم التوقف عنده في تعيين المراد عند الاستعمال لا في تعيين الموضوع له؛ لأنه عنده موضوع بالاشتراك اللفظي للوجوب والندب والإباحة والتهديد وذهب الغزالي وجماعة من المحققين إلى التوقف في تعيين الموضوع له أنه الوجوب فقط، أو الندب فقط، أو مشترك بينهما لفظاً، وعامة العلماء على أنه خاص للمعنى المخصوص، فقال بعضهم: إنه للندب؛ لأنه موضوع لطلب الفعل، وأدنى ما يترجح به جانب الوجود وهو الندب، والصحيح من القول: إنه للوجوب كما هو المذكور في المتن هذا ملخص كتب الأصول. والصحيح من المذهب إلخ: هذا هو قول الجمهور من الفقهاء وجماعة من المعتزلة كأبي الحسين البصري والجبائي في أحد قوليه [حاشية الشيخ أكرم الندوي: ص ٩٦]
Chapter on the Absolute Command: The people differed concerning the absolute command, i.e., the one stripped of any contextual indication (qarina) showing necessity or non-necessity, such as His statement, the Exalted: 'And when the Qur'an is recited, listen to it and be silent that you may receive mercy' (7:204), and His statement, the Exalted: 'And do not approach this tree, lest you be among the wrongdoers' (2:35). The correct view in the school is that it indicates obligation unless evidence establishes otherwise; because abandoning the command is disobedience just as complying is obedience. = The intended meaning, and it is that which obliges the command, i.e., the obligation of the act, like the prohibition of marrying nine wives, and like the obligation of tahajjud and duha. Then the intended meaning of persistence without abandonment; otherwise absolute persistence is evidence of sunna, but persistence without abandonment is evidence of obligation indicated by a formula; so it is an indicative obligation (wajib istidlali) by way that if it were not obligatory, he would have left it once to teach permissibility, and since he never left it in his life, it is known that it was obligatory, commanded by the formula of obligation, as in al-Ma'din. 'The people differed' etc.: Ibn al-Sharih from the followers of al-Shafi'i went that it requires suspension (tawaqquf) because it is used in many meanings, some of which are literal by agreement. So when absolute, it is probable for many meanings, and probability necessitates suspension until it is clarified—like recommendation in His statement: 'So write them' (24:33), and rebuke in His statement: 'Do whatever you will' (41:40), and inability in His statement: 'Then bring a surah like it' (2:23), and guidance in His statement: 'And seek witnesses' (2:282), and favor in His statement: 'Eat from what Allah has provided for you' (16:114), and honor like 'Enter it in peace, secure' (15:46), and discipline like 'Eat from what is before you', and choice like 'Be apes, despised' (2:65), and humiliation like 'Taste! Indeed, you are the mighty, the honorable' (44:49), and supplication like 'Our Lord, forgive me' (14:41), and contempt like 'Throw what you will throw' (10:80), and other things. Then know that the suspension (tawaqquf) according to him is in determining the intended meaning in usage, not in determining what it is coined for; because according to him it is coined with lexical homonymy (ishtirak lafzi) for obligation, recommendation, permission, and threat. And al-Ghazali and a group of investigators went to suspension in determining what it is coined for: whether it is only for obligation, or only for recommendation, or a lexical homonym between the two. And the generality of scholars hold that it is specific to a particular meaning. Some said it is for recommendation because it is coined to request an action, and the least that can be favored is the side of existence, which is recommendation. The correct statement is that it is for obligation, as mentioned in the text. This is a summary of the books of usul. 'The correct in the school' etc.: This is the statement of the majority of jurists and a group of the Mu'tazila like Abu al-Husayn al-Basri and al-Jubba'i in one of his two opinions. [Hashiyat al-Shaykh Akram al-Nadwi: p. 96]
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage is a systematic presentation of the usul dispute concerning the meaning of the absolute command (الأمر المطلق) — a command devoid of any contextual clue (قرينة) indicating whether it is for obligation, recommendation, etc. The Hanafi correct position is stated first: the absolute command indicates obligation (الوجوب) unless a contrary indicator appears. The reasoning is that abandoning a command is disobedience (معصية), and compliance is obedience (طاعة), which aligns with obligation. The text then details the views of others: Ibn al-Sharih (a Shafi'i) holds that the absolute command requires suspension (توقف) because it can be used in many different literal senses, such as recommendation, rebuke, incapacitation, etc. He believes the word is a lexical homonym (مشترك لفظي) for multiple meanings, so one must wait for clarification. Al-Ghazali and others also hold suspension but more specifically about what the word is coined for (whether obligation alone, recommendation alone, or both). The majority of scholars, however, hold that the command is for a specific meaning. Among them, some say it is for recommendation (الندب) because it requests an action and the minimal preference is toward existence. But the correct and relied-upon view in the Hanafi school is that it is for obligation (الوجوب), as stated in the matn. The passage also includes a marginal note identifying this as the view of the majority of jurists and some Mu'tazila. The discussion of persistence (مواظبة) in the first part relates to how continuous action can indicate obligation in a derivative manner (وجوب استدلالي).

Key Terms
الأمر المطلق (al-amr al-mutlaq): The command stripped of any contextual indication (قرينة) showing whether it implies obligation, recommendation, etc.
التوقف (al-tawaqquf): Suspension of judgment; not assigning a meaning until further evidence clarifies
الوجوب (al-wujub): Legal obligation; the command demands compliance and leaving it is sinful

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the correct view in the Hanafi school, what does an absolute command (الأمر المطلق) indicate?
A) It indicates recommendation (الندب) because it requests an action
B) It requires suspension (التوقف) until context clarifies
C) It indicates obligation (الوجوب) unless evidence shows otherwise
D) It is a lexical homonym for multiple meanings

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that an absolute command indicates obligation unless proven otherwise, placing the burden of proof on the one claiming a different meaning.

The Absolute Command Indicates Obligation
Analyzing the meaning of the absolute command and its implication

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the absolute command (al-amr al-mutlaq) and state the Hanafi position that it indicates obligation, distinguishing it from the opposing views that require suspension or recommend.

Core Idea
The absolute command in Hanafi usul indicates obligation by default; alternative meanings require evidence.

In this lesson, we dissect a foundational principle of Hanafi usul al-fiqh: the nature of the absolute command. The text explores the default legal implication of an imperative form when stripped of contextual clues. By the end, you will understand why the Hanafi school holds that such commands obligate action, and how this differs from other schools.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term and its definition
Locate the definition of 'absolute command' (al-amr al-mutlaq) in the text: the imperative form stripped of any contextual indicator (qarina) that would specify obligation or otherwise.
The text gives examples: 'When the Quran is recited, listen' and 'Do not approach this tree.'
2
State the Hanafi position and its proof
The text states: 'The correct position of the school is that it indicates obligation unless evidence to the contrary exists.' The proof is that abandoning a command is disobedience (ma'siya), and disobedience deserves punishment, which only occurs for abandoning an obligatory act.
This proof combines linguistic custom (urf) and rational analogy.
3
Compare with the opposing view of Ibn al-Sharih
Ibn al-Sharih (Shafi'i) argues that the imperative has many possible meanings, so it requires suspension (tawaquf) until clarification. The text lists many uses: recommendation, rebuke, incapacitation, etc.
The Hanafi response: these are secondary uses requiring a qarina; the default literal meaning is obligation.
4
Analyze the rational proof based on authority
The text argues that the degree of necessary compliance depends on the authority (wilaya) of the commander. If a human master can compel obedience under pain of punishment, Allah's authority is even greater, so His absolute command must imply obligation.
This is a dalil aqli (rational proof) supported by Quranic verses like 'Whoever disobeys Allah...'
5
Note the broader meaning of 'obligation' here
The text clarifies that 'obligation' (wujub) in this context includes both fard (certain) and wajib (less certain) in Hanafi terminology, as punishment attaches to abandoning either.
This is a nuance specific to this usul discussion.

اختلف الناس في الأمر المطلق أي: المجرد عن القرينة الدالة على اللزوم وعدم اللزوم نحو: قوله تعالى: ﴿وَإِذَا قُرِئَ الْقُرْآنُ فَاسْتَمِعُوا لَهُ وَأَنْصِتُوا لَعَلَّكُمْ تُرْحَمُونَ﴾، وقوله تعالى: ﴿وَلا تَقْرَبَا هَذه الشَّجَرَةَ فَتَكُونَا مِنَ الظَّالمين﴾، والصحيح من المذهب: أن الوجوبُ إلا إذا قام الدليل على خلافه؛ لأن ترك الأمر معصية كما أن الائتمار طاعة.

المراد، وهي

الأمر
أي وجوب الفعل كنكاح تسع، وكوجوب التهجد والضحى ثم المراد بالمواظبة من غير ترك، وإلا فالمواظبة المطلقة دليل على السنة، أما المواظبة من غير ترك دليل على الوجوب بصيغة فهو واجب استدلالي بطريق أنه لو لم يكن واجباً لتركه مرة تعليماً للجواز، ولما لم يترك قط في حياته علم أنه كان واجباً أمره بصيغة الوجوب كذا في "المعدن". اختلف الناس إلخ فذهب ابن الشريح من أصحاب الشافعي إلى أن موجبه التوقف؛ لأنه يستعمل في معان كثيرة بعضها حقيقة اتفاقاً، فعند الإطلاق يكون محتملاً لمعان كثيرة والاحتمال يوجب التوقف إلى أن يبين الندب كقوله تعالى: ﴿فَكَاتِبُوهُمْ﴾، والتوبيخ كقوله تعالى: ﴿اعْمَلُوا مَا شِئْتُمْ﴾، والتعجيز كقوله تعالى: ﴿فَأْتُوا بِسُورَةٍ مِّنْ مِثْلِهِ﴾، والإرشاد كقوله تعالى: ﴿وَاسْتَشْهِدُوا﴾، والامتنان كقوله تعالى: ﴿كُلُوا مِمَّا رَزَقَكُمُ اللهُ﴾، والإكرام نحو: ﴿ادْخُلُوهَا بِسَلامٍ آمِنِينَ﴾، والتأديب نحو: "كل مما يليك:"، والتخيير نحو: ﴿كُونُوا قِرَدَةً خَاسِئِينَ﴾، والإهانة نحو: ﴿وَذُقْ إِنَّكَ أَنتَ الْعَزِيزُ الْكَرِيمُ﴾، والدعا نحو: ﴿رَبَّنَا اغْفِرْ لِي﴾، والاحتقار نحو: ﴿أَلْقُوا مَا أَنْتُمْ مُلْقُونَ﴾، وغير ذلك، ثم اعلم التوقف عنده في تعيين المراد عند الاستعمال لا في تعيين الموضوع له؛ لأنه عنده موضوع بالاشتراك اللفظي للوجوب والندب والإباحة والتهديد وذهب الغزالي وجماعة من المحققين إلى التوقف في تعيين الموضوع له أنه الوجوب فقط، أو الندب فقط، أو مشترك بينهما لفظاً، وعامة العلماء على أنه خاص للمعنى المخصوص، فقال بعضهم: إنه للندب؛ لأنه موضوع لطلب الفعل، وأدنى ما يترجح به جانب الوجود وهو الندب، والصحيح من القول: إنه للوجوب كما هو المذكور في المتن هذا ملخص كتب الأصول. والصحيح من المذهب إلخ: هذا هو قول الجمهور من الفقهاء وجماعة من المعتزلة كأبي الحسين البصري والجبائي في أحد قوليه [حاشية الشيخ أكرم الندوي: ص ٩٦]

للائتمار، وإذا وجهتها إلى من يلزمه طاعتك من العَبيد لَزمه الائتمار لامحالة، حتى لو ترکه اختياراً يستحق العقاب عرفاً وشرعاً.

أحدهما
طَاوَعُوْك: أي التمروك، ويسمى الائتمار طاعة أي إن طاوعوك في قطع محبتهم عن أحبتهم فطاوعيهم أي: ائتمري آمريك في صرم حبلي. فاعْصي: أي أتركي أمر من ترك أمرك ويسمى ترك الأمر معصية. سبب للعقاب : لقوله تعالى: ﴿وَمَنْ يَعْصِ الله﴾، والعقاب لا يترتب إلا بترك الواجب والفرض. [عمدة الحواشي: ص ۷۸] وتحقيقه إلخ: أي تحقيق أن مقتضى الأمر مع عزل اللحظ عن مقتضیات خصوص القرائن الصارفة، وخصوصيات المواد وهو الوجوب، وأن مخالفة الأمر لما سميت في العرف معصية كان مقتضى الأمر وموجبه الوجوب؛ لأن معصية الله تعالى سبب موجب لاستحقاق العاصي العذاب، وإذا كان ضده موجباً للعقاب كان الجانب الموافق واجباً؛ لأن امتناع أحد الجانبين مستلزم لوجوب الآخر، وكذا وجوب لامتناع الآخر، وحاصل التحقيق: أن لزوم امتثال أمر الأمر على المأمور المخاطب يكون على قدر اختيار الأمر على المأمور وعلوه وقدرته عليه وعلى قدر هذه الولاية يكون قدر استحقاق العقوبة في مخالفته، فإذا كان مخالفة أمر المولى موجبة لاستحقاق عقوبة عظيمة في حق عبده مع أنه لم يخلقه ولم يوجد بدنه ونفسه وأعضاؤه، والنعم السابقة واللاحقة، وإنما ملكه رقبة ملكاً ناقصاً غير حقيقي ومع ذلك هو قابل الزوال والفناء كان مخالفة أمر الله تعالى وهو مالك ذرات العالم وخالقها ومالكها ملكاً تاماً حقيقياً موجبة لاستحقاق العقوبة بالطريق الأولى، فيكون موجب أمره هو الوجوب؛ لأن هو الوجوب؛ لأن معنى الوجوب ههنا أعم الوجوب ههنا أعم من الفرض والوجوب، ولا عقاب إلا في تركها، فتذكر كذا في بعض الحواشي.
بقدر ولاية الآمر: يعني إذا كان الأمر عالياً كان الائتمار واجباً، وإذا كان مساوياً يكون مندوباً، وإذا كان سافلاً لا يكون واجباً ولا مندوباً بل مباحاً كذا في "غاية التحقيق". وشرعاً: قال تعالى: ﴿وَمَا كَانَ لِمُؤْمِنٍ وَلَا
مُؤْمِنَةٍ إِذَا قَضَى اللَّهُ وَرَسُولُهُ أَمْراً أَنْ يَكُونَ لَهُمُ الْخِيَرَةُ مِنْ أَمْرِهِمْ﴾ معنى ذلك: أن الله ورسوله إذا حكماً
The first research in the Book of Allah (83). Section on the Absolute Command from the Mujtahids. Section on the Absolute Command: People differed regarding the absolute command, i.e., stripped of any contextual indication indicating obligation or non-obligation, like His saying: 'When the Quran is recited, listen to it and be silent that you may receive mercy' (7:204), and His saying: 'Do not approach this tree, lest you become among the wrongdoers' (2:35). The correct position of the school is that it indicates obligation unless evidence to the contrary exists, because abandoning a command is disobedience just as complying is obedience. (clarification) The intended meaning of 'its implication' is obligation of the act, like marrying nine women, and the obligation of tahajjud and duha. Then what is meant by 'continuous practice without omission' is that continuous practice in absolute terms indicates sunna, while continuous practice without omission indicates obligation by form (sigha). It is an inferred obligation (ijtihad) by way that if it were not obligatory, he would have left it once to teach permissibility, but since he never left it in his life, it is known that it was obligatory. This is mentioned in al-Ma'din. 'People differed' etc. Ibn al-Sharih from the followers of al-Shafi'i went that its implication is suspension (tawaquf) because the command is used in many meanings, some of which are agreed upon as literal. So when absolute, it is ambiguous, and ambiguity necessitates suspension until clarification of recommendation (nadb) as in His saying: 'So write for them' (24:33), and rebuke as in 'Do as you wish' (41:40), and incapacitation as in 'Then bring a surah like it' (2:23), and guidance as in 'And call to witness' (2:282), and favor as in 'Eat of what Allah has provided you' (6:142), and honor as in 'Enter it in peace, secure' (15:46), and discipline as in 'Eat from what is in front of you' (hadith), and choice as in 'Be apes, despised' (2:65), and humiliation as in 'Taste! Indeed you are the mighty, the honorable' (44:49), and supplication as in 'Our Lord, forgive me' (14:41), and contempt as in 'Throw what you have to throw' (10:80), and others. Then know: suspension according to him is about determining the intended meaning in usage, not about determining the assigned meaning (wad' al-lafz) because according to him it is assigned (by verbal homonymy) for obligation, recommendation, permissibility, and threat. Al-Ghazali and a group of researchers went to suspension in determining the assigned meaning: whether it is obligation only, recommendation only, or common between them verbally. The general body of scholars holds that it is specific to a particular meaning, so some said it is for recommendation because it is placed for requesting the act, and the least that can tilt towards existence is recommendation. The correct statement is that it is for obligation as mentioned in the text. This is a summary of the books of usul. 'The correct of the school etc.': This is the statement of the majority of jurists and a group of Mu'tazila like Abu al-Husayn al-Basri and al-Jubba'i in one of his two statements. [Footnote of Shaykh Akram al-Nadwi: p. 96]. (Continuation) The first research in the Book of Allah (84). Section on the Absolute Command. Al-Hamasi said: I obeyed your command to sever my rope. Command them regarding their loved ones with that. So if they obey you, then obey them, and if they disobey you, then disobey whoever disobeyed you. Disobedience with respect to the right of the Shari'a is a cause for punishment. Its verification is that the necessity of compliance is only to the extent of the authority of the commander over the addressee. For this, if the command form is directed to someone who is not obligated to obey you at all, it does not necessitate compliance. And if directed to someone who is obligated to obey you, like slaves, they must comply inevitably, such that if they abandon it willingly, they deserve punishment customarily and legally. Explanation: 'they obey you' means they comply with you, and compliance is called obedience, i.e., if they obey you in cutting off their affection from their loved ones, then obey them, i.e., comply with your commander in severing my rope. 'Disobey' means leave the command of one who left your command, and leaving a command is called disobedience. 'Cause for punishment' due to His saying: 'Whoever disobeys Allah' (Quran), and punishment results only from abandoning obligation and duty. [Umdat al-Hawashi: p. 78]. 'Its verification etc.': i.e., verification that the demand of the command, when one abstracts from specific contextual indications and particular material, is obligation. And since violating the command is called disobedience in custom, the demand and implication of the command is obligation. Because disobedience to Allah is a cause necessitating the disobedient's deserving punishment. And if its opposite is a cause for punishment, then the compliant side is obligatory, because the prohibition of one side necessitates the obligation of the other, and vice versa. The summary of the verification: The necessity of obeying the commander on the addressee is according to the authority of the commander over the addressee, his loftiness and power over him. And accordingly is the extent of deserved punishment for violation. So if violation of the command of a master necessitates deserving great punishment for his slave, even though the master did not create him nor bring into existence his body, soul, and limbs, and the previous and subsequent blessings, and only owns him by imperfect, non-real ownership that is subject to loss and annihilation, then violation of the command of Allah, who is the owner of the particles of the world, their Creator and Owner with complete real ownership, necessitates deserving punishment with greater reason. Thus the implication of His command is obligation, because the meaning of obligation here is more general than fard and wajib, and punishment only occurs for abandoning it. So remember. This is from some glosses. 'To the extent of the authority of the commander': means if the commander is superior, compliance is obligatory; if equal, it is recommended; if inferior, it is neither obligatory nor recommended but permissible. This is from Ghayat al-Tahqiq. 'And legally': Allah said: 'It is not for a believing man or believing woman, when Allah and His Messenger have decreed a matter, to have any choice in their matter' (33:36). The meaning is that when Allah and His Messenger decree, they have no choice.
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage is a discussion on the nature of the absolute command (al-amr al-mutlaq) in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. The text begins by stating the correct Hanafi position: the imperative form, when not accompanied by any contextual indicator (qarina) that would specify obligation or otherwise, indicates obligation (wujub) by default. This is because abandoning such a command is considered disobedience (ma'siya), and disobedience invites punishment, which only attaches to obligatory acts. The text then offers a detailed analysis of different scholarly positions. Ibn al-Sharih, a Shafi'i scholar, held that the absolute command requires suspension (tawaquf) because the command form is used in many senses (literal and metaphorical), so its meaning is ambiguous until clarified. Al-Ghazali and others suspended judgment regarding the primary assigned meaning (wad') of the command: whether it is solely for obligation, solely for recommendation, or a homonym. The majority of scholars, however, hold that it is specific to a particular meaning, with some arguing for recommendation (nadb). The text emphatically adopts the Hanafi position that it is for obligation, supported by the fact that the Prophet (peace be upon him) never neglected a command meant for obligation, and leaving a command is called disobedience. The subsequent pages provide a rational proof (dalil aqli) based on authority (wilaya): the degree of required compliance depends on the authority of the commander. If a human master, with imperfect ownership, can enforce obedience under pain of punishment, then Allah, the absolute owner and creator, has even greater claim. Therefore, His absolute command must imply obligation. The text also notes that the term 'obligation' (wujub) here is broader than the distinction between fard and wajib in Hanafi fiqh; punishment attaches to leaving either. Finally, a condition is mentioned: if the commander is superior, compliance is obligatory; if equal, recommended; if inferior, permissible.

Key Terms
الأمر المطلق (al-amr al-mutlaq): صيغة الأمر المجردة عن القرينة الدالة على اللزوم أو عدمه
الوجوب (al-wujub): طلب الفعل على سبيل الإلزام، وهو أعم من الفرض والوجوب باصطلاح الحنفية.
المواظبة (al-muwazaba): الملازمة على فعل الشيء. المواظبة المطلقة تدل على السنة، والمواظبة من غير ترك تدل على الوجوب.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position presented in this passage, what is the default ruling of an absolute command (al-amr al-mutlaq) in the absence of any contextual indicator?
A) It indicates recommendation (nadb).
B) It indicates obligation (wujub).
C) It requires suspension (tawaquf) until clarification.
D) It indicates permissibility (ibaha).

Key Takeaway
The absolute command in Hanafi usul carries a default implication of obligation, supported by rational proof and the concept of authority.

Command and Repetition: The Hanafi Position
Discussing whether a command to perform an act implies repetition or just a single performance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the Hanafi and Shafi'i positions on whether a command implies repetition, and apply the Hanafi rule that an absolute command requires only one performance.

Core Idea
An absolute command demands single compliance; repetition requires explicit condition.

This lesson examines a key Usul al-Fiqh dispute: does a command (amr) to perform an act imply repetition (takrār) or just a single fulfillment? The Hanafi school holds that an absolute command neither necessitates nor admits repetition—it is satisfied by one act. In contrast, some Shafi'i scholars argue that repetition is either required or possible. We will analyze the evidence and the practical implications of this disagreement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core claim
State what a command implies according to Hanafi usul: an absolute command does not necessitate or admit repetition; it is fulfilled once.
2
Examine the proof
Analyze the divorce agent analogy: if an agent divorces once, the command is exhausted and cannot be used again after remarriage.
This shows the command applies to the least of its kind.
3
Contrast opposing views
Distinguish between those who say command necessitates repetition (al-Muzani) and those who say it admits repetition (some Shafi'is). The difference lies in whether a contextual indicator is required.
Hanafis reject both; a single performance suffices.
4
Apply the condition rule
Understand that conditional or descriptive commands (e.g., purification after janābah, flogging for zina) are repeated with the recurrence of the condition or description.
This does not contradict the absoluteness of absolute commands.

الأمر بالفعل لا يقتضي التكرار، ولهذا قلنا: لو قال: "طلق امرأتي" فطلقها الوكيل ثم تزَوَّجَها المؤكلُ ليس للوكيل أن يُطلقها بالأمر الأول ثانياً.

الامرأة المطلقة
حقيقة ولا يحتمله
منه
بأمر فلا يكون لمؤمن ولا مؤمنة الخيرة من أمرهما إن شاؤوا فعلوا وإن لم يشاؤوا لم يفعلوا، بل يجب عليهم الائتمار بأمرهما، ومثل هذا الكلام لا يكون إلا في الأمر الواجب فعله. [الشافي: ص ۱۳۳] فما ظنك إلخ: أي يجب عليك طاعته والاتتمار بأمره سبحانه وتعالى. [الشافي: ص ۱۳۳] أوْجَدك: أنشأك وخلقك وأخرجك من ظلمة العدم. شآبيب النّعَمْ: الشايب جمع شؤبوب وهو أول المطر أي أول النعم وأشرفها كذا في "المعدن". لا يقتضي إلخ أي لا يوجب الاتيان بالمأمور به مرة بعد أخرى، والدوام على الإيمان إلى الموت ليس بتكرار ، بل من قبيل الثبات على الائتمار الأول، فلا يرد شيء. التكرار: اعلم أن القائلين بكون موجب الأمر هو الوجوب اختلفوا في إفادته التكرار، ومعنى التكرار أن يفعل فعلاً ثم بعد فراغه عنه يعود إليه، فقال بعضهم: إنه يوجب التكرار المستوعب لجميع الأمر إلا إذا قام دليل يمنع ويحكى هذا عن المزني وهو اختيار أبي إسحق الاسفرائني الشافعي وعبد القاهر البغدادي من أئمة الحديث وغيرهم، وقال بعض أصحاب الشافعي: إنه لا يوجب التكرار ولكن يحتمله، هذا ویروی عن الشافعي له، والفرق بين الموجب والمحتمل : أن الموجب يثبت من غير قرينة والمحتمل لا يثبت بدونها، وقال بعض مشايخنا: الأمر المطلق لا يوجب التكرار ولا يحتمله لكن المعلق بشرط كقوله تعالى: ﴿وَإِن كُنتُمْ جُنُباً فَاطَّهَّرُون، والمقيد بوصف كقوله تعالى : والزَّانِيَةُ وَالزَّانِي فَاجْلِدُوا يتكرر بتكريره، وهو قول بعض أصحاب الشافعي ممن قال: إنه لا يوجب التكرار ولكن يحتمله، والمذهب الصحيح المختار عندنا أنه لا يوجب التكرار ولا يحتمله سواء كان مطلقاً أو معلقاً بشرط أو مخصوصاً بوصف، إلا أن الأمر بالفعل يقع على أقل جنسه وهو أدنى ما يعد به متمثلاً، ويحتمل كل الجنس بدليله وهو النية، وفي هذا المقام بحث طويل لا يسعه هذا المختصر أوردناها في "التعليم العامي على الحسامي".
Chapter on the implication of the command: The command to perform an act does not imply repetition. For this reason we said: If someone said, 'Divorce my wife,' and the agent divorced her, then the principal remarried her, the agent cannot divorce her again by the first command. ... The woman divorced... in reality and does not admit it... from him... by a command. So it is not for a believing man or woman to have a choice in their affair; they cannot do it if they wish or not do it if they do not wish; rather they must comply with His command. And such speech is only concerning an act that is obligatory to perform. [al-Shafi, p. 133] 'What do you think...': meaning you must obey Him and comply with His command, glorified and exalted. [al-Shafi, p. 133] 'Created you': brought you into existence, created you, and brought you out from the darkness of non-existence. 'Abundant blessings': al-shāʼīb is plural of shuʼbūb, which is the first of the rain, i.e., the first and most honorable of blessings. Thus in 'al-Ma'dan'. 'Does not imply' etc.: meaning it does not necessitate performing the commanded act repeatedly. And persistence in faith until death is not repetition, but rather it is a form of constancy on the first compliance, so no objection arises. 'Repetition': Know that those who hold that the import of a command is obligation differed as to whether it indicates repetition. The meaning of repetition is to perform an act, then after finishing it, to return to it again. Some said it necessitates repetition covering all [times of] the command unless there is a preventing evidence. This is narrated from al-Muzanī and is the choice of Abū Isḥāq al-Isfarā'īnī al-Shāfi'ī and 'Abd al-Qāhir al-Baghdādī from the imams of hadith and others. And some of the followers of al-Shāfi'ī said it does not necessitate repetition but admits it, and this is narrated from al-Shāfi'ī. The difference between 'necessitating' and 'admitting' is that what is entailed is established without a contextual indicator, while what is admitted is not established without one. And some of our [Hanafi] scholars said: The absolute command does not necessitate repetition nor does it admit it. But the command conditional upon a condition, like His saying 'If you are in a state of janābah, then purify yourselves' [Quran 5:6], and that restricted by a description, like His saying 'The fornicator and the fornicatress, flog them' [Quran 24:2], is repeated with its repetition. This is the view of some Shāfi'īs who said it does not necessitate repetition but admits it. And the correct, chosen school according to us is that [the absolute command] does not necessitate repetition nor does it admit it, whether it is absolute, conditional upon a condition, or specific to a description. However, the command to perform an act applies to the least of its kind, which is the minimum by which one is considered to have complied, and it admits the entire kind with its evidence, which is intention. There is a lengthy discussion on this topic that this abridgment does not accommodate; we have mentioned it in 'al-Ta'līm al-'Āmmī 'alā al-Husāmī'.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage is a chapter on what a command implies (muqtada al-amr). The author states the Hanafi position clearly: a command to perform an act does not require repetition (lā yaqtadī al-takrār). He provides an example: if a man tells his agent 'Divorce my wife,' and the agent does so, then the man remarries her, the agent cannot use the original command to divorce her again. This shows the command is fulfilled once. The author then summarizes the views of other schools. Some, like al-Muzanī and certain Shāfi'īs, hold that a command necessitates repetition unless there is evidence to prevent it. Others among the Shāfi'īs hold that it does not necessitate repetition but admits it (i.e., it is possible that repetition is intended). The Hanafi position (from 'some of our scholars') is that an absolute command neither necessitates nor admits repetition; it simply requires one performance. However, commands that are conditional (e.g., 'If you are junub, purify yourselves') or restricted by a description (e.g., 'Flog the fornicator and fornicatress') are repeated with the recurrence of the condition or description. The author then declares the correct Hanafi school (al-madhhab al-ṣaḥīḥ al-mukhtār 'indanā) as the one that holds that the command does not necessitate nor admit repetition, whether absolute, conditional, or restricted. The command applies to the minimal act that counts as compliance, but it can also be applied to more by intention (niyyah). This is a key usul principle affecting many fiqh rulings.

Key Terms
التكرار (al-takrār): Performing a commanded act repeatedly after completion, such that it is done more than once.
الأمر المطلق (al-amr al-muṭlaq): A command that is not qualified by a condition or description indicating recurrence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the correct Hanafi school, what does an absolute command to perform an act imply?
A) It implies repetition unless prevented
B) It implies the possibility of repetition
C) It neither implies nor admits repetition; it is fulfilled by one performance
D) It implies repetition only when conditional

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that an absolute command is fulfilled by a single performance and does not imply repetition, while conditional or descriptive commands are repeated with the recurrence of their cause.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
10 min
Command Does Not Imply Repetition; The Rule of Generic Nouns
Usul discussion on command repetition and generic nouns

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on command repetition and apply the principle of generic nouns to determine the minimal scope of a command.

Core Idea
A command applies to the minimal instance; neither repetition nor the entire genus is implied without intention.

This lesson explores a foundational Hanafi usul rule: a single command does not require repeated performance, nor does it admit repetition. We will also examine how generic nouns (ism al-jins) operate: they default to the smallest individual instance but can be extended by intention. These principles resolve many practical fiqh questions, such as the validity of a second divorce under the same instruction, and the scope of an oath against drinking water.

Guided Path
1
Identify the usul dispute
Recognize that the central question is whether a command (amr) implies repetition (takrar). The Hanafi school holds that it does not, while some Shafi'i scholars disagree.
The dispute is about the inherent implication of the imperative form.
2
State the Hanafi position and its evidence
Memorize the rule: 'Al-amr bi al-fi'l la yaqtadi al-takrar.' The proof is the example of a divorce agent: one command suffices for one divorce; a second divorce requires a new command. Additionally, the imperative is an abbreviation of the full phrase 'do the act,' and abbreviation does not change the meaning.
The divorce example is concrete: if the wife is remarried, the original command no longer applies.
3
Understand the opposing views
Some Shafi'i scholars (al-Muzani, Abu Ishaq, Abd al-Qahir) argue that a command necessitates repetition unless counter-evidence appears, while others say it admits repetition (i.e., can be repeated with a qarina). The Hanafi rebuttal is that abbreviation does not introduce new implications.
The difference between 'necessitates' and 'admits' is crucial: the former requires repetition; the latter only allows it.
4
Analyze the rule of generic nouns
A generic noun (ism jins) like 'water' applies to the minimal individual (a drop) when used absolutely, but can cover the entire genus if so intended. This is illustrated by the oath example: if one swears not to drink water, the oath is broken by the smallest drop; but if he intended all water in the world, that intention is valid.
The generic noun defaults to the minimum; expansion requires explicit intention.
5
Apply to new cases
Practice applying these rules: e.g., a master telling his slave 'tazawwaj' (marry) permits only one marriage; a command to 'give charity' requires only one act unless intention or context indicates otherwise.
The same principle applies to conditional and qualified commands.

The first research on the Book of Allah. Based on this, we know that compliance is obligatory according to the authority of the commander. A section on what the command entails: i.e., from the two examples mentioned, if this is established, we say: indeed Allah is perfect king in every part of the universe, and He has disposal by bringing into existence and preserving as He wills and desires. And if it is established that the one with limited authority over a slave causes abandonment of compliance to be a reason for punishment, then what do you think about abandoning the command of the One who created you from nothing and showered upon you the downpours of blessings. A section on what the command entails: The command for an act does not necessitate repetition; for this reason we said: If he said, 'Divorce my wife,' and the agent divorced her, then the principal married her, the agent does not have the right to divorce her a second time by the first command. (There is a line of commentary about the divorced woman) It is real and does not admit (repetition) from him. A command: so there is no choice for a believer or a believer woman in their command; if they wish they do and if they do not wish they do not, rather they must comply with their command. And such speech only occurs in the obligatory command. [Al-Shafi: p. 133] 'What do you think' etc.: i.e., you must obey Him and comply with His command, Exalted is He. [Al-Shafi: p. 133] 'He created you': He originated and created you and brought you out from the darkness of non-existence. 'Downpours of blessings': al-shayyab is plural of shu'ub, which is the first rain, i.e., the first and most honorable of blessings, as in 'al-Ma'dan'. 'Does not necessitate' etc.: i.e., it does not require performing the commanded act again and again; and persistence in faith until death is not repetition, rather it is of the type of steadfastness on the first compliance, so nothing is problematic. Repetition: Know that those who say that the implication of the command is obligation differed regarding whether it indicates repetition. The meaning of repetition is that one does an act, then after finishing it returns to it. Some said: It necessitates repetition covering all (times) of the command unless evidence prevents, and this is narrated from al-Muzani and is the choice of Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini al-Shafi'i and Abd al-Qahir al-Baghdadi from the hadith scholars and others. Some of al-Shafi'i's companions said: It does not necessitate repetition but admits it, and this is narrated from al-Shafi'i. The difference between necessitating and admitting is that the necessitating is established without a qarina, while the admitting is not established without it. Some of our shuyukh said: The absolute command does not necessitate repetition nor does it admit it, but the command conditional on a condition, like the saying of Allah: 'And if you are junub, then purify yourselves' and the restricted by a description, like His saying: 'The fornicatress and fornicator, flog them', repeats with their repetition. This is the saying of some of al-Shafi'i's companions of those who say it does not necessitate repetition but admits it. The correct chosen madhhab with us is that it does not necessitate repetition nor does it admit it, whether it is absolute, conditional, or specific by description. Except that the command for an act applies to the least of its genus, i.e., the minimum by which one is considered to have performed it, and it admits the entire genus with its evidence, which is intention. In this topic there is a lengthy discussion that this compendium does not cover; we have mentioned them in 'al-Ta'lim al-Ammiy 'ala al-Husami'. [Page 87] The first research on the Book of Allah. Section on what the command entails: If he said: 'Marry me a woman,' this does not cover marriage repeatedly. And if he said to his slave: 'Marry,' this only covers once; because the command for an act is a request to actualize the act by way of abbreviation. For his saying 'Hit' is an abbreviation of 'Do the act of hitting', and the abbreviated and the extended (speech) are equal in ruling. Then the command to hit is a command to a known type of action, because the masdar is a generic noun. And the ruling of a generic noun is that it applies to the minimum when absolute, and admits the entire genus. The one. Based on this we said: If one swears not to drink water, he breaks his oath by drinking the smallest drop of it; and if he intended by it all the waters of the world, his intention is valid. Garment of water. [Commentary: 'Do the act of hitting' as 'Daraba' (hit) is an abbreviation of 'fa'ala fi'l al-darb' (did the act of hitting) in the past, and 'Yadribu' (he hits) is an abbreviation of 'yaf'alu fi'l al-darb' (does the act of hitting) in the future. 'Equal in ruling' i.e., in establishing the ruling or in conveying meaning; because the benefit of abbreviation is reducing wording, not changing the meaning of the extended. 'To a known type of action': the difference between genus and generic noun: applying a generic noun to an individual is in a real way, and it is not applied to many but rather to individual by way of substitution like 'man' and 'horse'; while genus applies to little and much equally like 'water' applies to a drop and the sea. Based on this, every genus is a generic noun without the reverse, so between them is general and specific absolute. 'And it admits the entire genus': i.e., when intending it; because the generic noun is a noun of an individual, not a plural or number form, and the noun of an individual does not admit number and repetition because there is opposition between the individual and number; for the individual is what has no composition, and number is the opposite, and between them is opposition. So the essence within the individual is intended, but the individual may be real like the single from the genus, for it is an individual really, and may be judgmental like the entire genus, for it is an individual judgmentally though really a number. So the genus is made like one thing. So we said: If he intended nothing, it defaults to the one; because it is individual really and judgmentally. If he intended the whole, because he intended the judgmental individual. If he intended more than the real individual and less than the judgmental individual—the capacity between the minimum and the whole—his intention is not valid; because it is pure number without any aspect of individuality, so the individual form does not apply to it, as in 'al-Ma'dan'. And based on this: i.e., that a generic noun applies to the minimum when absolute and admits the entire genus.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage is a classic Hanafi usul al-fiqh discussion on the nature of commands (amr) and generic nouns (ism jins). The author first establishes the groundwork: just as compliance with a limited authority is obligatory, compliance with Allah's commands is even more obligatory. Then the main fiqh rule is stated: a command does not imply repetition (takrar). This is supported by the example of a husband telling an agent to divorce his wife; if the wife is remarried to the husband, the agent cannot divorce her again based on the original command. The passage then delves into the scholarly dispute about whether a command necessitates repetition. Some Shafi'i scholars like al-Muzani and Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini held that it does unless there is counter-evidence. Others from the Shafi'i school say it does not necessitate but admits repetition, meaning it can be repeated if there is a qarina. The Hanafi position (our mashayikh) is that the absolute command neither necessitates nor admits repetition; the same applies to conditional or qualified commands. However, the command applies to the minimal instance of the act (adna ma yutba bihi mutamathilan), and can extend to the entire genus through intention (niyya). The discussion then shifts to generic nouns (ism jins). The author explains that a generic noun like 'water' applies to the smallest amount (e.g., a drop) when used absolutely, and can cover the whole genus if so intended. This is illustrated with the oath example: one who swears not to drink water breaks the oath with the smallest drop, but if he intended all water, his intention is valid. The commentary differentiates between genus (jins) and generic noun (ism jins): the latter applies to individuals individually, while the former applies to any amount. The Hanafi position is that the command does not imply repetition, and the generic noun defaults to the minimum but can be extended by intention.

Key Terms
الأمر (al-amr): A request to perform an act, understood as a command requiring compliance.
التكرار (al-takrar): Doing an act again after completing it; repetition.
اسم الجنس (ism al-jins): A noun that applies to a single individual of a kind in reality, not to multiple at once without substitution.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi madhhab, if a master tells his slave 'Tazawwaj' (marry), how many times can the slave marry based on that command?
A) Once
B) Twice
C) As many times as he wishes
D) Only if the master repeats the command

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi usul principle that a command does not imply repetition is essential for correctly understanding the scope of obligations and avoiding invalid multiple actions under a single instruction.

Commands and the Scope of Generic Nouns in Hanafi Usul
Analyzing commands' scope using the nature of اسم الجنس

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that a command defaults to a single instance of the genus and identify when intention can extend it to the entire genus.

Core Idea
A generic noun refers to the minimum unless intention elevates it to the legal individual.

This passage from Usul al-Shashi presents a foundational Hanafi usul principle: commands and generic nouns do not imply repetition. The key is the distinction between a real individual (one unit) and a legal individual (the whole genus). Through examples of oaths, divorce, and marriage, the author demonstrates how intention determines scope.

Guided Path
1
Understand the command as abbreviation
Recognize that 'strike' (اضرب) is short for 'do the act of striking'. This establishes that a command targets the act itself, not multiple acts.
The abbreviation does not change the meaning; it only shortens the wording.
2
Identify the generic noun
The verbal noun (e.g., ضرب) is a generic noun (اسم الجنس). It denotes the genus and applies to the minimum at absolute release. Example: oath not to drink water – smallest drop suffices.
Generic nouns differ from pure genus nouns; they apply to individuals alternately.
3
Distinguish real vs legal individual
The real individual (الفرد الحقيقي) is one unit; the legal individual (الفرد الحكمي) is the entire genus treated as one. Intention can raise the default to the legal individual but not to intermediate numbers.
Pure number (e.g., two) without individuality cannot be intended.
4
Apply to divorce and marriage
Examine the examples: 'Divorce yourself' yields one divorce by default; intention for three is valid (legal individual for free woman). Intention for two is invalid unless the wife is a slave-girl, where two is the entire genus. Similarly, 'Marry' to a slave defaults to one, but intention for two is valid.
The rule consistently applies: the singular form only carries the minimum unless intention extends to the legal individual.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في مقتضى الأمر
ولو قال: "زوجني امرأةً" لا يتناول هذا تزويجاً مرّةً بعد أخرى. ولو قال لعبده: "تزوج" لا يتناولُ ذلك إلا مرَّةً واحدةً؛ لأن الأمر بالفعل طلب تحقيق الفعل على سبيل الاختصار، فإن قوله: "اضرب" مختصر من قوله: "افعل فعل الضرب"، وللمختصر من الكلام والمطولُ سواء في الحكم، ثم الأمرُ بالضرب أمر بجنس تصرف معلوم، لأن المصدر اسم جنس وحكم اسم الجنس: أن يتناول الأدني عند الإطلاق، ويحتمل كل الجنس.
وعلى هذا قلنا: إذا حلف لا يشرب الماء يحنث بشرب أدنى قطرة منه ولو نوى به جميع مياه العالم صحت نيته.
فعلَ الضرب كما أن "ضرب" مختصر من فعل" الضرب" في الماضي، ويضرب" مختصر من "يفعل فعل الضرب" في الزمان الآتي. سواء في الحكم أي في إثبات الحكم أو في إفادة المعنى؛ لأن فائدة الاختصار تقليل اللفظ لا تغير المعنى المطول. بجنس تصرُّف إلى: الفرق بين الجنس واسم الجنس: أن إطلاق اسم الجنس على الفرد بطريق الحقيقة ولا يطلق على الكثير بل على فرد فرد بطريق البدلية كالرجل والفرس، والجنس على القليل والكثير على السواء كالماء يطلق على القطرة والبحر، وعلى هذا يكون كل جنس اسم جنس بدون العكس، فكان بينهما عموماً وخصوصاً مطلقاً.
ويحتمل كلَّ الجنس: أي عند النية؛ وذلك لأن اسم الجنس اسم فرد ليس بصيغة جمع ولا عدد، واسم الفرد لا يحتمل العدد والتكرار ما بين الفرد والعدد منافاة؛ إذ الفرد ما ليس فيه تركيب والعدد بالعكس، وبينهما تناف، فيراد به الماهية في ضمن الفرد لكن الفرد قد يكون حقيقياً كالواحد من الجنس، فإنه فرد حقيقة، وقد يكون حكمياً كالجنس بتمامه، فإنه فرد حكماً وإن كان عدداً حقيقةً، فيجعل الجنس بمنزلة شيء واحد، فقلنا: إذا لم ينو شيئاً ينصرف إلى الواحد؛ لأنه فرد حقيقة وحكماً إذا نوى الكل؛ لأنه نوى الفرد الحكمي، فإذا نوى الأكثر من الفرد الحقيقي، وأقل من الفرد الحكمي وهو القدر المتخلل بين الأدنى والكل لا نيته؛ لأنه عدد يصح محض ليس فيه معنى الفردية بوجه فلا تقع عليه صيغة الفرد.
ولهذا قلنا: إذا قال لها: "طلقي نفسك" فقالت: "طلقت" تقعُ الواحدة، ولو نوى الزوج الثلاث صحت نيته.
وكذلك لو قال لآخر: "طلقها" يتناول الواحدة عند الإطلاق، ولو نوى الثلاث صحت نيته، ولو نوى الثنتين لا يصح، إلا إذا كانت المنكوحة أمةً، فإن نية الثنتين في حقها نيَّةٌ لأنه عدد محض.
ولو قال لعبده: "تزَوَّج" يَقَعُ على تزوج امرأة واحدة ولو نوى الثنتينِ صحت نيته؛ لأن ذلك كل الجنس في حق العبد.
ولهذا إلخ: أي ولأن الأمر لا يقتضي التكرار ولا يحتمله، قلنا في قول الرجل: "طلقي نفسك" أنه يقع على الواحدة إن لم ينو شيئاً أو نوى واحدة أو ثنتين، وإن نوى ثلاثاً فعلى ما نوى؛ لأن الواحد فرد حقيقي متيقن، والثلاث فرد حكمي محتمل، ولا تصح نية الثنتين؛ لأنه عدد محض ليس بفرد حقيقي ولا حكمي، وليس مدلول اللفظ ولا محتملاً له، إلا إذا كانت تلك المرأة أمة؛ لأن الثنتين في حقها كالثلاثة في حق الحرة فهو واحد حكمي كالثلاث في حقها.
حاصل الفرق: أن المصدر الذي دل عليه الفعل فرد مع كونه جنساً، والفرد يصلح كل أفراد الجنس؛ لأن الضرب الذي دل عليه قوله: "اضرب" مثلاً الجمع أفراد جنس واحد من الضربات، وكذا الطلاق في قوله: "طلق"، وأما المثنى فعدد محض وليس بفرد لا حقيقةً ولا حكماً، فلا يحتمله اللفظ والنية إنما تحتمل فيما احتمله اللفظ، إلا إذا كانت المنكوحة أمة الغير تزوجها ليست تحته حرة فحينئذ تصح نية الثنتين؛ لأن كل أفراد الجنس طلاقها ثنتان.
Chapter on the Implication of the Command. And if one says: 'Marry me a woman,' this does not encompass marriage more than once. And if one says to his slave: 'Marry,' that only encompasses one time; because the command to an act is a request to realize the act in an abbreviated manner. For his saying: 'Strike' is an abbreviation of his saying: 'Do the act of striking,' and the abbreviated form of speech and the lengthy form are equal in ruling. Then the command to strike is a command to a genus of known action, because the verbal noun is a generic noun, and the ruling of a generic noun is that it applies to the lowest at absolute release, and it can encompass the entire genus. And upon this we said: If one swears not to drink water, he breaks the oath by drinking the smallest drop of it, and if he intended all the waters of the world, his intention is valid. The act of striking just as 'daraba' is an abbreviation of 'fa'ala fa'la al-darbi' in the past, and 'yadribu' is an abbreviation of 'yaf'alu fa'la al-darbi' in the future. Equal in ruling meaning in establishing the ruling or in conveying the meaning; because the benefit of abbreviation is reducing the wording, not changing the meaning of the lengthy form. By a genus of action to: the difference between genus and generic noun: that applying the generic noun to the individual is in the literal sense, and it is not applied to many but to each individual in an alternative way like 'man' and 'horse', and genus applies to few and many equally like 'water' applies to a drop and the sea, and upon this every genus is a generic noun without the reverse, so between them there is absolute generality and specificity. And it can encompass all the genus: meaning by intention; and that is because the generic noun is the name of an individual, not a plural form nor a number, and the name of an individual does not carry number and repetition; there is incompatibility between the individual and number; for the individual is that which has no composition, and number is the opposite, and between them is opposition. So what is meant is the essence within the individual, but the individual may be real like the one from the genus, for it is an actual individual, and may be legal like the whole genus, for it is a legal individual even if it is actually number. So we said: if he does not intend anything, it refers to the one; because it is an actual and legal individual; if he intends the whole, because he intended the legal individual; if he intends more than the actual individual and less than the legal individual, which is the amount between the lowest and the whole, his intention does not count; because it is pure number with no aspect of individuality at all, so the singular form does not apply to it. And for this we said: If he says to her: 'Divorce yourself,' and she said: 'I am divorced,' one divorce occurs, and if the husband intended three, his intention is valid. Similarly, if he said to another: 'Divorce her,' it encompasses one at absolute release, and if he intended three, his intention is valid, but if he intended two, it is not valid, unless the married woman is a slave-girl, for the intention of two in her case is an intention because it is pure number. And if the master said to his slave: 'Marry,' it applies to marrying one woman, and if he intended two, his intention is valid; because that is the entire genus for the slave. And for this etc.: i.e., because the command does not require repetition nor carry it, we said in the statement of a man: 'Divorce yourself' that it applies to one if he did not intend anything or intended one or two, and if he intended three, then it is as he intended; because the one is a certain real individual, and three is a probable legal individual, and the intention of two is not valid; because it is pure number, neither real nor legal individual, and it is neither the denotation of the word nor possible for it, unless that woman is a slave-girl; because two in her case is like three in the case of a free woman, so it is a legal individual like three in her case. The essence of the difference: that the verbal noun indicated by the verb is an individual while being a genus, and the individual can apply to all individuals of the genus; because the striking indicated by his saying 'Strike' for example includes individuals of one genus of strikes, and similarly divorce in his saying 'Divorce'; as for the dual, it is pure number and not an individual, neither actually nor legally, so the wording does not carry it, and intention only carries what the wording carries, unless the married woman is a slave-girl (whose marriage is not under a free woman), then the intention of two is valid; because all individuals of the genus of her divorce are two.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage from Usul al-Shashi discusses the legal implications of commands and the scope of generic nouns in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. The author begins by stating that a command (like 'marry' or 'strike') is an abbreviated form of 'do the act of X'. This abbreviation does not change the ruling; the command applies to the genus of the action. The key concept is اسم الجنس (generic noun), which at absolute release applies to the minimum instance of the genus but can potentially encompass the entire genus through intention. The author provides the example of an oath not to drink water: the swearer breaks the oath with the smallest drop, but if he intended all the waters of the world, that intention is valid. This shows that the generic noun 'water' can refer to a drop or the entire genus depending on intent. The passage then distinguishes between an actual individual (like one unit of the genus) and a legal individual (the entire genus treated as one). When no intention is specified, the command defaults to one actual individual. Intention can raise it to the legal individual (the whole genus) but not to an intermediate number (like two) because that is pure number with no individuality. This principle is applied to divorce examples: if a husband says 'divorce yourself' and the wife divorces, one divorce occurs. If he intended three, it counts as three. If he intended two, it does not count unless the wife is a slave-girl, because for a slave-girl, two divorces are like three for a free woman (i.e., the entire genus of divorce for her is two). Similarly, if a master tells his slave to 'marry', it applies to one marriage, but if he intended two, it is valid because for a slave, the entire genus of marriage is two (since a slave can only marry two women). The passage concludes by clarifying that the verbal noun from a verb is an individual that can represent all individuals of the genus, but the dual (two) is not an individual and thus cannot be intended unless the context makes it the entire genus.

Key Terms
اسم الجنس (ism al-jins): A noun that denotes a genus and applies to both few and many; it designates the essence within an individual.
الفرد الحقيقي (al-fard al-haqiqi): An actual single unit of a genus (e.g., one strike, one divorce).
الفرد الحكمي (al-fard al-hukmi): The entire genus treated as a singular entity for legal purposes (e.g., all divorces for a free woman = three).
الأمر (al-amr): A request to perform an action, abbreviated from 'do the act of X'; it does not imply repetition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a husband says to his wife 'Talaqi nafsaki' without specifying number, and she says 'Tallaqtu', how many divorces occur according to the Hanafi school?
A) One divorce
B) Two divorces
C) Three divorces
D) None

Key Takeaway
A command defaults to a single instance of the genus; intention can extend it to the entire genus but not to intermediate numbers.

The Command Implies a Single Occurrence
Explaining that the command does not imply repetition and that the verbal noun denotes a single genus-instance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that al-amr yufidu al-wahda (the command indicates one) to derive that a bare command yields one action, and distinguish between real and constructive individuals in interpreting intention.

Core Idea
The command yields one, not a specific number — one is a real individual, three can be constructive, but two is pure number.

This passage from Usul al-Shashi establishes the foundational Hanafi principle that a command (amr) does not imply repetition or any number beyond a single occurrence. Through examples from divorce and marriage, the author demonstrates how intention (niyyah) interacts with the command's scope, but only within what the word can possibly denote. Understanding the distinction between real individual (fard haqiqi), constructive individual (fard hukmi), and pure number (adad mahd) is crucial for interpreting texts and legal speech.

Guided Path
1
Identify the default implication of a command
The passage states that 'the command does not require repetition nor tolerate it.' This means the default when a command is uttered is a single occurrence of the act. Find the explicit statement in the text.
Look for the Arabic phrase: 'الأمر لا يقتضي التكرار ولا يحتمله'.
2
Understand the three types of individuals
The passage distinguishes between a real individual (fard haqiqi – e.g., one), a constructive individual (fard hukmi – e.g., three for a free woman), and pure number (adad mahd – e.g., two for a free woman). Identify what makes each distinct and why only two of them can be intended.
One is certain (متيقن), three is possible (محتمل), two is not a valid individual.
3
Apply the rule to the divorce examples
For a free woman: 'طلقي نفسك' yields one divorce if no intention, one intention of one, or intention of two – but intention of three makes it three. For a slave woman, intention of two is valid because two is the entire genus. Trace the reasoning in the text.
The key is whether the intended number is a valid individual of the genus of divorce.
4
Apply the rule to the marriage example
For a slave: 'تزوج' yields one marriage by default, but intention of two is valid because two is all individuals of the genus for the slave. See how the same principle applies.
The passage says: 'لأن ذلك كل الجنس في حق العبد'.
5
Connect the verbal noun as individual and genus
The masdar (e.g., 'ضرب' or 'طلاق') denotes a single instance while also representing the genus. This is why one suffices for all, but a number beyond the genus (like two for free woman) cannot be intended.
The passage explains: 'المصدر الذي دل عليه الفعل فرد مع كونه جنساً'.

Source Text
