فصل في مقتضى الأمر
ولهذا قلنا: إذا قال لها: "طلقي نفسك" فقالت: "طلقت" تقعُ الواحدة، ولو نوى الزوج امرأته الثلاث صحت نيته. وكذلك لو قال لآخر: "طلقها" يتناول الواحدة عند الإطلاق، ولو نوى الثلاث صحت بقوله: طلقها نيته، ولو نوى التنين لا يصح، إلا إذا كانت المنكوحة أمةً، فإن نية الثنتين في حقها نيَّةٌ 110 لأنه عدد محض بكُلِّ الجنس. ولو قال لعبده: "تزَوَّج" يَقَعُ على تزوج امرأة واحدة ولو نوى الثنتينِ صحت نيته؛ لأن ذلك كل الجنس في حق العبد. المولى ولهذا إلخ: أي ولأن الأمر لا يقتضي التكرار ولا يحتمله، قلنا في قول الرجل: "طلقي نفسك" أنه يقع على الواحدة إن لم ينو شيئاً أو نوى واحدة أو ثنتين، وإن نوى ثلاثاً فعلى ما نوى؛ لأن الواحد فرد حقيقي متيقن، والثلاث فرد حكمي محتمل، ولا تصح نية الثنتين؛ لأنه عدد محض ليس بفرد حقيقي ولا حكمي، وليس مدلول اللفظ ولا محتملاً له، إلا إذا كانت تلك المرأة أمة؛ لأن الثنتين في حقها كالثلاثة في حق الحرة فهو واحد حكمي كالثلاث في حقها. لا يصح حاصل الفرق: أن المصدر الذي دل عليه الفعل فرد مع كونه جنساً، والفرد يصلح كل أفراد الجنس؛ لأن الضرب الذي دل عليه قوله: "اضرب" مثلاً الجمع أفراد جنس واحد من الضربات، وكذا الطلاق في قوله: "طلق"، وأما المثنى فعدد محض وليس بفرد لا حقيقةً ولا حكماً، فلا يحتمله اللفظ والنية إنما يحتمل فيما احتمله اللفظ، إلا إذا كانت المنكوحة أمة الغير تزوجها ليست تحته حرة فحينئذ تصح نية الثنتين؛ لأن كل أفراد الجنس طلاقها ثنتان كذا في "الفصول". نيَّة بكُلِّ الجنس: أي فصار الطلاق من طريق الجنس واحداً كالثلاث في حق الحرة، فيقع. [الشافي: ص ١٣٦] كل الجنس إلخ ثم الكل على ثلاثة معان كل كلي وكل مجموعي وكل أفرادي، فالأول بمعنى الماهية نحو: كل إنسان نوع، والثاني بمعنى المجموع نحو كل إنسان لا يسعه الدار والثالث بمعنى الفرد نحو: كل إنسان يشبعه هذا الرغيف.
Section on the import of the command. Hence we said: If a man says to his wife, 'Divorce yourself' and she says 'I divorced,' one divorce occurs. If he intended his wife three (divorces), his intention is valid. Similarly, if he says to another, 'Divorce her,' it includes one divorce at the absolute (usage), and if he intended three, his intention is valid. If he intended two, it is not valid, unless the married woman is a slave-girl, for the intention of two in her case is an intention with the entire genus, because it is pure number. And if he says to his slave, 'Marry,' it applies to marrying one woman, and if he intends two, his intention is valid; because that is the entire genus for the slave. The commentary: 'Thus etc.' means because the command does not require repetition nor tolerate it. We said regarding the statement of a man, 'Divorce yourself,' that one divorce occurs if he did not intend anything or intended one or two. If he intended three, then it is as he intended; because one is a real individual certain, and three is a constructive individual possible. The intention of two is not valid because it is pure number, not a real nor constructive individual, and it is not a denotation of the word nor possible for it, unless that woman is a slave-girl; because two in her case is like three in the case of a free woman, so it is one constructive individual like three in her case. The summary of the difference: The verbal noun indicated by the verb is an individual while being a genus, and the individual suffices for all individuals of the genus; because the striking indicated by his statement 'Strike' for example gathers individuals of one genus of striking, and similarly divorce in his statement 'Divorce.' As for the dual, it is pure number and not an individual, neither reality nor constructively, so the word does not contain it and intention is only valid in what the word contains, unless the married woman is a slave-girl not having a free woman under him, then the intention of two is valid because all individuals of the genus for her divorce are two. As in 'al-Fusul.' The note: 'Intention with the entire genus' means that the divorce from the genus becomes one like three in the case of a free woman, so it occurs. [al-Shafi: p.136] 'All the genus etc.' Then 'all' has three meanings: all as a universal, all as a collective, all as distributive. The first is for the essence like 'Every human is a species', the second for the totality like 'Every human cannot fit in the house', the third for the individual like 'Every human is satiated by this loaf.'
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage discusses the import of the command (amr) in Hanafi usul. The author explains that the command does not imply repetition or a specific number; it indicates a single occurrence of the act (the verbal noun). Examples from talaq and marriage illustrate that the command yields one divorce or one marriage unless the speaker intends a number that can be considered a valid individual (fard) of the genus. One is a real individual (fard haqiqi), three is a constructive individual (fard hukmi) possible for the word to bear, but two is pure number (adad mahd) and not a valid individual. Exception is made for a slave-girl or slave, where two represents the entirety of the genus. The commentary clarifies the distinction between the verbal noun as an individual and as a genus, and the three meanings of 'kul' (every/all). The teaching is that intention cannot expand the command's scope beyond what the word can possibly mean.

Key Terms
الأمر (al-amr): The command; a request for an action from a superior to an inferior.
الفرد الحقيقي (al-fard al-haqiqi): A real individual; a single, certain instance of an action.
الفرد الحكمي (al-fard al-hukmi): A constructive individual; a number that is treated as a single unit in legal effect.
العدد المحض (al-adad al-mahd): Pure number; a numerical quantity that is not considered an individual of the genus.
الجنس (al-jins): Genus; the category of actions that includes all its individuals.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) None, the intention is invalid

Key Takeaway
The command indicates one occurrence by default; intention can expand to a constructive number but not to pure number.

The Servant Cannot Fix Time for an Absolute Command
Usul rule on acting without specified time

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul principle that a commanded act with no specified time cannot be restricted by the servant's assignment, and identify when specifying intention (ta'yin al-niyya) is required due to overlapping obligations.

Core Idea
The servant's designation does not override the Lawgiver's absolute command.

This lesson unpacks a key principle from Usul al-Shashi: when the Lawgiver leaves an act's time unspecified, the servant's own assignment does not fix it. This rule governs overlapping obligations like qada, kaffarah, and nadhr, and introduces the crucial requirement of specifying intention (ta'yin al-niyya) when multiple valid fasts compete on the same day. Understanding this sharpens your ability to derive rulings from absolute versus restricted textual language.

Guided Path
1
Identify the commanded act and its temporal status
Locate the act in question—here, qada of Ramadan. Determine whether the Lawgiver specified a time. The verse 'فعدة من أيام أخر' gives an absolute command without fixing a particular day.
The openness is the key: the command is mutlaq regarding time.
2
Apply the ruling: servant's assignment does not fix time
Even if the servant picks specific days for qada, those days are not exclusively for qada. Other valid fasts (kaffarah, nafl) are permitted on them, and qada can be done on other days as well.
This is because the servant cannot change a hukm shari'i (a rule of the Lawgiver).
3
Understand the necessity of ta'yin al-niyya
Because multiple valid fasts can coincide on the same day, you must specify your intention (e.g., 'this is for qada, not kaffarah') to ensure the fast counts for the intended obligation. The 'muzahim' (competing act) triggers this requirement.
Ta'yin al-niyya distinguishes between overlapping rights of the servant and Lawgiver.
4
Differentiate rights: servant's right vs. Lawgiver's right
Nafl is the servant's right—he can change or drop it. Qada and kaffarah are the Lawgiver's right—the servant cannot modify or drop them. This explains why a vowed fast (nadhr) is not fulfilled if one fasts that day for qada: the Lawgiver's absolute command takes priority.
The servant can obligate something new (vow) but cannot alter an existing divine obligation.
5
Apply the principle to the khul' example
The same logic: if spouses stipulate dropping maintenance and housing in khul', maintenance (servant's right) can be dropped, but housing during iddah (Lawgiver's right) cannot.
This shows the principle's reach beyond fasting.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في المأمور به
وإن لم يعين الشرع له وقتا، فإنَّه لا يتعين الوقت له بتعيين العبد، حتى لو عين العبد أياماً لقضاء رَمَضَان لا تتعيَّن هي للقضاء، ويجوز فيها صومُ الكفارة والنفل، ويجوز قضاء تلك الأيام فيها وغيرها.
ومن حكم هذا النوع: أنه يشترط تعيين النية لوجود المزاحم، ثُمَّ للعبد أن يوجبَ شيئًا على نفسه مؤقتًا أو غير موقت وليس له تغيير حكم الشرع. مثاله: إذا نذر أن يصوم يوماً بعينه لزمه ذلك، ولو صامه عن قضاء رمضان أو عن كفارة يمينه جاز؛ لأن الشرعَ جَعَل القضاء مطلقاً، مع أن الناذر عيَّن ذلك اليوم للصوم المنذور. ولو صامه عن نفل لا يقع عن المنذور بل يقع عما نوى؛ لأن النفل حق العبد، والقضاء والكفارة حق الشرع.
النفل: وهو حق العبد؛ إذ هو يستبد بنفسه من العبد، فجاز أن يؤثر فعله فيما هو حقه لا فيما هو حق الشرع.
وعلى اعتبار هذا المعنى قال مشايخنا: إذا شرطا الزوجان في الخلع أن لا نفقة لها ولا سكنى، سَقَطَت النفقة دون السكنى، حتى لا يتمكن الزوج من إخراجها عن بيت العدة؛ لأن السكنى في بيت العدة حق الشرع، فلا يتمكن العبد من إسقاطه بخلاف النفقة.
Chapter on the commanded act. And if the Lawgiver did not specify a time for it, then the time is not fixed by the servant's assignment, such that even if the servant designates days for making up Ramadan, they are not fixed for the makeup; it is permissible to fast the expiation and supererogatory fast on them, and it is permissible to make up those days on those days and others. And from the ruling of this type: it is necessary to specify the intention due to the presence of competition. Then the servant may obligate something upon himself with a time or without a time, but he does not have the right to change the ruling of the Lawgiver. Example: if he vows to fast a specific day, that is binding upon him; but if he fasts it for making up Ramadan or for expiation of an oath, it is valid, because the Lawgiver made the makeup absolute, even though the vower designated that day for the vowed fast. And if he fasts it as supererogatory, it does not count for the vowed fast but counts for what he intended; because supererogatory is the right of the servant, while makeup and expiation are the right of the Lawgiver. Supererogatory: it is the right of the servant; since it originates from the servant himself, so it is allowed that his action affects what is his right, not what is the right of the Lawgiver. And based on this meaning, our scholars said: if the spouses stipulate in khul' that she has neither maintenance nor housing, maintenance falls but housing does not, such that the husband cannot evict her from the waiting period home; because residence in the waiting period home is the right of the Lawgiver, so the servant cannot drop it, unlike maintenance.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a section of Usul al-Shashi dealing with the category of commanded acts for which the Lawgiver did not specify a time. The author first states the core ruling: such acts do not become fixed in time by the servant's own assignment. The example given is fasting to make up missed days of Ramadan. If the servant designates certain days for qada, those days are not exclusively for qada; other fasts (kaffarah, nafl) are valid on them, and qada can be done on other days as well. This is because the Lawgiver left the time of qada open-ended. The second ruling: when a competing act exists (i.e., any day can host multiple valid fasts), one must specify the intention (ta'yin al-niyya) to distinguish which obligation is being fulfilled. The author then addresses an objection: if the servant can obligate something upon himself (like a vow), why can't he fix a time for an existing obligation? The answer: obligating a new act is within the servant's capacity, but changing a ruling of the Lawgiver (e.g., restricting an absolute permission) is not. The example of a vow to fast a specific day illustrates this: if one fasts that day for qada or kaffarah, it counts for that purpose, not for the vow, because the Lawgiver made qada and kaffarah absolute. But if one fasts nafl, it counts as nafl, not for the vow. This highlights the distinction between rights of the servant (nafl) and rights of the Lawgiver (qada, kaffarah). The commentary then analogizes to khul': if spouses stipulate no maintenance and no housing, maintenance can be dropped by mutual agreement (right of the servant), but housing during the waiting period cannot be dropped because it is a right of the Lawgiver (established by Quran). Thus, the servant's action (contract) can affect what is his own right but cannot override divine commands.

Key Terms
تعيين النية (ta'yīn al-niyya): تخصيص النية بنوع معين من الصوم (قضاء، كفارة، نذر) عند وجود مزاحم.
حق العبد (ḥaqq al-'abd): ما جعله الشرع للعبد من التصرفات التي له إسقاطها أو تغييرها، كالنفل والنفقة في الخلع.
حق الشرع (ḥaqq al-shar'): ما أوجبه الشرع على العبد ولا يسقط بتصرفه كالسكنى في العدة والواجبات المطلقة.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Supererogatory fasts are a right of the Lawgiver and cannot be changed.
B) Supererogatory fasts are a right of the servant; he can affect what is his right.

Key Takeaway
The servant cannot restrict an absolute divine command by fixing a time; this principle separates the servant's rights from the Lawgiver's rights.

Hasan li 'Aynihi and Hasan li Ghayrihi in Hanafi Usul
Defining and distinguishing two types of goodness in commanded acts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define hasan li 'aynihi and hasan li ghayrihi, apply the ruling of the intermediary act (wasita), and distinguish between the two subtypes of hasan li ghayrihi as presented in Usul al-Shashi.

Core Idea
An act's goodness may be intrinsic or derived; the obligation of a means depends on the obligation of the end.

The passage introduces two categories of commanded acts: those good in themselves (hasan li 'aynihi) and those good due to another (hasan li ghayrihi). It focuses on the second, explaining when such acts become obligatory and when they do not. It also compares two subtypes: acts like walking to Jumu'ah, where the end is not achieved by the act itself, and acts like hudud, where the end is achieved by the act itself. Understanding this classification is key to determining obligations in fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of goodness
Read the first sentence: 'The second type: what is good by means of another.' Distinguish between hasan li 'aynihi (good in itself) and hasan li ghayrihi (good by means of another). Note the examples given.
The text assumes the first type (hasan li 'aynihi) was previously discussed.
2
Understand the ruling of the intermediary
The passage states: 'The ruling of this type is that that intermediary falls away.' This means that if the ultimate commanded act is not obligatory on a person, the intermediary (wasita) is also not obligatory. Study the exceptions: sick, traveler, menstruating woman, etc.
This is the core principle: obligation of the means depends on obligation of the end.
3
Apply the conditions: who is exempt
Identify which individuals are excused from the intermediary act. For walking to Jumu'ah: the sick and traveler. For wudu: the menstruating and postpartum woman. Also note scenarios where the intermediary must be repeated if broken.
The condition is the non-obligation of the ultimate act, not just any impediment.
4
Distinguish between the two subtypes of hasan li ghayrihi
The text compares 'complete' hasan li ghayrihi (walking, wudu) with the 'close' type (hudud, qisas, jihad). In the former, the end is not attained by the act of the commanded; in the latter, the end is attained by the act itself. This affects whether repetition is required.
This distinction prevents confusion: even though hudud are obligatory bare, they are still hasan li ghayrihi.

Source Text

فصل في حسن المأمور به
النوع الثاني: ما يكون حسناً بواسطة الغير، وذلك مثل السعى إلى الجمعة، والوضوء للصلاة، فإن السعي حسن بواسطة كونه مفضياً إلى أداء الجمعة، والوضوء حسن بواسطة كونه مفتاحاً للصلاة.
وحكم هذا النوع أنه يسقط تلك الواسطة، حتى أن السعي لا يجب على من لا جمعة عليه كالمريض والمسافر، ولا يجب الوضوء على من لا صلاة عليه كالحائض والنفساء، ولو سعى إلى الجمعة ثم حمل إلى موضع آخر قبل إقامة الجمعة يجب عليه السعي ثانياً، ولو كان معتكفاً في الجامع يكون السعي ساقطاً عنه. وكذلك لو توضأ فأحدث قبل أداء الصلاة يجب عليه الوضوء ثانياً، ولو كان متوضياً عند وجوب الصلاة لا يجب عليه تجديد الوضوء.
والقريب من هذا النوع الحدود والقصاص والجهاد، تجب عرياناً، ولا تسقط في حين من الأحيان، فعلم أن مطلق العوارض غير مسقط للوجوب بل بعضها الذي اعتبره الشارع في باب الحرج والضرورة البالغة إلى حد معتبر عند الشرع مسقط للوجوب، فافهم كذا في "المعدن".
واللباس: حتى خير بين أن يصلي قائماً ويتم الركوع والسجود، وبين أن يصلي قاعداً ويؤديهما بالإيماء.
والوضوء إلخ: فإن الوضوء تبريد وتنظيف الأعضاء وإضاعة الماء، وذلك ليس بحسن في ذاته أو ليس فيه معنى العبادة إنما حسن لأجل أداء الصلاة.
والقريب وفيه كلام وهو: أن قريب الشيء يلزم أن يكون غيره، فيلزم أن يكون الحدود ونحوها حسناً لعينه؛ إذ لا واسطة بين الحسن لغيره وبين الحسن لعينه، فإذا كان قريباً لما هو حسن لغيره يلزم أن يكون غيره، وغيره ليس إلا الحسن لعينه، بناء على انتفاء الواسطة وكون الحدود وأمثالها من الحسن لعينه بديهي البطلان كذا في بعض الحواشي. من هذا النوع: لأن القسم الأول أي السعي والوضوء كامل في كونه حسناً لغيره؛ لأن الغير لا يحصل بفعل المأمور، بخلاف هذا القسم أي الحدود والقصاص والجهاد؛ فإن الغير يحصل بفعل المأمور به.
فإن الحد حسن بواسطة الزجر عن الجناية، والجهاد حسن بواسطة دفع شر الكفرة وإعلاء كلمة الحق، ولو فرضنا عدم الواسطة لا يبقى ذلك مأموراً به، فإنه لولا الجناية لا يجب الحد، ولولا الكفر المفضي إلى الحراب لا يجب عليه الجهاد.
Chapter on the goodness of the commanded thing. The second type: what is good by means of another, such as walking to Friday prayer and wudu for prayer. For walking is good by means of its leading to performing Friday prayer, and wudu is good by means of its being a key to prayer. The ruling of this type is that that intermediary falls away, such that walking is not obligatory upon one for whom there is no Friday prayer, like the sick and the traveler, and wudu is not obligatory upon one for whom there is no prayer, like the menstruating and postpartum woman. If one walked to Friday then was carried to another place before the Friday prayer is established, walking becomes obligatory upon him a second time; and if one was in i'tikaf in the mosque, walking is dropped from him. Similarly, if one performed wudu then nullified it before performing prayer, wudu upon him a second time; and if one was in wudu at the time of obligation of prayer, renewal of wudu is not obligatory upon him. Close to this type are hudud, qisas, and jihad: they are obligatory bare and do not fall away at any time. So one knows that absolute impediments are not causes for falling of obligation, but only some of them, which the Lawgiver considered in the chapter of hardship and dire necessity to a degree considered by the Law, cause falling of obligation. Understand this, as in 'al-Ma'din'. And clothing: so that he is given a choice between praying standing and completing bowing and prostration, and between praying sitting and performing them by gesture. And wudu etc.: for wudu is cooling and cleaning of the limbs and wasting water, and that is not good in itself or has no meaning of worship; rather it is good for the sake of performing prayer. 'al-Qarib' (close) and there is discourse in it: that the close of a thing necessitates it be other than it, so necessitating that hudud and the like are good in themselves; for there is no intermediate between 'good by another' and 'good in itself'. Thus if it is close to what is good by another, it necessitates that it is other than it, and other than it is only 'good in itself', based on the absence of an intermediate. That hudud and the like are 'good in themselves' is obviously false, as in some glosses. From this type: because the first category, i.e. walking and wudu, is complete in being good by another; because the other (the intended good) is not attained by the act of the commanded, unlike this category, i.e. hudud, qisas, and jihad; for the other is attained by the act of the commanded. For the hadd is good by means of deterrence from crime, and jihad is good by means of repelling the evil of disbelievers and raising the word of truth; and if we suppose the intermediary absent, it would not remain commanded. For were it not for crime, the hadd would not be obligatory; and were it not for disbelief leading to war, jihad would not be obligatory upon him.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage is an usul definition and categorization of the commanded act (ma'mur bihi) in terms of its goodness (husn). The author, Usul al-Shashi, distinguishes between acts that are good in themselves (hasan li 'aynihi) and those that are good by means of another (hasan li ghayrihi). The focus here is on the second type. Examples include walking to Friday prayer and performing wudu for prayer. The key ruling is that the obligation of the intermediary act depends on the existence of the ultimate end (the other). If the ultimate end is not obligatory on someone (e.g., Friday prayer not obligatory on a traveler), then the intermediary act is also not obligatory. Similarly, if the intermediary is performed but the ultimate end is not achieved due to a break, the intermediary must be repeated. The passage then compares this to hudud, qisas, and jihad, which are 'close' to this type but differ in that the ultimate good (deterrence, defense) is attained by the very act itself. This distinction is crucial in Hanafi usul for determining the conditions of obligation and repetition.

Key Terms
حسن لغيره (hasan li ghayrihi): An act that is good not in itself but because it leads to another good act.
حسن لعينه (hasan li 'aynihi): An act that is good in itself, such as iman or prayer.
واسطة (wasita): The intermediary act that is not the ultimate commanded act.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) A traveler (musafir)
C) A person in i'tikaf in the mosque
D) A person who already performed wudu

Key Takeaway
The goodness of an act can be intrinsic or derived; the obligation of derived acts depends on the obligation of their ends, as illustrated by walking to Friday prayer and wudu.

The Principle of Ada vs Qada: The Obligation to Return the Specific Thing
Usul rule with fiqh applications to deposit, agency, usurpation, and sale defect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that ada is the original rule to determine that in deposits, agency, and usurpation, the specific property must be returned, and substitution is not allowed.

Core Idea
Ada is the origin; qada only when ada is impossible – the specific thing must be returned if possible.

This lesson introduces a foundational usul rule: the default legal requirement is to fulfill the exact obligation (performance in kind). Only when that is impossible does the law move to a substitute (re-performance). This principle governs many fiqh chapters, as illustrated by the examples from wadi'a, wakala, and ghasp.

Guided Path
1
Define ada and qada
Identify the two terms: ada is performing the exact act or delivering the exact item; qada is performing a substitute when the original is impossible.
Hanafi: ada is the asl (original rule).
2
State the rule
The rule: 'The principle in this chapter is ada, whether complete or deficient, and qada is only resorted to when ada is impossible.'
This is a universal usul principle.
3
Apply to wadi'a, wakala, and ghasp
The text says property becomes specific (mut'ayyan) in these contracts; the depositor/agent/usurper cannot keep the specific item and give its equivalent.
Because returning the specific item (ain) is ada; giving equivalent would be qada, which is not allowed while ada is possible.
4
Apply to sale with defect
If a defect appears after delivery, the buyer has the choice to keep the item (ada) or return it (qada), but cannot keep it and claim compensation.
The option is between two forms of ada (keeping) and qada (returning).
5
Recognize the limits of the rule
The rule holds only when ada is physically possible. If the item is destroyed or severely changed, qada becomes obligatory.
This leads to the next lesson on severe change.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

والأصح الإطلاق كما في "الهداية" و"الذخيرة"، وأما لو غصب أرضاً فغرس بها أو بنى قيل له: أقلع البناء والغرس وردَّها لحديث "ليس لعرق ظالم حق" رواه أبو داود والترمذي والنسائي في كتبهم كذا في "الحصول". جميعها للغاصب : أي جميع الأشياء المذكورة للغاصب؛ لأنه أحدث صنعة متقومة مصيرة حق المالك هالكاً من وجه أي من حيث الصورة ألا ترى أنه تبدل الاسم وفات أعظم المقاصد، والشيء إنما يقوم بصورته ومعناه، وحقه في الصنعة قائم من كل وجه، فيترجح على الأصل الذي هو فائت من وجه، ولا يجعل فعله سبباً لملك من حيث أنه مخطور، بل من حيث أنه أحدث الصنعة. ولو غَصَبَ فَضَةً إلخ: هذه عدة مسائل تخالف سائر مسائل الغصب في الحكم، فإنها لا ينقطع عنها حق المالك بعد تلك التغيرات أيضاً بخلاف سائر المسائل، أما في الذهب والفضة فعند أبي حنيفة له، وأما في الشاة فبالإتفاق ووجه الأوليين عند أبي حنيفة : أن العين باق فيهما من كل وجه صورة ومعنى، أما صورة؛ فلبقاء الاسم؛ لأن الدراهم والدنانير يسمى ذهباً وفضة، وأما معنى؛ فلأن المعنى الأصلي وهو الثمنية وكونهما موزونين باق جرى فيه الربا باعتبار الوزن فيهما، ووجه الثالث عند الكل: أن اسمها بعد الذبح باق يقال: شاة مذبوحة كما يقال: شاة حية، وعلى هذا القياس: إذا غصب قطناً ،فغزله، أو غزلاً فنسجه، فإن معظم مقاصدهما هو الغزل والنسج قد حصل وبحصول المقصود لا يعد تغيراً كذا في "المعدن". لا يَنْقَطَعُ إلخ والحاصل أن المؤثر في انقطاع حق المالك ثلاثة أمور : الأول: أن يتغير العين المغصوبة بفعل الغاصب فيزول اسمها و أعظم منافعها، والثاني: أن يختلط بملك الغاصب بحيث يمنع التمييز كخلط الدهن بالدهن، والثالث: أن يختلط بحيث يمكن التمييز لكن يخرج كما إذا غصب البناء فبنى عليها، وهذه الأمور المؤثرة لم توجد في تلك المسائل، فلا ينقطع عنها حق المالك كذا في "المعدن". ظاهر الرواية: المراد بظاهر The first research on the Book of Allah. Chapter on Performance and Re-performance. Then the principle in this chapter: it is performance (ada), whether complete or deficient, and re-performance (qada) is only resorted to when performance is impossible. For this reason, property is specific in deposit, agency, and usurpation. If the depositor, agent, or usurper wants to keep the specific thing and give its equivalent, he does not have that right. If someone sells something and delivers it, and then a defect appears in it, the buyer has the choice between keeping and returning it. And based on the fact that the origin is returning the specific thing (performance), al-Shafi'i says in his place: the obligation is on the original owner of the usurped property, even if it changed in the hand of the usurper with a severe change, and compensation is obligatory due to what he does or due to the defect caused by another's action. According to us, if the usurped property changes by the act of the usurper with a severe change such that its name or greatest benefits are lost, the ownership of the original owner ceases, and the usurper owns it and is liable for it, and it is not permissible for him to benefit until he pays its substitute, as in al-Ma'dan. 'And compensation is obligatory' means the difference between the two values, i.e., the usurped property is valued sound and defective, and the usurper is liable for the increase over the value of the defective. Based on this (i.e., the Shafi'i school we mentioned, that the obligation is to return the usurped property even if it changed severely) 'or a plank, etc.' with jim, a strong solid wood brought from India, called in Hindi 'Sakhu and Sal', used for doors and said to be carved and prepared for foundations. Al-Karkhi and al-Hinduwani said: it is not demolished if he built around the courtyard; but if he built on the plank itself, it is demolished because he is transgressing. The correct view is the absolute, as in al-Hidaya and al-Dhakhira. If he usurped land and planted or built, it is said to him: remove the building and planting and return it, due to the hadith 'There is no right for a wrongful root' narrated by Abu Dawud, al-Tirmidhi, and al-Nasa'i in their books, as in al-Husul. 'All of them for the usurper' means all the mentioned things are for the usurper because he produced a valuable craft that renders the owner's right extinct from one aspect, i.e., from the aspect of form. Do you not see that the name changed and the greatest purposes were lost? A thing is only valued by its form and meaning, and his right in the craft is established from every aspect, so it outweighs the original which is lost from one aspect. And his action is not made a cause for ownership from the aspect that it is prohibited, but from the aspect that he produced the craft. 'And if he usurped silver, etc.' These are several issues that differ from other usurpation cases in the ruling, for the owner's right is not cut off after those changes, unlike other cases. As for gold and silver, according to Abu Hanifa, it is his; as for the sheep, by consensus. The reasoning for the first two according to Abu Hanifa: the property remains in both from every aspect, form and meaning. As for form, due to the remaining name, because dirhams and dinars are called gold and silver. As for meaning, because the original meaning, which is being a medium of exchange and being weighed, remains; riba is considered by weight in them. The reasoning for the third according to all: its name after slaughter remains; it is said 'slaughtered sheep' just as it is said 'living sheep'. By analogy: if he usurped cotton and spun it, or yarn and wove it, then most of its purposes, spinning and weaving, have been achieved, and with the achievement of the purpose it is not considered a change, as in al-Ma'dan. 'The owner's right is not cut off, etc.' The summary is that what affects the cutting off of the owner's right are three things: first, that the usurped property changes by the act of the usurper such that its name and greatest benefits are lost; second, that it mixes with the usurper's property in a way that prevents distinction, like mixing oil with oil; third, that it mixes in a way that distinction is possible but it is removed, like if he usurped a building and built on it. These effective things were not found in those cases, so the owner's right is not cut off, as in al-Ma'dan. 'Zahir al-Riwaya': by zahir al-riwaya is meant the foundational issues that were transmitted from Abu Hanifa and his two companions; they are: al-Mabsut, al-Ziyadat, al-Jami' al-Saghir, al-Jami' al-Kabir, al-Siyar al-Saghir, al-Siyar al-Kabir. And these books are called...
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the chapter of Performance (Ada) and Re-performance (Qada) from the Usul al-Shashi. It begins by stating the fundamental principle: the original obligation is performance (ada), whether complete or deficient, and qada is only resorted to when ada is impossible. This is a key usul rule in Hanafi fiqh, applied across various chapters. The text then applies this rule to specific cases: in deposit (wadi'a), agency (wakala), and usurpation (ghasp), the specific property (ain) must be returned because it is the origin (ada); the alternative of paying an equivalent (qada) is not permissible as long as returning the original is possible. Similarly, in a sale, if a defect appears after delivery, the buyer has the choice to keep the item (ada) or return it (qada), but cannot keep it and claim compensation for the defect. The passage then contrasts the Hanafi position with that of Imam al-Shafi'i. According to al-Shafi'i, in ghasp, the original property must always be returned, even if it has undergone a severe change (taghayyur fahish). The Hanafi school, however, distinguishes: if the change is so severe that the name (ism) or the greatest benefits (a'zam al-manafi') are lost, then the original owner's ownership ceases, and the usurper becomes owner and must pay the value. Examples include grinding wheat into flour, building a house with a plank, or slaughtering and roasting a sheep—in these cases, the Hanafi ruling is that the usurper becomes owner because the original form and primary benefits are gone. The text further discusses cases where the property remains identifiably the same, such as silver beaten into coins, gold made into dinars, or a slaughtered sheep. Here, the owner's right is not cut off, and the usurper must return the specific items. This is because the name (e.g., still called silver/sheep) and the essential benefit (thumaniyya for silver, meat for sheep) remain. The commentary then summarizes three factors that cut off the original owner's right: 1) severe change (loss of name and main benefits), 2) mixing that prevents distinction, and 3) mixing where distinction is still possible but the property is removed (as when building on land). Finally, it defines 'Zahir al-Riwaya' as the six foundational books transmitted from Abu Hanifa and his two companions: al-Mabsut, al-Ziyadat, al-Jami' al-Saghir, al-Jami' al-Kabir, al-Siyar al-Saghir, and al-Siyar al-Kabir. This passage thus provides a comprehensive usul principle and its detailed application to ghasp, including a comparison with the Shafi'i school and an explanation of the authoritative transmission (Zahir al-Riwaya).

Key Terms
الأداء (al-ada): Performance of an obligation by delivering the exact same thing (ain) or performing the exact act.
القضاء (al-qada): Re-performance or substitution when the original performance (ada) is impossible.
العين (al-ayn): A specific, identifiable property, as opposed to a debt (dayn).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling if a depositor (wadi') wants to keep the deposited item and give its equivalent?
A) He may do so with the depositor's permission.
B) He must return the exact item; giving equivalent is not allowed.
C) He may choose to give equivalent if the item has not changed.
D) He must pay the value of the item if he keeps it.

Key Takeaway
The default obligation is to perform (ada) in kind; substitution (qada) is only allowed when performance in kind is impossible.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
8 min
Severe Change (Taghayyur Fahish) in Usurpation: Hanafi vs Shafi'i
Inter-madhhab dispute with detailed usul analysis of severe change in ghasp.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on taghayyur fahish in ghasp, identify the three factors that cut off the owner's right, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
When a usurper severely changes a property (loss of name or main benefit), ownership transfers to the usurper; otherwise, the original must be returned.

This lesson delves into a key application of the ada vs qada principle: what happens when a usurper changes the property? The Hanafi and Shafi'i schools diverge on whether the original must always be returned. Understanding the conditions that transfer ownership is crucial for muamalat.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi rule on severe change
If the usurper causes a severe change (taghayyur fahish) such that the object loses its name (ism) or its greatest benefits (a'zam al-manafi'), the original owner's ownership ceases, the usurper becomes owner and must pay the value (qima).
Examples: grinding wheat into flour, building with a plank, slaughtering and roasting a sheep.
2
Present the Shafi'i position
Al-Shafi'i holds that the usurper must always return the original property even if severely changed, and also pay compensation for the defect (arsh).
This is based on the principle that ownership of the property is not extinguished by change.
3
Identify the dalil from hadith
The hadith 'There is no right for a wrongful root' (laysa li-'irq zalimin haqq) is cited to support the Hanafi view that the usurper's structure on land is not retained; he must remove it, but the text uses it to illustrate that the craft may give ownership in some cases.
The hadith is from Abu Dawud, Tirmidhi, Nasa'i, and is ahad (zanni al-thubut).
4
List the three factors that cut off the owner's right
The text summarizes three factors: (1) severe change (loss of name and main benefits), (2) mixing that prevents distinction (e.g., oil with oil), (3) mixing that permits distinction but the property is removed (e.g., building on land).
These factors are taken from al-Ma'dan.
5
Analyze exceptions for gold, silver, and sheep
For gold and silver (according to Abu Hanifa) and for a slaughtered sheep (by consensus), the owner's right is not cut off because the name and essential benefits remain (e.g., still called gold, silver, sheep).
Similarly, cotton spun into yarn or yarn woven into cloth: the main purposes are achieved, so no severe change.

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the chapter of Performance (Ada) and Re-performance (Qada) from the Usul al-Shashi. It begins by stating the fundamental principle: the original obligation is performance (ada), whether complete or deficient, and qada is only resorted to when ada is impossible. This is a key usul rule in Hanafi fiqh, applied across various chapters. The text then applies this rule to specific cases: in deposit (wadi'a), agency (wakala), and usurpation (ghasp), the specific property (ain) must be returned because it is the origin (ada); the alternative of paying an equivalent (qada) is not permissible as long as returning the original is possible. Similarly, in a sale, if a defect appears after delivery, the buyer has the choice to keep the item (ada) or return it (qada), but cannot keep it and claim compensation for the defect. The passage then contrasts the Hanafi position with that of Imam al-Shafi'i. According to al-Shafi'i, in ghasp, the original property must always be returned, even if it has undergone a severe change (taghayyur fahish). The Hanafi school, however, distinguishes: if the change is so severe that the name (ism) or the greatest benefits (a'zam al-manafi') are lost, then the original owner's ownership ceases, and the usurper becomes owner and must pay the value. Examples include grinding wheat into flour, building a house with a plank, or slaughtering and roasting a sheep—in these cases, the Hanafi ruling is that the usurper becomes owner because the original form and primary benefits are gone. The text further discusses cases where the property remains identifiably the same, such as silver beaten into coins, gold made into dinars, or a slaughtered sheep. Here, the owner's right is not cut off, and the usurper must return the specific items. This is because the name (e.g., still called silver/sheep) and the essential benefit (thumaniyya for silver, meat for sheep) remain. The commentary then summarizes three factors that cut off the original owner's right: 1) severe change (loss of name and main benefits), 2) mixing that prevents distinction, and 3) mixing where distinction is still possible but the property is removed (as when building on land). Finally, it defines 'Zahir al-Riwaya' as the six foundational books transmitted from Abu Hanifa and his two companions: al-Mabsut, al-Ziyadat, al-Jami' al-Saghir, al-Jami' al-Kabir, al-Siyar al-Saghir, and al-Siyar al-Kabir. This passage thus provides a comprehensive usul principle and its detailed application to ghasp, including a comparison with the Shafi'i school and an explanation of the authoritative transmission (Zahir al-Riwaya).

Key Terms
الأداء (al-ada): Performance of an obligation by delivering the exact same thing (ain) or performing the exact act.
القضاء (al-qada): Re-performance or substitution when the original performance (ada) is impossible.
تغير فاحش (taghayyur fahish): A severe change in the property such that its name (ism) or greatest benefits (a'zam al-manafi') are lost.
صنعة متقومة (sina'a mutaqawwima): A valuable craft or transformation that creates a new property with monetary value.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a usurper takes a piece of land and builds a house on it, what is the ruling regarding the ownership of the house?
A) The usurper owns the house and must pay the value of the land.
B) The usurper must demolish the house and return the land.
C) The owner owns the house and must pay the usurper for construction costs.
D) The house becomes joint property between owner and usurper.

Key Takeaway
Severe change (taghayyur fahish) transfers ownership to the usurper; otherwise the specific property must be returned. Gold, silver, and slaughtered sheep are exceptions where the owner's right remains.

Definition and Types of Prohibition (Nahy)
Usul definition: nahy and its two types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define nahy in Hanafi usul, distinguish sensory from Shar'i prohibitions, and apply the rule that Shar'i prohibitions are for something else unless evidence indicates otherwise.

Core Idea
A prohibition is not always about the act itself; for Shar'i acts, the evil may be external.

This passage from Usul al-Shashi defines nahy (prohibition) and divides it into two categories: prohibitions of sensory acts and prohibitions of Shar'i acts. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying the ruling of a prohibition in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of nahy
Read the linguistic and technical definitions of nahy. Note that it is a request to abandon from a superior to an inferior.
The form 'Do not do' is used in many meanings, but its immediate implication is prohibition.
2
Recognize the multiple meanings of nahy
Identify that nahy can indicate prohibition, dislike, clarification, supplication, etc. By consensus, the form is metaphorical for other meanings except prohibition and dislike.
The chosen view is that nahy requires prohibition (tahrim).
3
Distinguish the two types of prohibitions
Sensory acts (af'al hissiyya) are known by sense and independent of Shari'ah, like adultery and drinking. Shar'i acts (tasarrufat shar'iyya) are defined by Shari'ah, like fasting, prayer, and sale.
Sensory acts are evil in themselves (qabih li-aynihi); Shar'i acts are evil for something else (qabih li-ghayrihi).
4
Analyze the ruling of each type
For the first type, the prohibition applies directly to the act itself. For the second type, the prohibition is attached to an external factor or attribute, not the act's essence.
Example: fasting on Eid is prohibited because it turns away from Allah's hospitality, not because fasting itself is evil.
5
Examine the Hanafi-Shafi'i dispute
The Hanafi school holds that Shar'i prohibitions are qabih li-ghayrihi unless evidence shows otherwise. The Shafi'i school defaults to qabih li-aynihi unless evidence shows otherwise.
The practical consequence is how we determine sinfulness and excuse.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في النهي: وهو المنع لغة، وفي اصطلاح أهل الأصول: هو استدعاء و ترك الفعل بالقول ممن هو دونه، وقيل: وهو قول القائل لغيره: لا تفعل على سبيل الاستعلاء، واختلفوا في موجبه على حسب اختلافهم في موجب الأمر لاستعماله في معان كثيرة كالتحريم والكراهة، وبيان العاقبة ،والدعاء والشفقة والإرشاد، والتحقير واليأس، لكن صيغته مجاز في غير التحريم، والكراهة بالاتفاق والمختار أن موجبة التحريم كذا في "الفصول".
والنَّهي نوعان: نهي عن الأفعال الحسية كالزنا وشرب الخمر، والكذب والظلم. ونَهي عن التصرُّفات الشرعيَّة: كالنَّهي عن الصوم في يوم النحر، والصلاة في الأوقات المكروهة وبيع الدرهم بالدرهمين.
الأفعال الحسية: وهي التي تعرف حساً ولا يتوقف تحققها على الشرع كالزنا، وشرب الخمر؛ فإنهما كانا معلومين ماهية قبل ورود الشرع، وباقيتين على حالهما، ولا يتغير بالشرع. كالزنا وشُرب الخمر إلخ: فإنها أفعال حسية يعني يتحقق حساً عند من يعلم الشرع ومن لا يعلم، ولا يتوقف وجودها على الشرع.
وحكم النوع الأوَّل: أن يكون المنهي عنه هو عينَ ما وَرَدَ عليه النهي فيكون عينه قبيحاً، فلا يكون مشروعاً أصلاً.
وحكم النوع الثاني: أن يكون المنهي عنه غير ما أضيف إليه النهي، فيكون هو بنفسه قبيحاً لغيره، ويكون المباشر مرتكباً للحرام لغيره لا لنفسه.
النهي عن الأفعال الشرعية كالنهي عن الصوم فإن الصوم لغة: الإمساك، ثم زيد عليه الوقت ،والنية والطهارة من الحيض والنفاس شرعاً، والصلاة لغة: الدعاء، وزيد عليها في الشرع أشياء هي الأركان كالقيام والقراءة والركوع والسجود، وشروط الحدث كالطهارة من وستر العورة، والاستقبال والنية وكذا زيد في البيع والإجارة على المعنى اللغوي أشياء شرعية يرجع بعضها إلى الأهل وبعضها إلى المحل، فكانت هذه الأشياء أموراً شرعية؛ لأنها توقفت على الشرع كذا في "المعدن".
فلا يكون مشروعاً: ويلحق بهذا النوع ما قبح لعينه شرعاً كالصلاة بغير طهارة وبيع الحر. [حاشية الشيخ أكرم الندوي: ص ۱۲۳] أصلاً أي لا ذاتاً ولا وصفاً، وهذا لا خلاف فيه لأحد؛ لأن النهي يدل على القبح في عنه، فينصرفه إلى الكامل وهو ما قبح لعينه، إلا إذا قام الدليل على خلافه فحينئذ يصير قبيحاً لمعنى في غيره الدواب كراسياً، والمشي في فعل واحد وغير ذلك من المناهي التي وردت عن الأفعال الحسية، فإن الدليل قد دل على النهي عنها بمعنى الأذى والشفقة لا بعين هذه الأشياء. وحكمُ النوع الثاني: وهذا القسم يتنوع نوعين: نوع منه ما هو قبيح لمعنى جاوره جمعاً كوطء الحائض، والبيع عند أذان الجمعة والصلاة في الأرض المغصوبة. ونوع منه ما هو قبح لمعنى اتصل به وصفاً كالربا، وصوم يوم النحر، وألحق الشافعي الله هذا النوع إلى القسم الأول وهو ما قبح ل(عينه إلا بدليل، وعند الحنفية هذا النوع يلحق بالقسم الثاني (وهو ما قبح (لغيره إلا بدليل. [حاشية الشيخ أكرم الندوي: ص ۱۲۳] غير ما أضيف إليه إلخ كالصوم في يوم النحر منهي عنه؛ لقوله عليه: "ألا لا تصوموا في هذه الأيام"، فالمنهي عنه هو الإعراض عنه ضيافة الله تعالى، فإن الإعراض منهي عنه؛ لقوله علية : "من لم يجب الدعوة فقد عصانا".
Concerning prohibition: It is prevention linguistically, and in the terminology of the scholars of Usul: it is a request to abandon an act by speech from one who is above the addressee. And it is said: it is one's saying to another: 'Do not do' in a commanding manner. And they differed about its requirement according to their difference about the requirement of command, due to its usage in many meanings like prohibition, dislike, clarification of consequence, supplication, compassion, guidance, belittlement, and despair. But its form is metaphorical in other than prohibition and dislike by consensus, and the chosen view is that its requirement is prohibition, as in 'al-Fusul'. And prohibition is of two types: prohibition of sensory acts like adultery, drinking wine, lying, and injustice. And prohibition of Shar'i acts: like the prohibition of fasting on the day of sacrifice, prayer in disliked times, and selling a dirham for two dirhams. Sensory acts: those which are known by sense and whose realization does not depend on Shari'ah, like adultery and drinking wine; for their essence was known before the coming of Shari'ah, and they remain as they were, and are not changed by Shari'ah. Like adultery and drinking wine etc.: for they are sensory acts meaning they are realized by sense for those who know Shari'ah and those who do not, and their existence does not depend on Shari'ah. The ruling of the first type: that the prohibited thing is exactly what the prohibition came upon, so it is evil in itself, and it is not legislated at all. The ruling of the second type: that the prohibited thing is other than what the prohibition is attached to, so it is evil for something else, and the doer commits sin for something else not for itself. Prohibition of Shar'i acts like the prohibition of fasting: for fasting linguistically is abstention, then time, intention, purity from menstruation and postpartum bleeding were added by Shari'ah. And prayer linguistically is supplication, and added to it in Shari'ah are the pillars like standing, recitation, bowing, and prostration, and conditions of purification like purification from, covering the private parts, facing the qiblah, and intention. Similarly, in sale and lease, Shar'i matters were added to the linguistic meaning, some pertaining to capacity and some to the subject matter. So these matters are Shar'i because they depend on Shari'ah, as in 'al-Ma'din'. So it is not legislated: and to this type is attached what is evil for itself by Shari'ah like prayer without purification and selling a free person. [Margin note of Shaykh Akram al-Nadwi: p. 123] Absolutely, i.e., neither in essence nor attribute, and there is no disagreement on this from anyone; because prohibition indicates evil in the prohibited thing, so it directs to the complete, which is what is evil for itself, unless evidence indicates otherwise, then it becomes evil for a meaning in something else. ... And the ruling of the second type: and this section is divided into two types: one type is what is evil because of a meaning that accompanies it externally, like intercourse with a menstruating woman, selling at the time of the Friday call to prayer, and prayer in usurped land. And one type is what is evil because of a meaning connected to it as an attribute, like usury and fasting on the day of sacrifice. Al-Shafi'i annexed this type to the first section, which is what is evil for itself unless evidence indicates otherwise, while according to the Hanafis, this type is annexed to the second section, which is what is evil for something else unless evidence indicates otherwise. [Margin note of Shaykh Akram al-Nadwi: p. 123] Other than what it is attached to, etc., like fasting on the day of sacrifice is prohibited due to his saying peace be upon him: 'Do not fast on these days.' So the prohibited thing is turning away from Allah's hospitality, for turning away is prohibited due to his saying: 'Whoever does not respond to the invitation has disobeyed us.'
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage defines nahy (prohibition) and outlines its types and rulings. Linguistically, nahy means prevention. In Usul, it is a request to abandon an act from a superior to an inferior. The form 'Do not do' is used in various meanings, but by consensus it is metaphorical except for prohibition and dislike. The chosen view is that its immediate requirement is prohibition. The author then divides nahy into two types: (1) prohibition of sensory acts (af'al hissiyya) which are inherently evil and known by sense independent of Shari'ah, such as adultery, drinking wine, lying, and injustice. These acts are evil in themselves (qabih li-aynihi) and are not legislated at all. (2) prohibition of Shar'i acts (tasarrufat shar'iyya) which are acts that have a linguistical meaning but are defined by Shari'ah, such as fasting, prayer, sale, and lease. The prohibition applies to certain instances of these acts (e.g., fasting on the day of sacrifice, prayer in disliked times, and selling a dirham for two dirhams). For these, the prohibited thing is not the act itself (e.g., fasting in general) but the particular instance that conflicts with a Shari'ah command (e.g., fasting on the day of sacrifice conflicts with the command to accept Allah's hospitality). Therefore, such acts are evil for something else (qabih li-ghayrihi). The passage then explains that the first type (sensory) is prohibited for itself, and there is no disagreement. The second type (Shar'i) is divided further: (a) evil due to an external accompanying factor (like intercourse during menstruation due to harm), and (b) evil due to an inherent attribute (like usury and fasting on the day of sacrifice). The Shafi'i school considers the latter (b) as evil for itself unless evidence indicates it is for something else, while the Hanafi school considers it evil for something else unless evidence indicates it is for itself. The example given is fasting on the day of sacrifice: the prohibition is not on fasting per se but on the act of refusing Allah's invitation, which is the real prohibited thing.

Key Terms
النهي (al-nahy): A request to abandon an act from a superior to an inferior, often in the form 'Do not do'.
الأفعال الحسية (al-af'al al-hissiyya): Actions that are known by the senses and whose existence does not depend on Shari'ah.
التصرفات الشرعية (al-tasarrufat al-shar'iyya): Actions that are defined by Shari'ah, having a linguistic basis with additional Shar'i conditions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is fasting on the day of sacrifice prohibited?
A) Because fasting itself is evil on that day.
B) Because it represents turning away from Allah's hospitality.
C) Because it is a sensory act.
D) Because it is usury.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, the prohibition of Shar'i acts indicates evil for something else unless evidence shows it is for the act itself.

The Hanafi Principle: Prohibition Does Not Invalidate Shar'i Acts
Explaining the Hanafi rule on prohibition's effect on shar'i transactions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that prohibition of shar'i acts leaves their essence valid, and distinguish between cases where prohibition is a true nahy (retaining validity) and cases where it is naskh/nafy (negation).

Core Idea
Prohibition of a shar'i act confirms its validity; otherwise, the prohibition would be impossible.

This passage from Usul al-Shashi sets out a foundational Hanafi rule: when a shar'i act is prohibited, it does not become intrinsically invalid. Instead, its essential validity remains, but it becomes unlawful due to an external factor (haram li-ghayrihi). The proof is rational: if prohibition nullified the act, the act would become inconceivable, making the prohibition a command to the incapable—absurd. This principle has important applications, such as in invalid contracts (bay' fasid) and explains why some prohibitions (like marrying relatives) are treated as negation rather than retention.

Guided Path
1
Define key terms
Identify what a 'shar'i act' (tasarruf shar'i) is—an act whose legal validity depends on shari'a (e.g., sale, marriage, prayer). Contrast with 'sensory acts' (af'al hissiyya) like adultery or murder, which exist regardless of shari'a.
Shari'a can declare sensory acts ugly, but they remain conceivable; shar'i acts require shari'a for their very conception.
2
Grasp the rational proof
The authors argue: if prohibition removed the act's legitimacy, the act would become inconceivable in shari'a, making the prohibition an address to an incapable person (nahy al-'ajiz), which is impossible for the Lawgiver.
This is a reductio ad absurdum: assuming nullification leads to impossibility, so the assumption is false.
3
Apply to an example: invalid sale
Consider a sale with an invalid condition (e.g., seller retains service for a month). The prohibition is due to an external factor (the condition). The sale remains valid in its essence (offer and acceptance from competent parties on a valid subject), so ownership transfers upon possession, but it must be rescinded (wajib al-naqd).
The condition does not affect the essence; it is an extra, so the act is qabih li-ghayrihi (ugly due to something else).
4
Recognize the exception: conflicting consequence
When the prohibition contradicts the very consequence of the act (e.g., marriage leads to permissibility of intimacy, but prohibition demands its unlawfulness), the two cannot coexist. In such cases, the prohibition is interpreted as negation (nafy) or abrogation (naskh), not as a confirming prohibition.
Examples: marrying polytheist women, stepmothers, relatives. These marriages are not just invalid but absolutely non-existent in shari'a.

Source Text

البحث الأول في كتاب الله
فصل في النهي
وعلى هذا قال أصحابنا : النهي عن التصرفات الشرعية يقتضي تقريرها ويُراد بذلك أن التصرف بعد النهي يبقى مشروعاً كما كان؛ لأنه لو لم يَبْقَ مشروعاً كان العبد عاجزاً عن تحصيل المشروع، وحينئذ كان ذلك نهياً للعاجز، وذلك من الشارع محال وبه فارق الأفعال الحسية؛ لأنه لو كان عينها قبيحاً لا يؤدى ذلك إلى نهى العاجز؛ لأنه بهذا الوصف لا يعجز العبد عن الفعل الحسي.
ويتفرع من هذا : حكم البيع الفاسد، والإجارة الفاسدة، والنذر بصوم يوم النحر، وجميع صور التصرفات الشرعية مع ورود النهي عنها .. فقلنا : البيع الفاسد يفيد الملك عند القبض باعتبار أنه بيع ويجب نقضه باعتبار كونه حراماً لغيره.
وعلى هذا: أي على أن النهي عن التصرفات الشرعية يكون حسناً بنفسه قبيحاً لغيره، قال أصحابنا : أن النهي الأفعال الشرعية كالصلاة والصوم والبيع وسائر العبادات أو المعاملات، يقع على القسم الأخير، وهو الذي يكون القبح فيه لغيره متصلاً به وصفاً حتى يبقى المنهي عنه بعد النهي مشروعاً بأصله عندنا وإن لم يكن مشروعاً بوصفه فافهم.
كان العبد عاجزاً إلخ لأن التصرف الشرعي بعد النهي لو لم يبق مشروعاً لم يكن متصوراً؛ لأن تصور الفعل الشرعي إنما يكون من حيث الشرع، وهو إنما يكون إذا كان مشروعاً إذا لم يكن متصور الوجوه يكون مقدور العبد، فيكون النهي عنه نهياً للعاجز، ونهي العاجز قبيح، وهو من الشارع محال؛ لأنه بمنزلة قول القائل للأعمى: لا تبصر ، وللأمي: لا تقرأ فهو قبيح، فكذا هذا بخلاف الحسي؛ لأن تصوره لا يعتمد المشروعية. العبد إلخ لأن تصوره لا يعتمد المشروعية؛ لأنه أمكن تحقق هذه الأفعال يعجز صفة القبح؛ لأنها توجد حساً، فلا يمتنع وجودها بسبب القبح، والمراد بالأفعال الحسية: ما يعرف حساً ولا يتوقف تحققها على الشرع، وقيل: ما يكون معانيها المعلومة قبل الشرع باقية على حالها بعد ورود الشرع لم يتغير به أصلاً كالزنا والقتل وشرب الخمر وغير ذلك كذا في "المعدن".

وهذا بخلاف نكاح المشركات ومنكوحة الأب، ومعتدة الغير ومنكوحته، ونكاح بالطلاق أو الموت المحارم، والنكاح بغير شهود؛ لأن موجب النكاح حل التصرف وموجَبَ الاستمتاع بالبضع، فاستحال الجمع بينهما، فيحمل النهي على النفي. الحل والحرمة المذكور
فأَمَّا موجَبُ البيع : ثبوت الملك، وموجب النهي: حرمة التصرف، في المنهي عنه النهي حَرمَةُ عن الشيء عنه بما روي أنه عليها نهى عن بيع حكم البيع الفاسد : بأن باع عبداً بشرط أن يخدم البائع شهراً، وهو منهي و شرط وغير ذلك؛ فإن النهي فيه ورد لمعنى في غير البيع، وهو الفضل الخالي عن العوض والشرط الفاسد فلا ينعدم به أصل المشروع؛ لأنه إيجاب وقبول من أهله في محله.
منهي يفيد الملك إلخ: لأنه إيجاب وقبول صدر من أهله في محله، ولا يختل من ذلك بشرط فاسد، فكان أمراً زائداً على العقد، فكان البيع الفاسد عقداً شرعياً بأصله، فكان مفيداً للملك، لكن لما كان حراماً شرعاً كان واجب النقض حقاً للشرع. وهذا بخلاف نكاح إلخ هذا جواب إشكال يرد على تفريع حكم البيع الفاسد، أو جواب نقض يرد على الكلي المذكور أعني أن النهي عن الأفعال الشرعية يقتضي بقاء المشروعية؛ وذلك لأن نكاح المشركات عنه؛ لقوله تعالى: ﴿وَلا تَنْكِحُوا الْمُشْرِكَاتِ﴾، ونكاح منكوحة الأب منهي عنه؛ لقوله تعالى: ﴿وَلا تَنْكِحُوا مَا نَكَحَ آبَاؤُكُمْ﴾ الآية، والنكاح فعل شرعي مع أنه لم يبق مشروعاً أصلاً، وكذلك قوله تعالى: ﴿حُرِّمَتْ عَلَيْكُمْ أُمَّهَاتُكُمْ﴾ إلى قوله: ﴿وَالْمُحْصَنَاتُ مِنَ النِّسَاءِ﴾ في معنى النهي عن نكاح المحارم وغيرها؛ لأن التحريم والنهي كل منهما بمعنى المنع، ومعتدة الغير في معنى منكوحة الغير، والنكاح بغير شهود منهي عنه؛ لقوله عليه: لا نكاح إلا بشهود ، ولأن النفي بمعنى النهي، ولا يلزم أن لا يقع النكاح بغير شهود، وقد يقع فيلزم الخلف في كلام الشارع وهو منتف في كلامه بالاتفاق، فأجاب بأن القول ببقاء المشروعية إنما هو فيما أمكن إثبات الحرمة مع شرعية موجبة، وههنا لم يكن ذلك؛ لأن موجب النكاح حل التصرف، وموجب النهي حرمة التصرف وهما متنافيان، فاستحال الجمع بينهما، فيحمل النهي المذكور على النفي والنسخ والنفي لا يقتضي بقاء المشروعية؛ لأن بقاءها إنما يلزم في النفي ضرورة اقتضاء تصور الفعل ليكون العبد مبتلى بين أن يأتي باختياره أو يترك باختياره، ولا ابتلاء في النفي فافهم كذا في "الفصول". والنكاح بغير شهود أي لا يلزم على أصلنا المحكم الاعتراض من جهة النكاح بغير شهود؛ لأنه منفي بقوله عليه: "لا نكاح إلا بشهود" رواه الدار قطني، فكان نسخاً لا نهياً، وكان كلامنا في النهي دون النفي والنسخ، فيكون ذلك إخباراً عن عدمه كقولك: "لا رجل في الدار"، وذلك لا يوجب بقاء المشروعية، بل يوجب انتفاءها ضرورة صدق الخبر.
The first research: Concerning the Book of Allah. Chapter on Prohibition. And upon this our companions said: Prohibition of shar'i transactions necessitates their confirmation, meaning that the transaction after prohibition remains legitimate as it was; because if it did not remain legitimate, the servant would be incapable of attaining what is legitimate, and then that would be a prohibition of the incapable, and that from the Lawgiver is impossible. And by this it differs from sensory acts; because if the act itself were inherently ugly, that would not lead to prohibition of the incapable; because with this description the servant is not incapable of the sensory act. And from this branches the ruling of an invalid sale, invalid lease, the vow to fast on the day of sacrifice, and all forms of shar'i transactions when prohibition comes upon them. So we said: An invalid sale confers ownership upon possession insofar as it is a sale, and it must be rescinded insofar as it is unlawful due to something external. And upon this: i.e., that prohibition of shar'i transactions is good in itself but ugly due to something else, our companions said: That the prohibition of shar'i acts like prayer, fasting, sale, and all acts of worship or dealings falls under the last category, which is that the ugliness is due to something else attached to it as a description, so that the prohibited act remains legitimate in its essence according to us, even if not legitimate in its description. So understand. 'The servant would be incapable' etc. Because if the shar'i transaction after prohibition did not remain legitimate, it would not be conceivable; for the conception of a shar'i act is only from the perspective of the law, and that is only if it is legitimate; if it is not conceivable, it cannot be within the servant's capability, so the prohibition of it would be a prohibition of the incapable, and prohibition of the incapable is ugly, and that from the Lawgiver is impossible; for it is like one saying to a blind person: 'Do not see', and to an illiterate: 'Do not read'—that is ugly. Similarly this. Unlike the sensory; because its conception does not depend on legitimacy; because it is possible for these acts to occur despite the attribute of ugliness, because they exist sensorily, so their existence is not prevented by ugliness. And by sensory acts is meant what is known by the senses and whose realization does not depend on the law; and it is said: what has its known meanings before the law remaining as they are after the law's advent, not changed by it at all, like adultery, murder, drinking wine, and others. Thus in 'al-Ma'din'. This is contrary to marriage of polytheist women, marriage of a stepmother, marriage of a woman in her 'iddah belonging to another, marriage of his own wife? [text unclear], marriage after divorce or death of relatives, marriage without witnesses; because the consequence of marriage is permissibility of intimacy and the consequence of prohibition is its unlawfulness, so it is impossible to combine them, so the prohibition is interpreted as negation. As for the consequence of sale: establishment of ownership, and the consequence of prohibition: unlawfulness of the transaction. The ruling of an invalid sale: e.g., selling a slave with the condition that he serve the seller for a month, which is prohibited due to a condition and other things; for the prohibition here came for a reason external to the sale, i.e., excess free from compensation and the invalid condition, so the essence of the transaction is not nullified; because it is offer and acceptance from competent parties concerning a valid subject matter. It confers ownership etc.: because it is offer and acceptance from competent parties concerning a valid subject matter, and it is not undermined by an invalid condition, which is an extra matter beyond the contract, so the invalid sale is a legally valid contract in its essence, so it confers ownership; but since it is legally unlawful, it must be rescinded as a right of the Law. This is contrary to marriage etc. This is a response to an objection raised against the branching of the ruling of an invalid sale, or a counter-argument against the aforementioned general principle that prohibition of shar'i acts implies continued validity; because marriage of polytheist women is prohibited by His saying: 'Do not marry polytheist women', and marriage of a stepmother is prohibited by His saying: 'Do not marry what your fathers married' the verse, and marriage is a shar'i act yet it does not remain legitimate at all; similarly His saying: 'Forbidden to you are your mothers' up to 'and married women' in the sense of prohibition of marriage of relatives etc.; for both prohibition and forbiddance mean prevention; and a woman in another's 'iddah is like another's wife; and marriage without witnesses is prohibited by his saying: 'No marriage except with witnesses'; and negation is in the sense of prohibition, and it does not necessitate that marriage without witnesses does not occur; it may occur, and then there would be a contradiction in the Lawgiver's speech, which is impossible by consensus. So he answered: The statement about continued validity applies only where it is possible to establish unlawfulness while maintaining the legitimacy of its consequence; but here that is not the case; because the consequence of marriage is permissibility of intimacy, and the consequence of prohibition is unlawfulness of intimacy, which are contradictory, so it is impossible to combine them; so the aforementioned prohibition is interpreted as negation and abrogation; and negation does not necessitate continued validity; because continued validity is necessitated in prohibition due to the necessity of conceiving the act so that the servant is tested to do it or leave it by choice; but there is no test in negation. So understand. Thus in 'al-Fusul'. And marriage without witnesses: i.e., the objection does not arise against our established principle concerning marriage without witnesses; because it is negated by his saying: 'No marriage except with witnesses' narrated by al-Daraqutni, so it is abrogation not prohibition; and our discussion is about prohibition, not negation and abrogation; so that is an announcement of its non-existence like saying: 'There is no man in the house', and that does not necessitate continued validity, but rather necessitates its non-existence due to the truthfulness of the statement.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage from Usul al-Shashi explains a key Hanafi principle regarding the effect of legal prohibition (nahy) on shar'i acts (acts whose validity depends on shari'a, like contracts and worship). The author begins by stating the rule: when a shar'i act is prohibited, the prohibition does not nullify the act's essential validity; it remains legitimate in its essence (aslan). The proof is rational: if prohibition removed the act's legitimacy entirely, the act would become inconceivable in shari'a, making the prohibition an address to the incapable (nahy al-'ajiz), which is impossible coming from the Lawgiver. This is likened to telling a blind person 'don't see'—it is absurd. This distinguishes shar'i acts from purely sensory acts (like adultery, murder) which are inherently evil (qabih bi 'aynihi) and whose occurrence does not depend on shari'a; they can exist despite prohibition, so the prohibition is meaningful. From this principle, the Hanafis derive that an invalid sale (bay' fasid)—e.g., a sale with an invalid condition—still transfers ownership upon possession because its essence (offer and acceptance from competent parties on a valid subject) is valid; the prohibition is due to an external factor (the condition). Therefore, the contract is valid in origin but must be rescinded because it is haram li-ghayrihi (unlawful due to an external cause). The passage then addresses exceptions that seem to contradict the rule: marriages prohibited by explicit Quranic verses (e.g., marrying polytheist women, stepmothers, relatives) and marriage without witnesses. In these cases, the marriage does not remain valid at all. The resolution is that these prohibitions are not of the same type; here the consequence (musabbab) of the act (permissibility of intimacy) is directly contradictory to the prohibition (unlawfulness of intimacy). Since they cannot coexist, the prohibition is interpreted as negation (nafy) or abrogation (naskh), not as a prohibition that leaves the act's essence intact. In negation, the act is simply non-existent in shari'a, so the test of choice does not apply. This is the Hanafi way of reconciling the general principle with specific textual prohibitions.

Key Terms
النهي (al-nahy): A divine address demanding abstention from an act.
التصرف الشرعي (al-tasarruf al-shar'i): An act whose legal validity depends on shari'a, such as contracts and worship.
القبح (al-qubh): Ugliness or evil; an attribute of an act that can be intrinsic (bi al-dhat) or extrinsic (bi al-ghayr).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi principle explained in this passage, what is the effect of a prohibition on a shar'i transaction like a sale?
A) The transaction becomes completely invalid and has no legal effect.
B) The transaction remains valid in its essence but is unlawful due to an external factor, requiring rescission.
C) The transaction becomes permissible as long as the prohibition is ignored.
D) The prohibition is considered abrogated because it is impossible to obey.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, prohibition of shar'i acts confirms their essential validity; the act becomes qabih li-ghayrihi (unlawful due to an external factor) and must be rescinded, but is not void ab initio.

The Effect of Prohibition (Nahy) on the Validity of Vows: A Hanafi Usul Principle
Establishing that prohibition does not negate essential legitimacy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that prohibition of legitimate acts does not negate their essential validity to derive rulings on vows during prohibited times.

Core Idea
Prohibition on an act does not void its essential legitimacy — the vow remains valid.

In this lesson, we explore a key Hanafi usul principle: when an act is prohibited due to a contingent description (wasf) but remains legitimate in essence (asl), a vow involving that act is valid. The text uses the example of fasting on prohibited days (Eid al-Adha and Tashriq) and contrasts the Hanafi position with that of Zufar and al-Shafi‘i. By understanding the distinction between essence and description, you will be able to navigate similar cases where a prohibition seems to conflict with the validity of an act.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principle: prohibition does not strip essential legitimacy
The text states that even when an act is prohibited (e.g., fasting on Eid), the act itself remains legitimate in its essence. This is a core Hanafi rule.
The wine ownership analogy is introduced to illustrate this: ownership persists despite prohibition of consumption.
2
Trace the proof: the wine ownership example
The author uses a qiyas (analogy): just as ownership of wine is established even though disposal is prohibited, the act of fasting is legitimate even when prohibited at certain times.
This shows that prohibition does not affect the essential legal capacity (ahl al-wujub).
3
Analyze the opposing view of Zufar and al-Shafi‘i
They argue that the prohibition makes the act a sin, so the vow is invalid, citing the hadith: 'La nadhra fi ma‘siyati Allah' (No vow in disobedience to Allah).
Key distinction: they consider any prohibited act as inherently sinful.
4
Understand the Hanafi response
The Hanafi reply distinguishes essence (asl) from description (wasf). The act is legitimate in essence; the prohibition attaches to the time (description). Hence, the vow is valid because it is based on the essence.
The hadith applies to acts that are inherently (dhātan) sinful, not those prohibited due to an external description.
5
Apply the rule to new cases
The same principle applies to praying during disliked times or any act of worship prohibited due to a contingent factor. The vow remains valid.
One should fulfill the vow but it is recommended to avoid the prohibited time by making up later.

فصل في النهي
وقد أمكن الجمع بينهما بأن يثبت الملك ويحرم التصرف، أليس أنه لو تخمر العصيرُ في ثبوت الملك وحرمت التصرف
ملك المسلم يبقى ملكه فيها ويحرم التصرف.
وعلى هذا قال أصحابنا له : إذا نذر بصوم يوم النحر ، وأيام التشريق يصح نذره؛ لأنه نذر بصوم مشروع وكذلك لو نذر بالصلاة في الأوقات المكروهة يصح؛ لأنه نذر بعبادة مشروعــــة؛
لما ذكرنا أن النهي يوجب بقاء التصرُّف مشروعاً، ولهذا قلنا: لو شَرَعَ في النقل في هذه الأوقات عن الأفعال الشرعية المكروهة لزمه بالشروع
وقد أمكن إلخ يعني أن النكاح بخلاف البيع حيث يمكن فيه القول ببقاء المشروعية، والعمل بحقيقة النهي؛ لأن البيع إنما شرع لملك العين والتحريم لا يضاده، والتحريم إنما يضاد المحل لا الملك، والحل في البيع يثبت تبعاً، فلا يضاده التحريم كذا في شرح "المنار".
لأنه نذر بصوم إلخ ولو كان الصوم في هذه الأيام غير مشروع لم يصح النذر به كما هو لسائر المعاصي، وقال زفر والشافعي لا يصح النذر به ولا يلزمه شيء؛ لأن النهي معصية، وقال علي: "لا نذر في معصية الله تعالى" قلنا: قد تقرر أن النهي عن الأفعال الشرعية يقتضي أن يكون المنهي عنه مشروعاً بأصله قبيحاً بوصفه، فكان النذر به نذراً بما هو مشروع بأصله، وأما وصف المعصية فإنما يتصل بفعله، ولهذا يفتى أن يفطر في هذه الأيام، ويقتضي في أيام آخر ليحصل الخلوص عن المعصية، ولو صام في هذه الأيام خرج عن العهدة؛ لأنه أداه كما التزم كذا في "المعدن".
Chapter on Prohibition. And it is possible to combine between them by establishing ownership and prohibiting disposal. Is it not that if grape juice becomes wine, ownership is established but disposal is prohibited? The ownership of a Muslim remains in it but disposal is prohibited. And on this our companions said: If one vows to fast on the day of Nahr and the days of Tashriq, his vow is valid because he vowed a legitimate fast. Similarly, if he vows to pray during disliked times, it is valid because he vowed a legitimate act of worship. Because we mentioned that prohibition requires the act to remain legitimate. For this we said: If one begins a supererogatory act during these disliked times, it becomes binding upon him to complete it. And it is possible etc., meaning that marriage is unlike sale where it is possible to say that legitimacy remains and the prohibition is acted upon literally; because sale is legislated for ownership of the essence and prohibition does not contradict it – prohibition only contradicts the subject, not ownership – and permissibility in sale is established as secondary, so prohibition does not contradict it, as in Sharh al-Manar. Because he vowed a fast etc., and if fasting on these days were not legitimate, the vow would not be valid, as with other sins. Zufar and al-Shafi'i said: The vow is not valid and nothing is binding on him, because prohibition is a sin, and 'Ali said: "No vow in disobedience to Allah." We said: It has been established that prohibition of legitimate acts requires that the prohibited act be legitimate in its essence but evil in its description, so the vow is a vow of what is legitimate in its essence. As for the description of sin, it pertains to the act itself. For this, the jurist rules that he should break the fast on these days and make it up on other days to achieve freedom from sin, and if he fasts on these days, he exits from liability because he performed it as he committed, as in al-Ma'din.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage opens with a discussion of the effect of prohibition (nahy) on acts. The author establishes a foundational principle: when an act is prohibited, the prohibition does not necessarily negate the essential legality (asl) of the act; rather, it may render the act prohibited due to an external description (wasf). The example of wine is given: ownership of wine is valid (one can own it) but consumption is prohibited. This shows that prohibition does not eliminate the legal capacity for ownership. The same principle applies to acts of worship. The Hanafi school (أصحابنا) holds that if one vows to fast on the day of Nahr (10th of Dhul Hijjah) or the days of Tashriq (11th, 12th, 13th), the vow is valid. This is because fasting on these days is originally legitimate (the act of fasting is an act of worship) but is prohibited due to a description (the time). Thus, the vow is valid since the act itself is not inherently evil. The text contrasts this with the views of Zufar and al-Shafi'i, who consider such a vow invalid because the prohibition makes the act a sin. The Hanafi response explains that the act is legitimate in its essence (asl) but prohibited due to a description (wasf), so the vow is valid. The act of fasting on a prohibited day still counts as fulfilling the vow, though one is advised to break it and make it up later to avoid sin. This ruling is based on the principle that prohibition of acts that are inherently legitimate (af'al shari'yyah) does not strip them of their essential validity.

Key Terms
النهي (al-nahy): A prohibition that indicates the act is forbidden but does not negate its essential legitimacy if the act is inherently valid.
الأصل والوصف (al-asl wa al-wasf): The essence (asl) of an act is its legal validity; the description (wasf) is the contingent attribute that may cause prohibition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view, what is the ruling on a vow to fast on the day of Eid al-Adha?
A) The vow is invalid and nothing is required.
B) The vow is valid, but one must fast on that day and cannot make it up.
C) The vow is valid, and one should fast that day, though it is recommended to break and make up later.
D) The vow is valid only if made before sunrise.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, a vow to perform an act prohibited due to description is valid because the act remains legitimate in essence.
