Prohibition Does Not Invalidate Legal Effects
Stating and proving the principle that prohibition does not negate rulings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul rule that a prohibition due to an external factor (not intrinsic) does not invalidate the legal effects of an act, and distinguish between nahy for essence vs. nahy for externals using examples from the text.

Core Idea
Prohibition does not annul legal effects when the ban is due to an external cause, not the act's essence.

This passage from Al-Shashi's Usul establishes a critical principle in Hanafi legal theory: a prohibited act can still produce valid legal consequences. Many students assume that if something is haram, it is legally void. However, the Hanafi school distinguishes between prohibitions based on the act's intrinsic evil (which nullifies) and those based on external factors (which do not). Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly deriving rulings from texts and resolving apparent contradictions between prohibition and legal effect.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Core Principle
The passage states that 'the prohibition of the act does not contradict the attachment of rulings.' Recognize that this is the central claim being defended. Note the examples provided: divorce during menstruation, ablution with usurped water, prayer on usurped land, etc.
The principle is stated explicitly as 'حرمة الفعل لا تنافي ترتب الأحكام'.
2
Understand the Distinction: Intrinsic vs. External Prohibition
The author explains that some prohibitions are due to a meaning in the act itself (like fasting on Eid), while others are due to an external factor (like harm in intercourse during menstruation). The latter type does not negate legal effects because the act is not intrinsically evil. Internalize the concept of 'nahy li ghayrihi' (prohibition due to something else).
The key phrase: 'غير منهي لمعنى في عينه بل لمعنى في غيره'.
3
Apply the Principle to Key Examples
Work through the examples from the passage: intercourse with a menstruating woman (establishes ihsan, dowry, etc.), divorce during menstruation (valid divorce), ablution with usurped water (valid purification). For each, note the prohibited element and the legal effect that still attaches.
Focus on the analogy: the prohibition is due to a separate factor (usurpation, harm), not the act's essence.
4
Analyze the Inter-Madhhab Dispute
The passage mentions al-Shafi'i's objection: a legal ruling is a blessing, so it cannot be attained via a sin. The Hanafi response is that the prohibition is not intrinsic, and the blessing comes from Allah, not the act's permissibility. Compare the two positions and note the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf).
The dispute is not about the ruling itself but about the causal link between prohibition and validity.

البحث الأول في كتاب الله
فصل في النهي
وارتكاب الحرام ليس بلازم للزوم الإتمام، فإنه لو صبر حتى حلت الصلاة بارتفاع حد الشارع في النفل جازت بلا كراهة الشمس وغروبها ودلوكها أمكنه الإتمام بدون الكراهة وبه فارق صوم يوم العيد، فإنه لو شرع فيه لا يلزمه عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد بحملها لأن الإتمام لا ينفك عن ارتكاب الحرام.
ومن هذا النوع: وَطَهُ الحائض، فإن النهى عن قربانها باعتبار الأذى؛ لقوله تعالى: وَيَسْأَلُونَكَ عَنِ الْمَحِيضِ قُلْ هُوَ أَذًى فَاعْتَزِلُوا النِّسَاءَ فِي الْمَحِيضِ وَلَا تَقْرَبُوهُنَّ حَتَّى يَطْهُرْنَ . ولهذا قلنا: يترتب الأحكام على هذا الوطء، فيثبت به إحصان الواطئ وتحل المرأة الصلاة، المطلقة ثلاثاً وارتكاب الحرام إلخ جواب سوال مقدر، وهو: أن يقال: كيف يصح القول بلزوم الإتمام بالشروع، وفيه التزام: ارتكاب الحرام كذا في "الفصول". وبه فارق إلخ أي بما ذكرنا أنه يمكن الإتمام في الصلاة بدون الكراهة فارق الشروع في الصلاة في هذه الأوقات من الشروع في صوم يوم النحر، فإذا شرع في الصوم في يوم النحر لا يلزمه الإتمام، وإذا أفسده لا يلزمه القضاء في ظاهر الرواية، وعن أبي يوسف أنه يلزمه القضاء والإتمام في الصوم كما في وجه الفرق أن الصوم مقدر باليوم فلا يمكنه الإتمام بدون الإعراض عن الأكل والشرب في ذلك اليوم، وهو مكروه، فلا يمكنه الإتمام بدون الكراهة بخلاف الصلاة؛ فإنها لا توجد بالوقت؛ لأنه ظرفها، والظرف لا تأثير له في إيجاد المظروف بل الصلاة توجد بأفعال معلومة، والوقت مجاور لها، فلا يمكن فساد الوقت مؤثراً في فسادها فتدبر كذا في "المعدن". هذا النوع إلخ: هذا ومن جواب إشكال يرد عليه أن النهي عن الأفعال الحسية يقتضي قبحاً بعينه فلا يكون مشروعاً أصلاً أي لا بأصله ولا بوصفه، وهو أن الوطء فعل حسي مع أنه يبقى مشروعاً بعد النهي، ولهذا تثبت الأحكام الشرعية به، فأجاب بأن الوطء حالة الحيض وإن كان فعلاً حسياً لكنه لمعنى الأذى لا لمعنى في عينه حتى لو ذهب الأذى جاز الوطء فلا يكون حراماً لعينه، فكان هذا بمنزلة الاستثناء من حكم النوع الأول كذا في "الفصول". فَاعْتَزِلُوا النِّسَاءَ: أي فاجتنبوهن ﴿وَلَا تَقْرَبُوهُنَّ في المحيض أي الحيض، والمحيض للحيض كالمخيط للخيط. يترتب الأحكام: وإنما يترتب الأحكام المشروعة على هذا الوطء؛ لأنه غير منهي لمعنى في عينه بل لمعنى في غيره. إحصان الواطئ: أي للرحم، وإحصان الرجم أن يكون حراً عاقلاً بالغاً مسلماً قد دخل بامرأة دخولاً حلالاً، - منهي .

البحث الأول في كتاب الله
فصل في النهي
للزوج الأول، ويثبت به حكمُ المهر والعدة ،والنفقة ولو امتنعت عن التمكين لأجل بوطء الحائض الصداق كانت ناشزة عندهما، فلا تستحق النفقة، وحُرمة الفعل لا تنافي ترتب الأحكام كطلاق الحائض والوضوء بالمياه المغصوبة والإصطياد بقوس مغصوبة والذبح بسكين مغصوبة، والصلاة في الأرض المغصوبة، والبيع في وقت النداء، فإنه يترتب الحكم على هذه التصرفات مع اشتمالها على الحرمة.
أحكام الوجود والصحة التصرفات و وباعتبار هذا الأصل قلنا في قوله تعالى: ﴿وَلَا تَقْبَلُوا لَهُمْ شَهَادَةً أَبَداً: إن الفاسق من =
فإذا وطئ الحائض كان محصناً كما إذا وطئ غير الحائض حتى لو زنى بعد ذلك كان حده الرجم دون الجلد كذا في "المعدن". وتحل إلخ: يعني إذا كانت المرأة مطلقة بثلاث تطليقات فتزوجها زوج آخر دخل بها في حالة الحيض وطلقها تحل للزوج الأول.
حكم المهر إلخ: فإنه يجب المهر على الزوج بهذا الوطء كاملاً، وكذا تحب العدة بهذا الوطء لو طلقها، وكذا تحب النفقة بهذه العدة. كانت ناشزة عندهما وعند أبي حنيفة رحله تستحق النفقة كما لو امتنعت بعد ما وطئت في غير حالة الحيض عن التمكن بالوطء تترتب الأحكام المشروعية على هذا الوطء؛ لأنه غير منهي لمعنى في عينه. وحُرمة الفعل إلخ جواب سوال مقدر، وهو: أن يقال: إن الوطء في هذه الصورة حرام فلا يصلح أن يكون سبباً للأحكام المشروعة؛ إذ الحكم المشروع نعمة وكرامة، فلا ينال بما هو حرام ومعصية كما قال الشافعي بحله، فأجاب بأن حرمة الفعل لا تنافي ثبوت الأحكام شرعاً كذا في "المعدن". كطلاق الحائض: فإنه وإن كان حراماً لكنه يترتب عليه الأحكام الشرعية من الحرمة والفرقة ووجوب العدة وغيرها من الأحكام كسائر الطلاقات شرعاً. والوضوء إلخ: فإن التوضيء بها كان حراماً لكنه يترتب عليها الأحكام الشرعية من إباحة الصلاة، ومس المصحف وغيرهما. والإصطياد بقوس إلخ: فإن الاصطياد بهذ القوس وإن كان حراماً لكنه يترتب عليه الأحكام الشرعية من حل الصيد وإفادة الملك وغيرهما كذا في "المعدن" والذبح إلخ فإن الذبح بهذا السكين وإن كان حراماً لكنه يترتب عليه حل المذبوح وغيره. والصلاة إلخ: فإن الصلاة في الأرض المغصوبة وإن كان حراماً لكنه يترتب عليها جواز الصلاة لكنها تكره لقبح جاوره. والبيع إلخ فإن البيع في هذا الوقت وإن كان منهياً لكنه يفيد الملك وغيره من أحكام البيع، ثم الحرمة في الأمور المذكور لاشتمالها على شغل ملك الغير، واستعماله بدون الإذن فافهم وباعتبار إلخ: أي أن حرمة الفعل لا تنافي ثبوت الأحكام أو باعتبار أن النهي عن التصرفات الشرعية يقتضي بقاء المشروعية كذا في "المعدن".
The first research on the Book of Allah. Chapter on Prohibition. Committing the forbidden is not necessary for the obligation of completion, because if he waits until prayer becomes permissible by the sun reaching its zenith and its setting and its decline, he can complete it without dislike. And with this it differs from the fast of the day of Eid, for if he begins it, it is not binding upon him according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, because completion cannot be separated from committing the forbidden. And from this type: intercourse with a menstruating woman, for the prohibition of approaching her is due to harm; because of His saying: 'And they ask you about menstruation. Say: It is harm, so keep away from women during menstruation and do not approach them until they become pure.' And for this reason we say: rulings attach to this intercourse, so it establishes the man's ihsan and makes the woman permissible for the prayer, the one divorced three times. And 'committing the forbidden' etc. is the answer to a presumed question: How can one validly say that completion becomes obligatory upon beginning, and in it there is the commitment of committing a forbidden act? So it is in 'al-Fusul'. And 'and with this it differs' etc. meaning by what we mentioned that it is possible to complete the prayer without dislike, so the beginning of prayer in these times differs from beginning the fast on the day of Nahr. For if he begins fasting on the day of Nahr, completion is not binding upon him, and if he invalidates it, making it up is not obligatory in the apparent narration. And from Abu Yusuf it is reported that he must make it up and complete the fast, as in the aspect of the difference: that fasting is determined by the day, so he cannot complete it without refraining from eating and drinking on that day, which is disliked, so he cannot complete it without dislike. Unlike prayer, for it does not exist by time, because time is its container, and the container has no effect in bringing about the contained. Rather, prayer exists by known actions, and time is adjacent to it, so corruption of time cannot be effective in its corruption, so contemplate. So it is in 'al-Ma'din'. 'This type' etc.: this is from the answer to an objection directed against it: That the prohibition of sensory acts implies a particular ugliness, so it is not legislated at all, i.e., neither in its essence nor in its attribute. And it is that intercourse is a sensory act, yet it remains legislated after the prohibition, and for this reason legal rulings are established by it. He answered that intercourse during menstruation, although a sensory act, is due to the meaning of harm, not due to a meaning in its essence, so that if the harm goes away, intercourse becomes permissible. So it is not forbidden for its own essence, so this is like an exception from the ruling of the first type. So it is in 'al-Fusul'. 'So keep away from women': i.e., avoid them. 'And do not approach them in menstruation', i.e., menstruation, and 'al-mahid' for menstruation is like 'al-makhit' for a thread. 'Rulings attach': and legal rulings attach to this intercourse only because it is prohibited not for a meaning in its essence, but for a meaning in something else. 'The man's ihsan': i.e., for the womb, and ihsan for stoning is that the man be free, sane, adult, Muslim, and has engaged in lawful intercourse. For the first husband. And by it is established the ruling of dowry, waiting period, and maintenance. And if she refrains from submission for the sake of the dowry, she is considered nashiz according to them, and she does not deserve maintenance. And the prohibition of the act does not contradict the attachment of rulings, like divorce during menstruation, ablution with usurped water, hunting with a usurped bow, slaughtering with a usurped knife, prayer in usurped land, and selling at the time of the call. For rulings attach to these acts despite their containing prohibition. And by virtue of this principle we said in His statement: 'And never accept their testimony', that the sinner is not. When he has intercourse with a menstruating woman, he becomes muhsan just as if he had intercourse with a non-menstruating woman, so that if he commits adultery after that, his punishment is stoning, not flogging. So it is in 'al-Ma'din'. 'And it becomes permissible' etc.: meaning if a woman is divorced by three divorces, and another husband marries her and enters her during menstruation and then divorces her, she becomes permissible for the first husband. 'Ruling of dowry' etc.: the dowry is obligatory upon the husband from this intercourse in full, and likewise the waiting period becomes obligatory by this intercourse if he divorces her, and likewise maintenance is obligatory during this waiting period. She is nashiz according to them, and according to Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him, she deserves maintenance, just as if she refrained after being touched in a non-menstrual state. Legal rulings attach to this intercourse because it is not prohibited for a meaning in its essence. And 'the prohibition of the act' etc. is an answer to a presumed question: It might be said that the intercourse in this situation is forbidden, so it cannot be a cause for legal rulings, for a legal ruling is a blessing and honor, so it cannot be attained by what is forbidden and sinful, as al-Shafi'i said regarding its permissibility. He answered that the prohibition of the act does not contradict the establishment of rulings legally. So it is in 'al-Ma'din'. Like divorce during menstruation: although it is forbidden, legal rulings attach to it, such as prohibition, separation, obligation of waiting period, and other rulings like all other divorces legally. And ablution with it: ablution with it was forbidden, but legal rulings attach to it, such as permissibility of prayer, touching the mushaf, etc. And hunting with a bow: hunting with this bow, although forbidden, legal rulings attach to it, such as the permissibility of the prey and the acquisition of ownership, etc. So it is in 'al-Ma'din'. And slaughtering with it: slaughtering with this knife, although forbidden, legal rulings attach to it, such as the permissibility of the slaughtered animal, etc. And prayer on usurped land: prayer on usurped land, although forbidden, legal rulings attach to it, such as the validity of prayer, but it is disliked due to the ugliness of adjacency. And selling at the time of the call: selling at this time, although prohibited, it establishes ownership and other rulings of sale. Then the prohibition in the aforementioned matters is due to their involving the occupation of another's property and using it without permission, so understand. And 'by virtue of' etc.: meaning that the prohibition of the act does not contradict the establishment of rulings, or considering that the prohibition of legal acts necessitates the continuation of legality. So it is in 'al-Ma'din'.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage from al-Shashi's Usul establishes a key principle: that a prohibition (nahy) does not necessarily invalidate the legal effects of an act. The author first addresses a question about prayer during prohibited times. He argues that if one begins prayer at a disliked time (like sunrise), he is obliged to complete it, but he can wait until the time becomes permissible to avoid dislike. This distinguishes prayer from fasting on Eid, where completion is not obligatory because the fast would inherently involve the forbidden act of fasting on that day. Then the author moves to the central example: intercourse with a menstruating woman. The prohibition here is due to harm (adha), not an intrinsic defect in the act itself. Therefore, this intercourse still produces legal consequences: it establishes ihsan (making the man liable to stoning if he later commits zina), it makes a woman divorced three times permissible for her first husband (through a valid intervening marriage), and it necessitates the full dowry, waiting period, and maintenance. The author anticipates the objection that a prohibited act cannot be a cause for beneficial rulings, citing al-Shafi'i's view. He refutes this by listing multiple cases where prohibited acts still yield valid legal outcomes: divorce during menstruation (though forbidden, the divorce is effective), ablution with usurped water (though sinful, the ablution is valid for prayer), hunting with a usurped bow (the prey becomes permissible), slaughtering with a usurped knife (the meat is halal), prayer on usurped land (the prayer is valid but disliked), and selling during the Friday call (the sale is valid). The underlying rationale is that the prohibition in these cases is due to an external factor (misappropriation of property) or due to a temporary harm, not because the act is intrinsically evil. Thus, the legal efficacy remains intact. The author concludes by stating that this principle is based on the idea that a prohibition of a shar'i act (like sale, prayer) does not negate its legality; rather, it may even imply the continuation of legality.

Key Terms
النهي (al-nahy): Prohibition, a demand to abstain from an act.
حرمة الفعل (hurmat al-fi'l): The prohibition of the act, i.e., it being forbidden (haram).
ترتب الأحكام (tarattub al-ahkam): The attachment of legal rulings (like validity, obligation, etc.) to an act.
إحصان (ihsan): Legal status of being muhsan (protected), which makes an adulterer liable to stoning.
أذى (adha): Harm or discomfort, used in the Quran to justify prohibition of intercourse during menstruation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi usul principle discussed, which of the following acts, though prohibited, still produces legal effects?
A) Fasting on the day of Eid without completing it
B) Intercourse with a menstruating woman for establishing ihsan
C) Prayer at the exact moment of sunrise without waiting
D) All of the above

Key Takeaway
A prohibited act retains its legal effects if the prohibition is due to an external factor; the Hanafi school distinguishes between intrinsic and extrinsic prohibitions.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
8 min
Preferring Literal Over Metaphorical Meaning
First principle: literal over metaphorical

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that literal (haqiqa) meaning is preferred over metaphorical (majaz) when interpreting legal texts, and identify the thamarah al-khilaf with the Shafi'i view on the daughter of zina case.

Core Idea
Literal meaning is always preferred unless a clear context shifts to metaphorical.

This passage from Usul al-Shashi introduces a fundamental principle for resolving ambiguity in legal texts: the literal meaning (haqiqa) takes priority over the metaphorical (majaz). The principle is illustrated by a famous dispute between Hanafis and Shafi'is over whether a daughter born from zina is included in the Quranic prohibition 'your daughters.' Understanding this principle sharpens your ability to derive rulings from texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed term
The word 'bint' (daughter) appears in the Quranic verse 'Forbidden to you are your mothers and your daughters' (al-Nisa' 4:23). This word has both a linguistic literal meaning (biological offspring) and a religious metaphorical meaning (legitimate offspring by marriage or lineage).
The Hanafis treat the religious meaning as a metaphor relative to the linguistic literal meaning.
2
Apply the principle of preferring literal meaning
According to the first rule stated in the passage, when a word has both literal and metaphorical meanings, the literal is more appropriate (firsta) in the absence of a clear contextual indicator (qarina) that shifts to the metaphorical. Here, no such indicator exists.
The Hanafi school prioritizes the literal meaning as the default unless there is evidence to the contrary.
3
Test the application: Is the daughter from zina a literal 'bint'?
A child born from zina is biologically the offspring of the adulterer. Linguistically, any biological daughter is a 'bint.' Thus, the literal meaning covers her.
Custom (urf) and common parlance do not change the literal linguistic meaning.
4
Derive the ruling
Since the literal meaning applies, the Quranic prohibition includes the daughter from zina. Therefore, the adulterer is forbidden to marry her, and any marriage is invalid (batil).
This leads to practical consequences: no dower, no inheritance, etc.
5
Contrast with the Shafi'i position
Imam al-Shafi'i considers the religious meaning (haqiqa shar'iyya) as the true intended meaning, arguing that in religious discourse, 'bint' implies legitimate lineage. Thus, the daughter from zina is not a 'bint' in the relevant sense, so marriage is valid.
The thamarah al-khilaf: Hanafi prohibits marriage; Shafi'i permits it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في تعريف طريق المراد بالنصوص: اعلم أن لمعرفة المراد بالنصوص طرقاً: — ١ - منها: أن اللفظ إذا كان حقيقةً لمعنى ومجازاً لآخر، فالحقيقة أولى. مثاله : ما قال علماؤنا الله : البنت المخلوقة من ماء الزنا يحرم على الزاني نكاحها، وقال الشافعي له: يحل. ما قلنا؛ لأنها بنته حقيقةً، فتدخل تحت قوله تعالى: ﴿وَحُرِّمَتْ عَلَيْكُمْ أُمَّهَاتُكُمْ وَبَنَاتُكُمْ﴾. و الصحيح: أُمَّهَاتُكُمْ وَبَنَاتُكُمْ . ويتفرَّع منه الأحكام على المذهبين من حل الوطء، ووجوب المهر، ولزوم النفقة، وجَرَيانِ التوارث، وولاية المنع عن الخروج والبرون داره عنده لا عندنا. ٢ - ومنها: أن أحد المحملين إذا وجَبَ تخصيصاً في النص دون الآخر فالحملُ على ما لا يستلزم التخصيص أولى. مثاله: في قوله تعالى: أَوْ لا مَستُمُ النِّسَاء ، فالملامسة لو حملت على الوقاع كان النص معمولاً به في جميع صُور وجوده، ولو حملت على المس باليد كان النص مخصوصاً به في كثير من الصور، فإن مس المحارم، والطفلة الصغيرة جداً غير ناقض للوضوء في أصح قولي الشافعي حاله. وقال الشافعي الله إلخ لأنها لا تدخل تحت قوله تعالى: ﴿وَبَنَاتُكُمْ﴾، وذلك؛ لأن المراد بالبنات: المنسوبات إلى الشخص في تخاطب الناس، ولا ريبة أن المخلوقة من ماء الزاني لا تنسب إليه عرفاً وعادةً. ولنا: أن اللفظ إذا كان حقيقة لمعنى ومجازاً لآخر فالحقيقة أولى على ما سبق. بنته حقيقةً فهذا اللفظ يحتمل أن يراد به الحقيقة الشرعية أو اللغوية، فحمل الشافعي حلاله على الشرعية، ونحن حملنا على اللغوية؛ لأن الشرعية كالمجاز بالنسبة إلى اللغوية. من حل الوطء وهذا ظاهر؛ لأنه لما صح للزاني نكاح بنته هذه ترتب أحكام النكاح عنده، وهي مذكورة في الكتاب ولم يترتب عندنا؛ لعدم صحة النكاح. على الوقاع: اعلم أن الملامسة يحتمل المعنيين: الوقاع والمس، فالشافعي له أراد المس باليد، ونحن رجحنا معنى الوقاع؛ لأنه إذا أريد به الوقاع كان النص معمولاً به في جميع الصور؛ لأن في كل صورة وجد الوقاع كان ناقضاً للطهارتين: الصغرى والكبرى موجباً للتيمم عند فقد الماء، ولو حمل المس باليد يلزم تخصيص النص في الصور المذكورة في المتن.
A chapter on defining the way to the intended meaning from texts: Know that there are ways to know the intended meaning from texts: — 1. One of them: That a word, if it is literal for a meaning and metaphorical for another, then the literal is more appropriate. Its example: What our scholars said: The daughter created from the water of zina is forbidden for the adulterer to marry. And al-Shafi'i says: It is permissible for him. That which we said: because she is his real daughter, so she falls under His statement: 'Forbidden to you are your mothers and your daughters.' The correct is: 'your mothers and your daughters.' And from it branch out rulings according to the two schools: such as permissibility of intercourse, obligation of dower, obligation of maintenance, inheritance, and guardianship of preventing her from leaving and going out of his house; according to him, not according to us. 2. Another: That if one of the two possible interpretations necessitates specification (takhsis) of the text while the other does not, then carrying the text on the one that does not necessitate specification is more appropriate. Its example: In His statement: 'Or you have touched women.' So if 'touching' is interpreted as intercourse, the text is applied in all of its occurrence forms; and if it is interpreted as touching with the hand, the text becomes specified in many forms; because touching mahrams, and a very young girl who is not desired and not adolescent is not nullifying of wudu in the more correct of al-Shafi'i's two opinions. And al-Shafi'i said: because she does not fall under His statement: 'your daughters'; that is because the intended meaning of 'daughters' is those attributed to the person in the speech of people, and there is no doubt that the one created from the water of the adulterer is not attributed to him customarily and habitually. For us: that the word, if it is literal for a meaning and metaphorical for another, then the literal is more appropriate as previously mentioned. 'His real daughter' so this word can be interpreted as either the religious reality or the linguistic reality; al-Shafi'i carried it on the religious reality, and we carried it on the linguistic reality; because the religious reality is like metaphor relative to the linguistic reality. 'From the permissibility of intercourse' and this is apparent; because when the adulterer's marriage to this daughter is valid according to him, the rulings of marriage follow, which are mentioned in the book; and they do not follow according to us, due to the invalidity of the marriage. 'On intercourse': Know that 'touching' can have two meanings: intercourse and touching (with hand). Al-Shafi'i intended hand-touching, and we gave preponderance to the meaning of intercourse; because if intercourse is intended, the text is applied in all instances; because in every instance where intercourse occurs, it nullifies both minor and major purity and necessitates tayammum when water is absent. If it is carried on hand-touching, it necessitates specification of the text in the instances mentioned in the text.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage is a direct statement of two important usul al-fiqh principles (qawa'id) for determining the intended meaning (murad) from legal texts (nusus). The first principle: When a word (lafz) has both a literal (haqiqa) and a metaphorical (majaz) meaning, the literal meaning takes precedence because it is more direct and certain. The example given is the case of a daughter born from adultery (zina). The Hanafis (our scholars) rule that the adulterer cannot marry this daughter because she is his real daughter linguistically (haqiqa lughawiyya) and thus falls under the Quranic prohibition in Surah al-Nisa', verse 23: 'Forbidden to you are your mothers and your daughters.' They argue that the word 'bint' (daughter) in the verse is literal for a biological relationship, even if born out of wedlock. Imam al-Shafi'i holds that such a daughter is not a real daughter in the religious sense (haqiqa shar'iyya) because the child of zina is not attributed to the father; thus she is not included in the verse, making it permissible for the adulterer to marry her. This leads to different legal outcomes (thamar al-khilaf): according to the Hanafis, the marriage is invalid, so no intercourse, no dower, etc.; according to al-Shafi'i, it is valid, so all marital rulings apply. The second principle: When a word can be interpreted in two ways, and one interpretation necessitates specification (takhsis) of the text while the other does not, the interpretation that does not require specification is preferred because it keeps the text generally applicable. The example is the verse 'Aw lamastum al-nisa' (or you have touched women) from Surah al-Ma'idah verse 6. The Hanafis interpret 'lams' as sexual intercourse (wata'), so the verse applies universally: any intercourse nullifies wudu and necessitates ghusl and tayammum. Imam al-Shafi'i interprets it as touching with the hand, which requires specifying that touching mahrams or a young child does not nullify wudu, thus specifying the text. The Hanafis argue that interpretation that avoids specification is stronger because it maintains the general applicability of the text. The passage thus illustrates how these usul principles are applied to generate actual fiqh rulings and highlights the difference between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools.

Key Terms
الحقيقة (al-haqiqa): The literal or real meaning of a word, as opposed to metaphorical
المجاز (al-majaz): Metaphorical meaning of a word, used due to a connection with the literal meaning
تخصيص (takhsis): Specifying or limiting the application of a general text to exclude certain instances

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is a daughter born from zina prohibited for her biological father to marry?
A) Because she is considered a 'bint' in the religious (shar'i) sense.
B) Because the Quranic verse 'wa banatukum' includes her under the linguistic literal meaning of 'daughter'.
C) Because the Prophet explicitly prohibited such marriages.
D) Because of a consensus among all scholars.

Key Takeaway
When a word has both literal and metaphorical meanings, the Hanafi school prefers the literal meaning unless a clear contextual indicator points otherwise.

Avoiding Specification (Takhsis) in Interpretation
Second principle: avoid takhsis

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that an interpretation that avoids specifying (takhsis) a general text is preferred over one that requires specification, using the example of 'lams' in wudu.

Core Idea
Interpretations that keep a text general are stronger than those that force exceptions.

The second principle in this passage deals with situations where a word has two possible interpretations, one of which would require limiting the general application of a text (takhsis), while the other keeps it fully applicable. The Hanafi rule prefers the interpretation that avoids takhsis, because the general force of the text should be maintained. The classic example is the Quranic phrase 'or you have touched women' (al-Ma'idah 5:6) — interpreting it as intercourse keeps the ruling universal, while interpreting it as hand-touching forces exceptions for mahrams and children.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ambiguous term
The word 'lams' (touch) in the Quranic verse 'or you have touched women' can mean either intercourse (wata') or touching with the hand (mass). Both are linguistically possible.
This is a case of ishtirak (polysemy) or a word with multiple potential meanings.
2
Determine the consequence of each interpretation
If 'lams' is interpreted as intercourse, the ruling (nullification of wudu) applies to every instance of intercourse — the text is applied universally. If interpreted as hand-touching, the text becomes specified: it does not apply to touching mahrams or very young children, who do not nullify wudu in the Shafi'i view.
Takhsis reduces the scope of the general command.
3
Apply the principle: prefer interpretation without takhsis
The second rule states: when one possible meaning necessitates takhsis and the other does not, the one without takhsis is preferred. Here, the intercourse interpretation avoids takhsis, so it is stronger.
This principle preserves the generality of the text.
4
Derive the ruling
Thus, 'lams' in the verse is interpreted as intercourse. Therefore, any sexual intercourse (with a woman) nullifies wudu and requires ghusl if seminal emission, and also nullifies tayammum if performed.
5
Contrast with the Shafi'i position
Imam al-Shafi'i interprets 'lams' as hand-touching, leading to takhsis: touching without desire, or touching a mahram, does not nullify wudu. The Hanafis reject this because it unnecessarily specifies the general text.
The practical difference: For Hanafis, any skin-to-skin contact between spouses does not break wudu unless it is intercourse. For Shafi'is, deliberate skin-to-skin touch (with desire) breaks wudu, but not for mahrams.

فصل في تعريف طريق المراد بالنصوص: اعلم أن لمعرفة المراد بالنصوص طرقاً: — ١ - منها: أن اللفظ إذا كان حقيقةً لمعنى ومجازاً لآخر، فالحقيقة أولى. مثاله : ما قال علماؤنا الله : البنت المخلوقة من ماء الزنا يحرم على الزاني نكاحها، وقال الشافعي له: يحل. ما قلنا؛ لأنها بنته حقيقةً، فتدخل تحت قوله تعالى: ﴿وَحُرِّمَتْ عَلَيْكُمْ أُمَّهَاتُكُمْ وَبَنَاتُكُمْ﴾. و الصحيح: أُمَّهَاتُكُمْ وَبَنَاتُكُمْ . ويتفرَّع منه الأحكام على المذهبين من حل الوطء، ووجوب المهر، ولزوم النفقة، وجَرَيانِ التوارث، وولاية المنع عن الخروج والبرون داره عنده لا عندنا. ٢ - ومنها: أن أحد المحملين إذا وجَبَ تخصيصاً في النص دون الآخر فالحملُ على ما لا يستلزم التخصيص أولى. مثاله: في قوله تعالى: أَوْ لا مَستُمُ النِّسَاء ، فالملامسة لو حملت على الوقاع كان النص معمولاً به في جميع صُور وجوده، ولو حملت على المس باليد كان النص مخصوصاً به في كثير من الصور، فإن مس المحارم، والطفلة الصغيرة جداً غير ناقض للوضوء في أصح قولي الشافعي حاله. وقال الشافعي الله إلخ لأنها لا تدخل تحت قوله تعالى: ﴿وَبَنَاتُكُمْ﴾، وذلك؛ لأن المراد بالبنات: المنسوبات إلى الشخص في تخاطب الناس، ولا ريبة أن المخلوقة من ماء الزاني لا تنسب إليه عرفاً وعادةً. ولنا: أن اللفظ إذا كان حقيقة لمعنى ومجازاً لآخر فالحقيقة أولى على ما سبق. بنته حقيقةً فهذا اللفظ يحتمل أن يراد به الحقيقة الشرعية أو اللغوية، فحمل الشافعي حلاله على الشرعية، ونحن حملنا على اللغوية؛ لأن الشرعية كالمجاز بالنسبة إلى اللغوية. من حل الوطء وهذا ظاهر؛ لأنه لما صح للزاني نكاح بنته هذه ترتب أحكام النكاح عنده، وهي مذكورة في الكتاب ولم يترتب عندنا؛ لعدم صحة النكاح. على الوقاع: اعلم أن الملامسة يحتمل المعنيين: الوقاع والمس، فالشافعي له أراد المس باليد، ونحن رجحنا معنى الوقاع؛ لأنه إذا أريد به الوقاع كان النص معمولاً به في جميع الصور؛ لأن في كل صورة وجد الوقاع كان ناقضاً للطهارتين: الصغرى والكبرى موجباً للتيمم عند فقد الماء، ولو حمل المس باليد يلزم تخصيص النص في الصور المذكورة في المتن.
A chapter on defining the way to the intended meaning from texts: Know that there are ways to know the intended meaning from texts: — 1. One of them: That a word, if it is literal for a meaning and metaphorical for another, then the literal is more appropriate. And from it branch out rulings according to the two schools: such as permissibility of intercourse, obligation of dower, obligation of maintenance, inheritance, and guardianship of preventing her from leaving and going out of his house; according to him, not according to us. 2. Another: That if one of the two possible interpretations necessitates specification (takhsis) of the text while the other does not, then carrying the text on the one that does not necessitate specification is more appropriate. Its example: In His statement: 'Or you have touched women.' So if 'touching' is interpreted as intercourse, the text is applied in all of its occurrence forms; and if it is interpreted as touching with the hand, the text becomes specified in many forms; because touching mahrams, and a very young girl who is not desired and not adolescent is not nullifying of wudu in the more correct of al-Shafi'i's two opinions. Reconciling Multiple Quranic Readings: The Hanafi Method
Explaining the principle of acting on multiple Quranic readings to derive rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of reconciling multiple qira'at by assigning each to a specific legal context and derive the resulting fiqh rulings.

Core Idea
Multiple Quranic readings are each a revelation; Hanafis reconcile them by assigning each to a distinct context.

In this passage from Usul al-Shashi, we encounter a foundational principle of Hanafi legal theory: when a Quranic verse is transmitted in two canonical readings (qira'at), both must be given legal effect rather than discarding one. The author illustrates this with two detailed examples from the Quran—one on wudu and wiping over khuffs, and one on the purity of a menstruating woman. By mastering this principle, you will understand how Hanafi jurists derive nuanced rulings that honor the full spectrum of Quranic revelation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principle
Locate the general rule statement: 'when a text is read with two readings or narrated with two versions, acting upon it in a manner that gives effect to both versions is better than acting upon one of them.' Recognize that this is a rule of reconciliation (jam').
The principle builds on the idea that both readings are divinely revealed and authoritative.
2
Analyze the first example (Q 5:6)
Examine the two readings of the verse on wudu: 'arjulakum' (accusative) and 'arjulikum' (genitive). Note how the genitive reading is linked to the state of wearing khuffs, while the accusative applies when not wearing khuffs.
This yields the Hanafi ruling that wiping over khuffs is permissible and that the permissibility is even rooted in the Quran, though majority holds it is from sunnah.
3
Analyze the second example (Q 2:222)
Look at the two readings of 'yathurna' (takhfif) and 'yattahhirna' (tashdid). Understand that the takhfif reading applies when menstruation lasts exactly ten days (maximum), where mere cessation brings purity, while the tashdid reading applies for less than ten days, where ghusl is required for complete purity.
This distinction leads to practical rulings about intercourse and prayer obligations.
4
Formulate the operational rule
Synthesize: each reading is assigned a specific scenario (halah) so that both are given effect. The rule is not to pick one reading over the other but to find contexts where each applies.
This method differs from tarjih (preference) and is a form of jam' (reconciliation).
5
Apply to a new hypothetical
Suppose a third qira'ah existed for another verse. Using the same method, ask: What are the distinct contexts that could give effect to each reading?
This reinforces the mental habit of seeking reconciliation before tarjih.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في تعريف طريق المراد بالنصوص
ويتفرع منه الأحكامُ على المذهبين من إباحة الصَّلاة، ومس المصحف، ودخول المسجد، وصحة الإمامة، ولزوم التيهم عند عدم الماء، وتذكر المس في أثناء الصلاة. ۳ – ومنها: أن النص إذا قُرئَ بقراءتين أو رُوي بروايتين كان العَمَلُ به على وجه يكون عملاً بالوجهين أولى من العمل بأحدهما. مثاله: في قوله تعالى: ﴿وأرجُلَكُمْ﴾ قُرِئَ بالنصب عطفاً على المغسول، وبالخفض عطفاً على الممسوح، فحملت قراءة الخفض على حالة التخفف، وقراءة النصب على حالة عدم التخفف. وباعتبار هذا المعنى قال البعض: جواز المسح ثبت بالكتاب. وكذلك قوله تعالى: ﴿حَتَّى يَطْهُرْنَ﴾ قُرِئَ بالتشديد والتخفيف، فيعمل بقراءة التخفيف فيما إذا كانت أيامها عشرةً، وبقراءة التشديد فيما إذا كانت أيامها دون العشرة. وعلى هذا قال أصحابنا : اذا انقطع دم الحيض لأقل من عشرة أيام لم يجز وطء الحائض حتى تغتسل؛ لأن كمال الطهارة يثبت بالإغتسال، ولو انقَطَعَ دَمُها لعشرة أيام أدم حيضها جاز وطؤُها قبل الغسل؛ لأن مطلق الطهارة ثبت بانقطاع الدم. ولهذا قلنا: إذا انقَطَعَ دم الحيض لعشرة أيام في آخر وقت الصلاة تلزمها فريضة الوقت وإن لم يبق من الوقت مقدار ما تغتسل فيه، ولو انقَطَعَ دَمُها لأقل من عشرة أيامٍ في آخر وقت الصَّلاة إن بقي من الوقت مقدار ما تغتَسِلُ فيه وتُحْرَمُ للصَّلاة ذلك الوقت.
Section on defining the method of intended meaning from texts. From it branch rulings according to the two schools: permissibility of prayer, touching the Mushaf, entering the mosque, validity of imamah, and obligation of tayammum when water is absent, and remembering touching during prayer. 3 – Among them: that when a text is read with two readings or narrated with two versions, acting upon it in a manner that gives effect to both versions is better than acting upon one of them. An example: in the saying of the Most High: {And your feet} — it was read with nasb as an accusative upon 'the washed', and with khafd as a genitive upon 'the wiped'. So the reading of khafd was interpreted as the case of wearing light footwear, and the reading of nasb as the case of not wearing light footwear. And by virtue of this meaning, some said: the permissibility of wiping is established by the Book. Likewise the saying of the Most High: {until they become pure} — it was read with tashdid and takhfif. So the reading of takhfif is acted upon when her days are ten, and the reading of tashdid is acted upon when her days are less than ten. Upon this our scholars said: when menstrual blood stops for less than ten days, it is not permissible to have intercourse with the menstruating woman until she performs ghusl, because complete purity is established by ghusl. But if her blood stops after ten days of her menstruation, it is permissible to have intercourse with her before ghusl, because absolute purity is established by cessation of blood. For this we said: if menstrual blood stops after ten days at the end of prayer time, the obligatory prayer of that time becomes incumbent upon her even if there remains no time sufficient for ghusl. And if her blood stops for less than ten days at the end of prayer time, then if there remains time sufficient for her to perform ghusl and enter into prayer for that time.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage from Usul al-Shashi expounds a key principle of Hanafi usul: when a Quranic verse is established with multiple qira'at (variant readings), both readings must be given effect to the extent possible, rather than discarding one. The author presents two illustrative examples. First, the verse on wudu (Q 5:6) reading 'arjulakum' (accusative) and 'arjulikum' (genitive). The Hanafis reconcile them by applying the genitive reading to the case of wearing khuffs (light leather socks), permitting wiping over them, and the accusative to the case without khuffs, requiring washing. Hence some scholars maintain that the permission to wipe is from the Quran itself, though the majority hold it is from the sunnah. Second, the verse on menstruation (Q 2:222) reading 'yathurna' (with takhfif, i.e., yathurna) and 'yattahhirna' (with tashdid). The takhfif reading applies when the menstrual flow ends after ten days (the maximum duration), indicating that mere cessation brings purity; while the tashdid reading applies for less than ten days, emphasizing that complete purification requires ghusl due to the possibility of return of blood. From this, Hanafi fiqh derives that a woman whose period ends after ten days may have intercourse without ghusl, and if it ends at the end of prayer time, she must pray even without time for ghusl. If it ends before ten days, she must have enough time for ghusl. The passage also hints at a dispute with the Shafi'is regarding the ruling on touching a woman breaking wudu: the first paragraph mentions 'two schools' about rulings after touching, but the commentary clarifies that Hanafis hold wudu is not nullified by touch whereas Shafi'is do.

Key Terms
قراءتين (qira'atayn): Two variant canonical readings of a Quranic verse, both transmitted mutawatir.
حالة التخفف (halat al-takhaffuf): The state of wearing light footwear (khuffs) that permits wiping instead of washing feet in wudu.
مطلق الطهارة (mutlaq al-tahara): Absolute purity from menstruation, achieved by the mere cessation of blood after the maximum period (ten days).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how do Hanafis apply the two readings of ۞حَتَّى يَطْهُرْنَ۞?
A) They act on the tashdid reading only because it is more emphatic.
B) They act on the takhfif reading for menstruation lasting ten days, and the tashdid reading for less than ten days.
C) They ignore the takhfif reading as abrogated.
D) They apply both readings simultaneously to the same situation.

Key Takeaway
When a Quranic text has multiple valid qira'at, Hanafi jurists do not choose one over the other; instead, they assign each to a distinct legal scenario to honor both as authoritative revelations.

Deriving Rulings from Quranic Readings on Menstruation
Textual analysis of qira'at to derive fiqh rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi method of deriving rulings from multiple Quranic readings (qira'at) by identifying how each reading indicates a different legal scenario.

Core Idea
Two Quranic readings of the same word yield two distinct rulings based on the possibility of blood return.

In this passage, the author shows how the light and heavy readings of the word 'ayyamiha' in the Quranic verse on menstruation lead to different fiqh rulings. Understanding this method is crucial for Hanafi usul, as it demonstrates how seemingly minor textual variations can have significant legal consequences. We will walk through the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two qira'at
Recognize that the word 'ayyamiha' is recited with two different vocalizations: takhfif (light) and tashdid (heavy). The light reading suggests a complete duration of ten days, while the heavy reading indicates a duration less than ten.
These are not contradictions but complementary readings.
2
Link each reading to a legal scenario
Associate the light reading with a ten-day menstruation period and the heavy reading with a period shorter than ten days. The interpretation is based on the possibility of blood return after cessation.
After ten days, return is impossible; before ten, it is possible.
3
Derive the ruling on intercourse
If blood stops at ten days, absolute purity is established, allowing intercourse without ghusl. If it stops before ten, complete purity via ghusl is required. The need for ghusl emphasizes purity when return is possible.
4
Extend to prayer obligation
If blood stops at ten days at the end of prayer time, the prayer becomes obligatory immediately even if time for ghusl is lacking. If it stops before ten, prayer is only obligatory if enough time remains for ghusl and prayer. The obligation returns with final cessation, but is conditional on capability for the doubtful case.
5
Confirm with hadith evidence
The maximum period of ten days is confirmed by the hadith that menstruation does not exceed ten days. This supports the interpretation that the light reading applies to the maximum duration.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في تعريف طريق المراد بالنصوص فيعمل بقراءة التخفيف فيما إذا كانت أيامها عشرةً، وبقراءة التشديد فيما إذا كانت أيامها دون العشرة. وعلى هذا قال أصحابنا رحمهم الله: اذا انقطع دم الحيض لأقل من عشرة أيام لم يجز وطء الحائض حتى تغتسل؛ لأن كمال الطهارة يثبت بالإغتسال، ولو انقَطَعَ دمُها لعشرة أيام أدم حيضها جاز وطؤُها قبل الغسل؛ لأن مطلق الطهارة ثبت بانقطاع الدم. ولهذا قلنا: إذا انقَطَعَ دم الحيض لعشرة أيام في آخر وقت الصلاة تلزمها فريضة الوقت وإن لم يبق من الوقت مقدار ما تغتسل فيه، ولو انقَطَعَ دَمُها لأقل من عشرة أيامٍ في آخر وقت الصَّلاة إن بقي من الوقت مقدار ما تغتَسِلُ فيه وتُحْرَمُ للصَّلاة ذلك الوقت فيُعمل إلخ وإنما حملنا قراءة التخفيف على العشرة، وقراءة التشديد على ما دون العشرة؛ لأن الدم بعد العشرة لا يحتمل العود؛ لأن الحيض لا يزيد على العشرة، فلا يحتاج إلى تأكيد الطهارة بالاغتسال، وفيما دون العشرة احتمال العود قائم فاحتيج إلى تأكيدها بالاغتسال أو ما يقوم مقامه فيترجح الانقطاع على جانب عدم الانقطاع. وعلى هذا: أي لأجل أن حمل قراءة التخفيف على العشرة والتشديد على ما دونها. لأن مطلق إلخ لأن قراءة التشديد تناسب القطع دون العشرة؛ لأنها دالة على كمال التطهر وهو بالغسل، وما يجري مجراه، وعند الأقل يمكن العود، فيتأكد القطع بالغسل، وقراءة التخفيف تلائم القطع على العشرة؛ لأنها مخبرة عن مطلق الطهارة وهو حاصل بمجرد الطهارة؛ إذ خرجت عن الحيض، وعند العشرة لا يمكن العود؛ لأنه لا يزيد عليها؛ إذ هي أقصى مدته على ما ثبت بالحديث فافهم كذا في "الحصول". ولهذا: أي ولأجل أن مطلق الطهارة يثبت بانقطاع الدم. تلزَمُها إلخ لأن لزوم الفريضة إنما يسقط منها للتخفيف للحائض، فإذا زال الحيض يعود اللزوم كما كان، وقد زال الحيض بقراءة على انقطاع الدم بعشرة فتلزمها الفريضة. إن بقي من الوقت: إنما شرط إن توقف الشمس حين عرض عليه الخيل الصافنات الجياد، وفاتته صلاة العصر. لزمتها الفريضة: لأن وقت الغسل ههنا من وقت الحيض؛ لأنها لا تعتبر قبله طاهرة عملاً بالتشديد، ووقت التحرية لإثبات القدرة لها على الفعل كذا قيل. وإلا إلخ أي وإن لم يبق من الوقت الذي انقطع دمها فيه مقدار ما تغتسل فيه وتحرم للصلاة.
Section on defining the way to understand the intended meaning from the texts. So the light reading is applied when her days are ten, and the heavy reading when her days are less than ten. Based on this, our companions (may Allah have mercy on them) said: If the blood of menstruation stops at less than ten days, it is not permissible to have intercourse with the menstruating woman until she performs ghusl, because complete purity is established by ghusl. But if her blood stops at ten days, meaning her menstruation period, it is permissible to have intercourse with her before ghusl, because the absolute purity is established by the cessation of blood. For this reason we said: If the blood of menstruation stops at ten days at the end of the prayer time, the obligatory prayer of that time becomes incumbent upon her even if there is not enough time remaining for her to perform ghusl. But if her blood stops at less than ten days at the end of the prayer time, then if there remains enough time for her to perform ghusl and enter into the prayer, it is incumbent; otherwise not. And we only interpreted the light reading as applying to ten days, and the heavy reading to less than ten, because after ten days the blood is not expected to return, since menstruation does not exceed ten days; so there is no need to emphasize purity by ghusl. In the case of less than ten, the possibility of return exists, so it is necessary to emphasize it by ghusl or something that stands in its place, so that the cessation outweighs the non-cessation. And based on this: that is, because we interpreted the light reading as for ten and the heavy as for less than ten. Because the heavy reading suits cessation at less than ten, as it indicates complete purification, which is by ghusl and what is analogous to it; and when there is a possibility of return, the cessation is emphasized by ghusl. The light reading suits cessation at ten, because it indicates absolute purity, which is achieved by mere purity, since she has left the state of menstruation, and at ten there is no possibility of return because it does not exceed that, as it is the maximum period as established by hadith. Understand. Thus in al-Husul. And for this: that is, because absolute purity is established by the cessation of blood. It becomes incumbent upon her, etc., because the obligation of the prayer was lifted from her as a concession for the menstruating woman; when the menstruation ends, the obligation returns as it was, and the menstruation has ended by the reading indicating cessation of blood at ten days, so the prayer becomes obligatory. If time remains: The condition is that there remains enough time to perform ghusl and enter the prayer, because the one responsible must have the capability to perform the obligation even if it is potential, and that exists here at the end of the prayer time because the time can be extended, as was done for Sulayman (peace be upon him) when the sun was stopped for him while he was reviewing the horses, and he missed the Asr prayer. It becomes obligatory: because the time for ghusl here is from the time of menstruation, since she is not considered pure before that according to the heavy reading, and the time for seeking capability to perform the action is as stated. Otherwise: that is, if there does not remain from the time in which her blood stopped enough time for her to perform ghusl and enter the prayer.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage is a textual analysis showing how two different Quranic readings (qira'at) of the same verse lead to different fiqh rulings. The author explains that the light reading (takhfif) of the word 'ayyamiha' (her days) indicates a complete period of ten days, while the heavy reading (tashdid) indicates a period less than ten. Based on this, Hanafi scholars derive that if a woman's menstruation stops at exactly ten days, she becomes pure and intercourse is allowed even without ghusl, because the cessation is final. If it stops before ten days, intercourse is not allowed until she performs ghusl, as the possibility of return remains. This difference also affects the obligation of prayer: if the blood stops at ten days at the end of prayer time, she must pray even if she cannot perform ghusl in time, because the obligation returns immediately. If it stops before ten days, she only has to pray if she has enough time for ghusl and the prayer. The author uses rational arguments (istiqra') and hadith evidence to support the maximum limit of ten days. The commentary then expounds on these points, quoting from al-Husul and using the example of Prophet Sulayman to illustrate the extension of time.

Key Terms
قراءة التخفيف (qira'at al-takhfif): The Quranic reading with a light pronunciation, indicating a period of ten days for menstruation.
قراءة التشديد (qira'at al-tashdid): The Quranic reading with a heavy pronunciation, indicating a period less than ten days.
مطلق الطهارة (mutlaq al-tahara): Absolute purity that results from the mere cessation of blood, sufficient for allowing intercourse after ten days.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position derived from the two Quranic readings, when is a menstruating woman allowed to have intercourse without performing ghusl?
A) When her blood stops at less than ten days
B) When her blood stops at exactly ten days
C) When she has performed ghusl at any time
D) When she has prayed one prayer after the blood stops

Key Takeaway
Two authentic Quranic readings can yield distinct rulings by applying each to a different scenario, harmonizing the text with reality.

When a Text is Silent: The Case of Vinegar and Impurity Removal
Analyzing a weak derivation from a command

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a weak istinbat by recognizing when a text is silent on a disputed point and evaluate the Hanafi critique of the Shafi'i argument using the hadith of washing blood.

Core Idea
A text that is silent cannot prove a ruling.

In this passage from Usul al-Shashi, the author demonstrates a common error in legal derivation: using a hadith that commands washing with water to argue that only water can remove impurity. The dispute between the Hanafis and Shafi'is over the purity of a spot after removal by vinegar hinges on whether the text's silence permits alternative means. This lesson will guide you through the analysis of this weak istidlal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed ruling
What is the practical difference between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools regarding impurity removal by vinegar? The Hanafis hold that physical removal (zawal hissi) purifies the spot, while al-Shafi'i holds that only water can remove impurity.
The dispute is not about the obligation to wash with water when blood is present, but about the state after removal by another substance.
2
Locate the hadith and its apparent implication
The hadith commands: 'Scrape it, then rub it, then wash it with water.' One might infer that since water is specified, other liquids do not suffice. This is the Shafi'i inference.
The inference is based on mafhum al-mukhalafa (opposite meaning), which is not accepted in all contexts.
3
Analyze the Hanafi objection
The author argues that the hadith is restricted to the situation when blood is present on the spot. It does not address what happens after the blood is removed by another means like vinegar. The text is silent (al-nass sākit ‘anhu), so it cannot be used to prove that vinegar does not work.
This is a key Hanafi usul principle: a text cannot be stretched to cover a point it does not address.
4
Understand the Shafi'i perspective
Al-Shafi'i holds that the command to wash with water implies exclusivity. If another substance could remove impurity, the command would be pointless. Moreover, impurity is a legal concept that requires a specific remover (water).
The Shafi'i argument relies on the idea that explicit mention of a specific means negates other means.
5
Apply the principle to similar cases
The author also mentions the example of fish in water and the ruling of impurity. This reinforces that the text is not general and cannot be used beyond its scope.
This step helps solidify the principle of textual silence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذلك التمسك بقوله عليه السلام: 'حتيه ثم اقرصيه ثم اغسليه بالماء' لإثبات أن الخل لا يُزيل النجس ضعيف؛ لأن الخبر يقتضي وجوب غسل الدم بالماء، فيتقيد بحال وجود الدم على المحلّ ولا خلاف فيه، وإنما الخلاف في طهارة المحل بعد زوال الدم بالخل. ولا خلاف فيه: لكن قيل : الحرمة لا لكرامة آية النجاسة إلا أن فيه قيوداً أيضاً كما حقق. في فساد الماء على أنه يشمل السمك أيضاً، وهو لا ينجس عنده، فحينئذ أن المراد بها ما فيه الحياة بالدم السائل. لا يُزيل النجس إلخ لأن الأمر يقتضي وجوب غسله بالماء فلو جاز غسله بغير الماء، لزم ترك الائتمار وهو غير جائز، ولأن فيه تنصيصاً على أن إزالة النجاسة بالماء لا غير، والتنصيص بالشيء يدل على نفي ما عداه عنده أي عند الشافعي رحمه الله وإنما الخلاف إلخ: ونحن نقول بطهارة؛ لزواله حساً، وعنده لا يطهر والنص ساكت عنه، فلا التمسك به.
And similarly, adhering to his statement (peace be upon him): 'Scrape it, then rub it, then wash it with water' to prove that vinegar does not remove impurity is weak; because the report requires washing the blood with water, so it is restricted to the condition of the existence of blood on the spot, and there is no dispute about that. Rather, the dispute is about the purity of the spot after the removal of the blood by vinegar. There is no dispute about it: but it was said: the prohibition is not due to honor... except that it has restrictions as well as has been verified. Regarding the corruption of water, that it includes fish as well, and it is not impure according to him, so then that what is meant by it is that which has life through flowing blood. 'It does not remove impurity' etc., because the command requires washing it with water, so if it were permissible to wash it with other than water, it would necessitate abandoning the command, which is not permissible, and because it explicitly states that removal of impurity is only by water, and explicit mention of something indicates the negation of what is other than it according to him, i.e., according to al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him. And the dispute etc.: and we say it is pure due to its removal physically, while according to him it does not become pure, and the text is silent about it, so adhering to it is not valid.
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage presents an example of a weak istinbat (derivation) attempt. The hadith commands washing blood from a garment with water. Someone might argue that since the command specifies water, any other substance (like vinegar) cannot remove impurity because the command implies exclusivity. However, the author argues this is weak because the hadith only addresses the situation when blood is present on the spot; it does not address what happens after the blood is removed by another substance. The dispute is about the purity of the spot after removal by vinegar, not about the obligation of washing with water. The author notes that the text is silent on the efficacy of vinegar, so using this hadith to prove vinegar does not work is invalid. The Hanafi position is that the spot becomes pure because impurity has been physically removed, while al-Shafi'i holds it does not become pure. The passage illustrates the principle that a text cannot be used to prove a point it does not address.

Key Terms
النص ساكت عنه (al-nass sākit ‘anhu): The text is silent about it; meaning the proof does not address the specific point of contention.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the hadith about washing blood with water considered weak for proving that vinegar does not remove impurity?
A) Because the hadith is not authentic.
B) Because the text is silent about the removal of impurity by vinegar after the blood is gone.
C) Because vinegar is not mentioned in the hadith at all.
D) Because the hadith only applies to menstrual blood.

Key Takeaway
A text that is silent on a point cannot be used to prove a ruling on that point; the hadith about washing blood cannot prove vinegar's ineffectiveness.

Interpreting the Letter Waw as Circumstantial vs Conjunctive
Explains rules for interpreting waw as حال or عطف with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule for interpreting the waw as conjunctive (عطف) or circumstantial (حال) to contractual statements and identify the thamarah al-khilaf when the intention is claimed.

Core Idea
The waw is literally conjunctive; only abandoned for circumstantial when conjunction is legally impossible and evidence supports it.

The letter waw (واو) is one of the most common particles in Arabic, and its interpretation can radically change a legal ruling. In Hanafi usul, the default is that waw joins two elements equally (عطف). However, when that conjunction leads to an impossibility or legal absurdity, it is interpreted metaphorically as a circumstantial clause (حال), making the second element a condition of the first. This lesson unpacks the criteria for this shift, using classical Hanafi examples including a well-known dispute between Abu Hanifa and his two disciples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the default meaning of waw
In Hanafi usul, the literal and apparent meaning of waw is conjunction (عطف). The burden of proof lies on the one who claims a metaphorical meaning.
2
Recognize when conjunction becomes impossible
If the literal conjunction would lead to a legal impossibility or absurdity (e.g., a master being owed money by his own slave), then the waw is re-interpreted as circumstantial.
3
Check for evidence supporting the circumstantial reading
The text must admit the meaning of hal, and there must be evidence (dilalah) that the speaker intended the circumstantial meaning. Mere possibility is not enough.
4
Apply to the divorce example and the intra-Hanafi dispute
When a wife says 'طلقني ولك ألف', Abu Hanifa treats the waw as conjunctive (no condition), while the Sahibayn treat it as circumstantial (creating a condition). The practical difference: wife owes nothing vs. owes the amount.
5
Distinguish between diyanatan and qada'an
If a speaker intends a circumstantial meaning contrary to the apparent, that intention is valid between him and Allah (diyanatan) but not in court (qada'an), because the court follows the apparent meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في تقرير حروف المعاني
ولو قال للحربي: "انزل وأنت آمن" لا يأمن بدون النزول..
وإنما تحمل "الواو" على الحال بطريق المجاز، فلابد من احتمال اللفظ ذلك، وقيام الدلالة على ثبوته كما في قول المولى لعبده: "أد إلي ألفاً وأنت حر"، فإن الحريَّة تتحقق حال الأداء وقامت الدلالة على ذلك، فإن المولى لا يستَوْجِبُ على عبده مالاً مع قيامِ الرِّقِ فيه، وقد صح التعليق به فحمل عليه.
ولو قال: "أنت طالق وأنت مريضة أو مصليّة" تطلق في الحال، ولو نوى التعليق صحت نيته فيما بينه وبين الله تعالى؛ لأن اللفظ وإن كان يحتمل معنى الحال إلا أن الظاهر خلافه، وإذا تأَيَّدَ ذلك بقصده ثَبَتَ.
ولو قال: "خذ هذه الألف مضاربةً واعمل بها في البز" لا يتقيَّدُ العملُ في البزّ، ويكون المضاربة عامةً؛ لأن العمل في البز لا يصلح حالاً لأخذ الألف مضاربة، فلا يتقيَّدُ صدر الكلام به.
وعلى هذا قال أبو حنيفة: إذا قالت لزوجها: "طلقني ولك ألف" فطلقها، لا يجب له عليها شيء؛ لأن قولها: "ولك ألف" لا يفيد حال وجوب الألف عليها، بل هو عطف على ما سبق وليس للحال حتى يكون شرطاً؛ لأن أصل الطلاق أن يكون بلا مال؛ لأنه إن ذكر المال سمي خلعاً ويصير يميناً من جانب، وليس أيضاً من صيغ العدة والنذر حتى يلزم عليها وفاؤه فكان لغواً. وعندهما: هذه الواو ليست كما كانت عنده بل للحال، والحال في معنى الشرط للعامل، فيصير كأنها قالت: "طلقني والحال أن لك ألفاً علي"، فلما قال الزوج: طلقت أو...
وعلى هذا: أي على أن ما لا يصلح حالاً لا يجعل الواو فيه للحال، قال أبو حنيفة محله: إذا قالت لزوجها: "طلقني ولك ألف درهم"، فطلقها لا يجب شيء للزوج عليها؛ لأن قولها "ولك ألف" معطوف على ما سبق وليس للحال حتى يكون شرطاً؛ لأن أصل الطلاق أن يكون بلا مال؛ لأنه إن ذكر المال سمي خلعاً ويصير يميناً من جانب، وليس أيضاً من صيغ العدة والنذر حتى يلزم عليها وفاؤه فكان لغواً. وعندهما: هذه الواو ليست كما كانت عنده بل للحال، والحال في معنى الشرط للعامل، فيصير كأنها قالت: "طلقني والحال أن لك ألفاً علي"، فلما قال الزوج: طلقت أو...
Chapter on the determination of particles of meaning. If one says to a harbi: 'Descend and you are safe', he is not safe without descending. The waw is interpreted as circumstantial (al-hal) by way of metaphor, so it requires the word to admit that meaning and evidence to establish it, as in the master's statement to his slave: 'Pay me a thousand and you are free.' For freedom occurs at the time of payment and evidence for that is present, since the master is not entitled to money from his slave while slavery exists, and the suspension has been validly attached to it, so it is interpreted accordingly. If one says: 'You are divorced and you are sick or praying,' she is divorced immediately; if he intended suspension, his intention is valid between him and Allah Almighty because the word can admit the meaning of hal but the apparent meaning contradicts it, and when it is supported by his intention, it is established. If one says: 'Take this thousand as mudaraba and work with it in cloth,' the work is not restricted to cloth, and the mudaraba is general, because working in cloth is not suitable as a hal for taking the thousand as mudaraba, so the beginning of the statement is not restricted by it. On this, Abu Hanifa said: When a wife says to her husband: 'Divorce me and you have a thousand,' and he divorces her, nothing is obligatory upon her because her statement 'and you have a thousand' does not establish a hal of obligation of the thousand upon her; rather it is conjoined to what preceded and is not for hal so as to be a condition, because the original in divorce is without wealth; for if wealth is mentioned it is called khul' and becomes an oath from one side, and it is also not from the formulas of indemnity or vow so that its fulfillment would be binding, so it is in vain. According to the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): this waw is not as per Abu Hanifa but is for hal, and hal is in the meaning of a condition for the operative word, so it becomes as if she said: 'Divorce me while you have a thousand owed to me,' and when the husband said 'You are divorced' or...
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage is a detailed usul discussion on the interpretation of the Arabic particle 'waw' (واو) in contractual and conditional statements. The author states that the waw can be either conjunctive (عاطفة) or circumstantial (حال). The default is conjunction, but when conjunction leads to an impossibility or legal absurdity, the waw is interpreted as circumstantial by way of metaphor. The first example is a harbi (enemy combatant) being told 'descend and you are safe' – safety cannot precede descent, so the waw must be circumstantial, meaning safety is contingent upon descending. Similarly, a master telling his slave 'pay me a thousand and you are free' – since the master cannot demand money from his own slave (as the slave's property belongs to the master), the conjunction would imply an obligation that cannot exist, so the waw is circumstantial, making freedom conditional upon payment. In contrast, when a husband says to his wife 'you are divorced and you are sick,' the apparent situation (a husband typically would not divorce his wife while she is sick due to compassion) allows the literal conjunction, so the divorce is immediate. However, if he intended the waw as circumstantial to suspend divorce on sickness, that intention works between him and Allah (dyanatan) but is not accepted in court (qada'an) because it contradicts the apparent meaning. The next example: 'take this thousand as mudaraba and work with it in cloth' – since working in cloth is subsequent to taking the money, it cannot be a circumstantial hal (which must be concurrent), so the waw is conjunctive and the mudaraba is unrestricted. The passage then applies this principle to a case of divorce: a wife says 'divorce me and you have a thousand' – according to Imam Abu Hanifa, the waw is conjunctive, so the statement does not establish a condition; the divorce is without compensation because mentioning wealth in divorce turns it into khul', but here the wife did not intend khul', and there is no binding promise. According to the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), the waw is circumstantial, making the thousand a condition of divorce, so the wife owes the amount. The text also notes that when a speaker intends a metaphorical interpretation contrary to the apparent meaning, his intention is valid only between him and Allah, not in judicial proceedings, due to the presumption of falsehood.

Key Terms
الواو للحال (al-waw lil-hal): The letter waw used to indicate a circumstantial clause, metaphorically, when conjunction is impossible or legally invalid.
العطف (al-‘atf): Conjunction using waw, the literal and default meaning.
ديانة وقضاء (diyānatan wa qaḍā’an): Diyanatan: between the person and Allah; qada’an: enforceable in court. Intention contrary to apparent meaning is valid diyanatan but not qada’an.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example 'خذ هذه الألف مضاربة واعمل بها في البز', why is the waw interpreted as conjunctive rather than circumstantial?
A) Because the word 'البز' (cloth) is specific.
B) Because working in cloth is subsequent to taking the money, so it cannot be a simultaneous hal.
C) Because mudaraba requires unrestricted use of capital.
D) Because the speaker intended to restrict the mudaraba to cloth.

Key Takeaway
The waw is literally conjunctive; it shifts to circumstantial only when conjunction is legally impossible and evidence supports the shift — this protects legal certainty.

The Particle Fa Indicating Immediate Sequence
Establishing the rule that 'fa' indicates immediate sequence without delay in contracts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that the particle 'fa' indicates immediate sequence (al-ta'qib ma'a al-wasl) to interpret contractual statements and distinguish it from the particle 'waw'.

Core Idea
'Fa' forces immediate sequence; 'waw' does not – changing the particle changes the legal outcome.

In this passage, we learn a foundational usul rule about the Arabic particle 'fa'. The Hanafi school holds that 'fa' in legal expressions like 'فهو حر' (so he is free) creates an immediate legal effect without any delay. This distinction is crucial for correctly interpreting whether a statement constitutes acceptance, rejection, or a condition in contracts. Understanding this rule will sharpen your ability to analyze contractual language in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the particle and its function
Look for the particle 'fa' in the buyer's statement 'فهو حر'. Note that it is used to connect the emancipation to the sale.
2
Apply the Hanafi rule of 'fa'
The Hanafi rule states that 'fa' indicates immediate sequence with continuity. That means the second event (being free) follows the first (the sale) without any gap.
This is based on the linguistic meaning of 'fa'.
3
Interpret the statement in context
Since the emancipation must be immediate upon the sale, the only way that makes sense is if the buyer accepts the sale. Therefore, the statement implies acceptance by necessity (iqtida').
This is the key reasoning: the 'fa' forces the immediate effect, so the contract must be valid.
4
Contrast with 'waw'
If the buyer had said 'وهو حر' (and he is free) using 'waw', there is no implication of sequence. The emancipation is not tied to the sale immediately, so the statement cannot be interpreted as acceptance. It would be a rejection.
This shows the critical difference between the two particles.
5
Derive the ruling
Thus, 'فهو حر' after a sale offer constitutes an acceptance of the sale, and the slave becomes free immediately following the sale. This is the relied-upon Hanafi position.
This ruling applies in similar contractual contexts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الفاء إشارة إلى الفرق "الفاء" للتعقيب مع الوصل، ولهذا تستعمل في الأجزية؛ لما أنها تتعقب الشرط. جمع جزاء قال أصحابنا الله : إذا قال: "بعتُ منك هذا العبد بألف"، فقال الآخر: "فهو المشتري يكون ذلك قبولاً للبيع اقتضاء ويثبت العتق منه عقيب البيع، بخلاف ما لو قال: "وهو حر" أو "هو حر" فإنه يكون ردًّا للبيع. وإذا قال للخياط: "أنظر إلى هذا الثوب أيكفيني قميصاً؟" فنظر فقال: "نعم" فقال صاحب الثوب: "فاقطعه" فقطعه، الخياط الثوب طلقت أو طلقت أو فعلت كان تقديره طلقت بذلك الشرط، فكان المال شرطاً وبدلاً للطلاق، فكان معاوضة في معنى الخلع، فيجب الألف ويكون الطلاق بائناً كما في احمل هذا المتاع ولك درهم" حيث كان الدرهم بدلاً فافهم. بحقيقة اللفظ وهي العطف؛ لأن المعاوضة في الإجارة أصلية لم تشرع إلا بالبدل كسائر البيوع، وجاز أن تعارض أمراً أصلياً آخر، فأمكن حمل اللفظ على المجاز باعتبار معنى المعاوضة كذا في "المعدن". الفاء للتعقيب: أي مع الوصل يعني موجبه وجود الثاني بعد الأول بغير مهلة حتى لو قال: ضربت زيداً فعمرواً، كان المعنى أن ضرب عمرو وقع عقيب ضرب زيد و لم تطاولت المدة بينهما، ولفظ التعقيب يشير إلى أنه ليس للمقارنة، ولفظ الوصل يشير إلى أنه ليس للتراخي كذا في "الفصول". مع الوصل: إلا إذا دل الدليل كما في قولهم: نكحت فولدت"، و"كل حي يولد فيموت"، وقول الراوي: "زنى ماعز فرجم . لما أنها تتعقب إلخ: بلا فصل كما في قوله: "إن دخلت الدار فأنت طالق"، أن الطلاق يقع عند وجود الدخول من غير فصل وتراخ كذا في "الفصول". عقيب البيع: أي يجعل الآخر قابلاً للبيع ثم معتقاً؛ لأن الفاء في قوله: "فهو حر" للتعقيب، فالمشترى أثبت الحرية عقيب البيع الصادر من البائع، وذلك لا يكون إلا بقبول العقد، فيكون قوله: "فهو حر" مقتضياً قبول العقد بطريق الاقتضاء، وصار كأنه قال: قبلت فهو حر كذا في "المعدن".
Section on the Fa. Indication of the difference. 'Fa' is for immediate sequence with continuity, and therefore it is used in the results (jaza') because they follow the condition. It is a collection of results. Our companions (may Allah be pleased with them) said: If he says: 'I sold you this slave for a thousand,' and the other says: 'So he is free,' that becomes an acceptance of the sale by implication and emancipation is established from him following the sale, contrary to if he said: 'He is free' or 'He is free,' then it would be a rejection of the sale. And if he says to the tailor: 'Look at this cloth, does it suffice me for a shirt?' and he looked and said: 'Yes,' and the owner of the cloth said: 'So cut it,' and he cut it, the cloth. [The continuation from the previous case:] 'I divorce you' or 'I divorce you' or 'I have done' – its estimation is 'I divorce you by that condition,' so the money becomes a condition and a substitute for the divorce, so it becomes a compensation in the sense of khul', so the thousand is obligatory and the divorce is irrevocable, as in 'Take this goods and you have a dirham,' where the dirham is a substitute, so understand. With the literal meaning, which is conjunction; because compensation in hire is original, not legislated except with a substitute like other sales, and it is permissible to oppose another original matter, so it is possible to carry the word metaphorically considering the meaning of compensation – thus in al-Ma'din. 'Fa' is for immediate sequence: i.e., with continuity, meaning its requirement is the existence of the second after the first without delay, such that if he said: 'I hit Zayd so 'Amr,' the meaning is that hitting 'Amr occurred following hitting Zayd and the interval between them did not prolong. The word immediate sequence indicates that it is not for simultaneity, and the word continuity indicates that it is not for delay – thus in al-Fusul. With continuity: except when evidence indicates, as in their saying: 'She married so she gave birth,' and 'Every living being is born so it dies,' and the narrator's saying: 'Ma'iz committed adultery so he was stoned.' Because they follow the condition, etc.: without separation as in his saying: 'If you enter the house, you are divorced,' that divorce occurs at the occurrence of entry without separation and delay – thus in al-Fusul. Following the sale: i.e., it makes the other a buyer then an emancipator; because the fa in his saying 'he is free' is for immediate sequence, so the buyer establishes freedom following the sale issued by the seller, and that is not possible except by acceptance of the contract, so his saying 'he is free' necessitates acceptance of the contract by way of implication, and it becomes as if he said: 'I accept, so he is free' – thus in al-Ma'din.
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage explains the rule for the particle 'fa' in legal language. The author states that 'fa' indicates immediate sequence with continuity (al-ta'qib ma'a al-wasl), meaning the second action follows the first without any delay. This is why it is used for the results of conditions (jaza'). The key example is in a sale: if the seller says 'I sold you this slave for a thousand' and the buyer says 'فهو حر' (so he is free), this is interpreted as an acceptance of the sale with the immediate emancipation of the slave, because the 'fa' links the freedom to the sale immediately. However, if the buyer says 'وهو حر' (and he is free) using 'waw', it would be a rejection of the sale, because 'waw' does not imply sequence. Another example is when a person asks a tailor to look at cloth and then says 'فاقطعه' (so cut it); the cutting follows immediately upon the confirmation. The passage also revisits the previous dispute about the wife's statement, explaining that according to the Sahibayn, if the husband says 'طلقت' (I divorce you) using 'fa', it would make the money a condition, leading to the obligation of the thousand and a ba'in divorce. The commentary elaborates that 'fa' must indicate immediate sequence unless context suggests otherwise, as in examples like 'marry then give birth' where there is a natural interval. Finally, it affirms that the buyer's statement 'فهو حر' by implication accepts the contract because the immediate sequence of freedom requires the contract to be valid.

Key Terms
الفاء للتعقيب (al-fa li'l-ta‘qib): The particle 'fa' used to indicate that the second action occurs immediately after the first with no gap.
الاقتضاء (al-iqtida'): Implication by necessity; the wording implies something that must be understood for the statement to be valid.
التعقيب مع الوصل (al-ta‘qib ma‘a al-wasl): Immediate sequence with continuity; the second event follows the first without any intervening delay.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example from the lesson, if the buyer says 'فهو حر' after the seller says 'بعت', what is the legal effect according to the Hanafi school?
A) The buyer rejects the sale.
B) The buyer accepts the sale and the slave is free immediately.
C) The buyer accepts the sale but the slave is not free.
D) The buyer's statement has no legal effect.

Key Takeaway
The particle 'fa' in Arabic contracts forces immediate sequence, turning a statement like 'فهو حر' into an acceptance by implication; using 'waw' instead would make it a rejection.
