The Effect of Fa in Contract Acceptance: Implication by Necessity
Explaining the effect of fa in a sale acceptance statement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul principle of iqtida' to determine whether a statement containing fa constitutes acceptance of a sale offer.

Core Idea
Fa indicates immediate succession, so 'فهو حر' implies acceptance since freedom requires ownership first.

Imagine a seller says 'I sold you this slave for one thousand,' and the buyer responds 'فهو حر' (so he is free). Is the sale concluded? Hanafi usul teaches that the particle fa (فاء) triggers an implication: because emancipation can only occur after ownership, the buyer must have accepted the offer. This lesson dissects the linguistic and legal reasoning behind this ruling, focusing on the concept of iqtida' (implication by necessity).

Guided Path
1
Identify the offer and response
Recognize the seller's statement as an offer (ایجاب) and the buyer's response as a statement containing the particle fa.
The offer is 'بعتُ منك هذا العبد بألف' (I sold you this slave for one thousand).
2
Analyze the role of fa
In Arabic, fa indicates immediate succession (تعقیب). The word 'فهو' means 'so he is...', linking the response directly to the offer.
This linguistic rule is foundational in Hanafi usul.
3
Apply the principle of iqtida'
Emancipation (عتق) is valid only after ownership. For the speaker to declare 'فهو حر' immediately after an offer, he must have accepted the sale to establish ownership. Hence, acceptance is implied by necessity.
The implication (اقتضاء) arises because the statement would be invalid without the unspoken acceptance.
4
Distinguish between cases with and without fa
If the response omits fa (e.g., 'هو حر'), there is no immediate succession, and the statement is treated as a rejection of the offer, not acceptance.
The presence or absence of fa is decisive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أصحابنا الله : إذا قال: "بعتُ منك هذا العبد بألف"، فقال الآخر: "فهو المشتري" يكون ذلك قبولاً للبيع اقتضاء ويثبت العتق منه عقيب البيع، بخلاف ما لو قال: "وهو حر" أو "هو حر" فإنه يكون ردًّا للبيع. عقيب البيع: أي يجعل الآخر قابلاً للبيع ثم معتقاً؛ لأن الفاء في قوله: "فهو حر" للتعقيب، فالمشترى أثبت الحرية عقيب البيع الصادر من البائع، وذلك لا يكون إلا بقبول العقد، فيكون قوله: "فهو حر" مقتضياً قبول العقد بطريق الاقتضاء، وصار كأنه قال: قبلت فهو حر كذا في "المعدن".
Our companions (may Allah be pleased with them) said: If one says, 'I sold you this slave for one thousand,' and the other says, 'So he is the buyer,' that constitutes acceptance of the sale by implication, and emancipation is established from him immediately after the sale, unlike if he said, 'He is free' or 'He is free,' for that would be a rejection of the sale. (Commentary:) 'Immediately after the sale': i.e., he makes the other a buyer and then a manumitter; because the fa in his statement 'So he is free' is for immediate succession, so the buyer affirmed freedom immediately after the sale issued from the seller, and that is not possible except by accepting the contract. Thus his statement 'So he is free' necessitates acceptance of the contract by way of implication (iqtida'), and it is as if he said, 'I accept, so he is free.' As in 'al-Ma'din.'
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage applies the rule of الفاء to a specific legal scenario: a sale offer followed by a response containing fa. If the buyer says 'فهو المشتري' (so he is the buyer), it counts as acceptance by implication due to the fa, because it shows immediate acceptance. Similarly, if he says 'فهو حر' (so he is free), it also implies acceptance of the sale since emancipation follows immediately after ownership. However, if he says 'هو حر' (he is free) without the fa, it is a rejection, because without fa, it is not linked to the offer. The commentary clarifies that the fa necessitates acceptance because emancipation can only occur after ownership is established, so the speaker must have accepted the sale first.

Key Terms
اقتضاء (iqtida'): Implication; a statement that necessitates an unstated element for its validity.
قبول (qabul): Acceptance in a contract, which must be expressed either explicitly or by implication.
تعقيب (ta'qib): Immediate succession; the function of the particle fa to indicate that one event follows another without delay.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is a clear rejection of the offer.
B) It is an acceptance by implication and the slave is freed.
C) It is neither acceptance nor rejection; the sale remains pending.
D) It is an acceptance but the slave is not freed until explicit verbal acceptance.

Key Takeaway
The particle fa in a statement like 'فهو حر' after a sale offer triggers an implication (iqtida') that the speaker accepted the offer, because the stated action cannot occur without prior ownership.

The Particle Fa: Immediate Succession (Ta'qib) in Hanafi Legal Commands
Explains the legal effect of 'fa' as requiring immediate succession.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul rule that the particle 'fa' imposes immediate succession (ta'qib) to distinguish between conditional and absolute commands.

Core Idea
The letter fa binds the commanded action to immediately follow its condition; without it, the command is absolute.

This passage demonstrates through concrete fiqh examples how the presence or absence of the particle 'fa' changes legal liability, contract validity, and divorce occurrence. Understanding this rule is essential for properly interpreting commands in legal texts and distinguishing between conditional and absolute obligations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic structure
Look for the presence of fa in the conditional or imperative phrase. Determine if it is used for immediate succession (ta'qib) or for causation (ta'lil).
The default in legal contexts is ta'qib unless evidence suggests causation.
2
Apply the rule of ta'qib
If fa appears, the commanded action is conditional upon the preceding event and must be executed immediately after that event, without delay.
Immediacy is understood as sequential without significant gap.
3
Contrast with absolute command
If fa is absent (e.g., using 'waw' or imperative alone), the command is absolute and carries no temporal restriction, giving full discretion.
An absolute command (amr mutlaq) is valid at any time after issuance.
4
Analyse the examples
Work through the tailor, sale, and divorce examples to see how the presence/absence of fa determines the ruling (liability, completion, or no effect).
In the divorce example, the condition requires consecutive entries; otherwise, no divorce.
5
Note the secondary usage
Recognize that fa can also indicate causation (ta'lil), but this is a separate function not conflicting with ta'qib.
When fa indicates causation, it links a ruling to its reason, not necessarily requiring immediacy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الفاء
فإذا هو لا يكفيه كان الخياط ضامناً؛ لأنه إنما أمره بالقطع عقيب الكفاية، بخلاف ما لو قال: "أقطعه" أو "واقطَعْه" فقَطَعَه، فإنه لا يكون الخياط ضامناً.
ولو قال: "بعتُ منك هذا الثوب بعشرة فاقطعه فقَطَعَه ولم يقل شيئًا كان البيع تاما، ولو قال: إن دخلت هذه الدار فهذه الدار فأنت طالق"، فالشرط دخولُ الثانية عقيب دخول الأولى متصلاً به، حتى لو دخلت الثانية أولاً أو آخراً لكنه بعد مدة لا يقع الطلاق. وقد يكون الفاء لبيان العلة.
كان الخياط ضامناً إلخ لأنه لم يجز قطعه إلا متعقباً على وجود الكفاية لا مطلقاً؛ لأن الفاء في قوله: "فاقطعه" للتعقيب، فكأنه قال: "إن كفاني قميصاً فاقطعه"، فإذا لم يكف كان القطع حاصلاً بدون الإذن، فكان موجباً للضمان كذا في "المعدن". فإنه لا يكون إلخ لأن قوله: "أقطعه" إذن مطلق، فلا يكون القطع بعده موجباً للضمان؛ لعموم الإجازة بلا تقييد قيد فيه. لا يقع الطلاق لعدم وجود الشرط، وهو دخول الثانية عقيب دخول الأولى متصلاً به؛ لأن موجب الفاء التعقيب، فيقتضي أن يكون الشرط دخول الثانية بعد دخول الأولى، فإذا دخلت الثانية أولاً لم يوجد الاتصال كذا في "المعدن".
Chapter on the letter Fa: So if he (the cloth owner) does not suffice, the tailor is liable; because he only ordered the cutting immediately after sufficiency, unlike if he said: 'Cut it' or 'and cut it' and he cut it, then the tailor is not liable. And if he said: 'I sold you this cloth for ten and cut it' and he cut it without saying anything, the sale is complete. And if he said: 'If you enter this house, then this house, then you are divorced,' the condition is entering the second immediately after entering the first consecutively, such that if she enters the second first or later but after a period, divorce does not occur. And sometimes fa is for clarification of the cause. 'The tailor is liable' etc. because he did not permit cutting except immediately following the existence of sufficiency, not absolutely; because fa in his statement 'so cut it' is for immediate succession, so it is as if he said: 'If he suffices me with a shirt, then cut it.' So if he did not suffice, cutting occurred without permission, thus it necessitated liability. This is in al-Ma'din. 'Then he is not' etc. because his saying 'Cut it' is absolute permission, so cutting thereafter does not necessitate liability due to the generality of the permission without a restriction. Divorce does not occur due to absence of the condition, which is entering the second immediately after entering the first consecutively; because the effect of fa is immediate succession, so it requires that the condition be entering the second after entering the first, and if she enters the second first, connection is not found. This is in al-Ma'din.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage is a legal analysis of the Arabic particle 'fa' (ف) which indicates immediate succession (ta'qib). The commentator explains several examples from fiqh to illustrate how the presence or absence of 'fa' changes the ruling. The first example: if a cloth owner tells a tailor 'If you suffice me with a shirt, then (fa) cut it,' the tailor is only authorized to cut immediately after sufficiency. If he cuts before, he is liable (damān). But if the owner says simply 'Cut it' (without fa), he has absolute permission and no liability. The second example involves a conditional sale: 'I sold you this cloth for ten, so (fa) cut it'—the sale is complete once cut. The third example is a conditional divorce: 'If you enter this house, then this house, then (fa) you are divorced'—the condition requires immediate successive entry of the second house after the first; otherwise no divorce. The commentator cites al-Ma'din throughout. The passage type is a usul rule demonstrating how a linguistic particle (fa) has binding legal consequences in Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
التعقيب (al-ta'qib): Immediate succession without delay between two events
الفاء (al-fa'): A particle that, when used in legal language, imposes immediate succession (ta'qib)

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because the cutting follows the command immediately
B) Because the command is absolute without temporal restriction
C) Because the tailor has discretion to cut whenever he wants
D) Because the owner implicitly consented to any cutting

Key Takeaway
The particle fa in legal commands imposes immediate succession (ta'qib); its absence makes the command absolute, and it may also indicate causation in other contexts.

Fa as Causation: Immediate Effect in Manumission and Aman
Istinbat: deriving immediate rulings from fa as causation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul principle that 'fa' indicates causation (ta'lil) when a declarative follows an imperative and the attribute is continuous, to derive that manumission and aman are immediate.

Core Idea
When fa conjoins an imperative and a declarative describing a continuous state, it signals causation—the result is immediate, not conditional.

The phrase 'Pay me a thousand so you are free' appears conditional, but Hanafi usul interprets 'fa' as causation because conjunction between imperative and declarative is impossible. Freedom is a continuous state, so the meaning becomes 'Pay because you are free'—manumission is immediate and the thousand becomes a debt. Similarly, 'Dismount so you are safe' grants immediate aman. This lesson unpacks the istinbat behind these rulings from al-Jami' al-Saghir.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the linguistic conflict
Identify that 'fa' links an imperative (pay/dismount) with a declarative (you are free/you are safe). Hanafi usul holds that conjoining a declarative to an imperative without a cause is invalid, so 'fa' cannot be simple conjunction (al-‘atf).
The imperative is a request; the declarative states a fact. Conjunction would create an impossible mixed construction.
2
Interpret fa as causation
Since conjunction is impossible, 'fa' must indicate causation (fa al-ta‘lil). The meaning becomes: 'You are free, so pay a thousand' or 'Pay because you are free.' The condition is not suspensive but explanatory.
This is a standard Hanafi istihsan based on linguistic necessity.
3
Consider the attribute's continuity
Freedom and safety are continuous states—they exist before the command and continue after. Because continuity is inherent, causation is plausible: the existing state prompts the command.
If the attribute were momentary (e.g., a single act), causation might fail.
4
Derive the legal effect
Manumission and aman are immediate (munajjiz). The thousand becomes a debt on the freed slave; the dismount is not a condition for safety. The ruling is based on the absence of any textual indication of suspension.
This is the mufta bihi position from Zahir al-Riwaya.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مثاله: إذا قال لعبده: "أد إلي ألفاً فأنت حر كان العبدُ حرا في الحال وإن لم يؤد شيئًا.
ولو قال للحربي: "انزل فأنت آمن كان آمناً وإن لم ينزل. وفي "الجامع" ما إذا قال:
"أَمرُ امرأتي بيدك فطلقها" فطلقها في المجلس طلقت تطليقةً بائنة، ولا يكون الثاني توكيلاً وهو قوله: فطلقها بطلاق غير الأول، فصار كأنه قال: طلقها بسبب أن أمرها بيدك.
ولو قال: "طلقها فجعلتُ أمرها بيدك" فطلقها في المجلس طلقت تطليقةً رجعية.
ولو قال: "طلقها وجعلتُ أمرها بيدك" وطلقها في المجلس طلقت تطليقتين.
كان العبد حراً إلخ فالحرية دائمة الوجود حيث كانت موجودة قبل الأداء تبقى بعده إلى مدة، فلا يتوقف على أداء الألف بل يكون حراً، ويصير الألف ديناً على العبد الذي صار حراً. كان العبد حراً إلخ: لأن الفاء دخلت على العلة؛ لأن الحرية دائم فيصير متراخياً عن الأداء نظراً إلى البقاء، فأشبه التراخي في الابتداء، فيصح دخول الفاء عليه، فصار معناه أد إلى ألفاً لأنك حرّ، فلا يتعلق العتق بالأداء ويتنجز العتق؛ لأنه لا دلالة في الكلام على التعليق، وإنما حملت الفاء على العلة لتعذر حقيقتها وهو العطف؛ لما سبق أن عطف الخبرية على الطلبية غير جائز، وكذلك المسئلة الآتية كذا في "المعدن".
وفي الجامع إلخ الجامع الصغير، كتاب في فروع الحنفية للإمام الرباني محمد بن الحسن الشيباني له، وهو من كتب ظاهر الرواية. [الشافي: ص ١٧٤] ما سبق من الأمثلة من نظائر دخول الفاء على العلة، وهذه المسائل من نظائر دخول الفاء على حكم العلة، ولهذا يقع الطلاق الواحد إذا طلقها؛ لأن الفاء لبيان حكم العلة، فكان قوله: "فطلقها" أمراً بمباشرة ما فوض إليه من الأمر باليد كذا في "المعدن". بائنةً: لأن المفوض بالأمر باليد هو البائن؛ لأنه كناية ولا يقع بالكناية إلا الواحد البائن كذا في "المعدن". بسبب أن أمرَها بيدك فكان الثاني وهو قوله: "فطلقها" هو الطلاق المفوض، ولا يكون توكيلاً بالطلاق غير الأول، فلا تقع إلا واحدة، ولو كان للعطف تقع تطليقتان طلقت إلخ أي يقع في هذه الصورة الطلاق الواحد الرجعي؛ لأنه تفويض لصريح الطلاق وهو رجعي، والأمر باليد بيان له تطليقتين لأن قوله: "طلقها" توكيل لصريح الطلاق، وقوله: "جعلت أمرها بيدك تفويض الطلاق إليه، لكنه ليس بحكم الأول؛ لأن الواو لا يحتمل ذلك، فكان الثاني غير الأول فصار المأمور وكيلاً بتطليقتين: أحدهما: بائن وهو الأمر باليد كناية، والأخرى: فإذا قال في المجلس طلقتها، فقد أتى بما وكل به وهو الطلقتان فوقعتا، لكنه يكون كلاهما بائنين؛ لأن الرجعي يصير بائناً مع البائن؛ لأنه إذا وقع البائن فلا رجعة بعده ترجيحاً للمحرم كذا في "المعدن".
Example: If he says to his slave: 'Pay me one thousand, so (fa) you are free,' the slave is free immediately even if he does not pay anything. And if he says to a harbi: 'Dismount, so (fa) you are safe,' he is safe even if he does not dismount. And in al-Jami': if he says: 'My wife's affair is in your hand, so (fa) divorce her,' and he divorces her in the session, one irrevocable divorce (ba'in) occurs, and the second part (his saying 'so divorce her') is not an agency for a different divorce; it becomes as if he said: 'Divorce her because her affair is in your hand.' And if he says: 'Divorce her, then (fa) I have placed her affair in your hand,' and he divorces her in the session, one revocable divorce (raj'i) occurs. And if he says: 'Divorce her and (wa) I have placed her affair in your hand,' and he divorces her in the session, two divorces occur. 'The slave is free' etc. Freedom is continuous in existence: it existed before payment, remains after for a time, so it does not depend on payment of the thousand; rather, the slave is free and the thousand becomes a debt on the freed slave. 'The slave is free' etc. because fa entered upon the cause; because freedom is continuous, so it becomes delayed from payment in consideration of continuity, thus resembling delay in the beginning, so it is valid for fa to enter upon it. Thus the meaning becomes: 'Pay me a thousand because you are free,' so manumission is not suspended on payment and becomes immediate; because there is no indication of suspension in the speech. Rather, fa is interpreted as causation because its literal meaning (conjunction) is impossible, since conjoining a declarative to an imperative is not permissible. Similarly for the following case, as in al-Ma'din. 'And in al-Jami'...' al-Jami' al-Saghir, a book on Hanafi furu' by Imam Muhammad ibn al-Hasan al-Shaybani, which is among the books of Zahir al-Riwaya. The previous examples are parallels of fa entering upon the cause, and these cases are parallels of fa entering upon the ruling of the cause. For this reason, one divorce occurs if he divorces her; because fa is for clarifying the ruling of the cause, so his statement 'so divorce her' is a command to execute what was delegated to him of the affair of the hand (tamlik). 'Irrevocable' because what is delegated by the affair of the hand is irrevocable divorce; because it is a kinayah (indirect expression) and only one irrevocable divorce occurs by kinayah. 'Because her affair is in your hand, so the second (i.e., 'so divorce her') is the delegated divorce, and it is not an agency for a different divorce, so only one occurs. If it were for conjunction, two would occur. 'One revocable divorce occurs' in the second formulation; because it is a delegation of explicit divorce, which is revocable, and the affair of the hand is an elaboration of it. 'Two divorces' because his saying 'divorce her' is an agency for explicit divorce, and his saying 'and I have placed her affair in your hand' is a delegation of divorce to him, but it is not governed by the first; because wa (and) does not carry that, so the second is different from the first. Thus the commanded becomes an agent for two divorces: one irrevocable (by the hand, a kinayah) and another; so if he says in the session 'I divorced her,' he has executed what he was authorized for, namely two divorces, and both fall. However, both become irrevocable because the revocable becomes irrevocable when combined with an irrevocable; because when an irrevocable occurs, there is no return after it, preferring the prohibited. This is in al-Ma'din.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage applies the rules of 'fa' to specific legal cases from al-Jami' al-Saghir by Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani. The first case: if a master says to his slave 'Pay me 1000, so you are free,' the slave is free immediately; the condition is interpreted as causation because freedom is continuous. The 1000 becomes a debt. Second case: saying to a harbi 'Dismount, so you are safe' grants safety immediately. Then three divorce cases: (1) 'My wife's affair is in your hand, so divorce her' — 'fa' indicates that the delegation is the cause, so the agent executes one irrevocable divorce. (2) 'Divorce her, then (fa) I have placed her affair in your hand' — 'fa' here is for causation linking the command to the delegation, resulting in one revocable divorce. (3) 'Divorce her and (wa) I have placed her affair in your hand' — 'wa' (and) indicates separate acts, so two divorces occur. The commentary from al-Ma'din explains that in the third case, both divorces become irrevocable when combined. The passage type is istinbat, deriving rulings from usul principles applied to fiqh cases. The authority weight is Zahir al-Riwaya as the cases come from al-Jami' al-Saghir.

Key Terms
الفاء للتعليل (al-fa' al-ta'lil): The particle 'fa' used to indicate causation, i.e., the second clause is the cause or reason for the first.
ظاهر الرواية (Zahir al-Riwaya): The six core books of Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani, considered the most authoritative transmitted texts of the Hanafi school
الجامع الصغير (al-Jami' al-Saghir): A book by Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani on Hanafi furu', part of Zahir al-Riwaya

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is 'fa' in the phrase 'Pay me a thousand, so you are free' interpreted as causation rather than conjunction?
A) Because conjunction between an imperative and a declarative is impossible
B) Because the thousand is a debt
C) Because freedom is a continuous state
D) Because the slave asked for it

Key Takeaway
When 'fa' links an imperative to a declarative describing a continuous state, Hanafi usul interprets it as causation, making the result immediate.

The Effect of 'Fa' and 'Waw' in Divorce Delegation
Explaining effects of fa in divorce delegation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the usul principle governing 'fa' and 'waw' in delegation contexts and derive the number and type of divorces from each phrasing.

Core Idea
Fa connects the ruling to its cause, yielding one divorce; waw separates them, yielding two.

This passage from Al-Jami' al-Saghir demonstrates how a single particle—'fa' or 'waw'—can change the legal outcome. By analyzing the language, we uncover the Hanafi rule that 'fa' indicates a causal link (sababiyya) while 'waw' indicates independence. Understanding this refines your ability to interpret contractual language.

Guided Path
1
Identify the operative phrases
Locate the two constructions: 'أمر امرأتي بيدك فطلقها' (fa) and 'طلقها وجعلت أمرها بيدك' (waw). These are the texts for analysis.
The first uses fa, the second uses waw.
2
Classify the conjunction
In Hanafi usul, 'fa' denotes ta'qib (immediacy) and often sababiyya (causation). 'Waw' denotes pure conjunction without causal implication.
This classification is based on the dominant usage in Arabic and confirmed by Hanafi jurists.
3
Apply the usul rule
Since 'fa' links the divorce command to the delegation as a cause, only one divorce occurs (the delegated one). With 'waw', the commands are independent, so two divorces occur—one from the sarih command and one from the delegation.
The rule: 'al-fa' li ta'lil al-hukm bi al-illah' (fa is for making the ruling dependent on the cause).
4
Determine the type of divorce
The first divorce is ba'in because the delegation phrase 'أمر امرأتي بيدك' is a kinayah for divorce, which in Hanafi law effects one ba'in talaq. In the second scenario, the sarih command yields a raj'i divorce, but when combined with the ba'in from the delegation, both become ba'in to avoid contradiction.
The commentary explains: 'الرجعي يصير بائناً مع البائن'.
5
Derive the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical outcome: the number and revocability of divorces depend on the particle used. This shows the importance of precise wording in legal documents.
Mufta bihi: as per the text, the first scenario gives one ba'in, the second gives two ba'in.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الجامع ما إذا قال: "أمر امرأتي بيدك فطلقها" فطلقها في المجلس طلقت تطليقةً بائنة، ولا يكون الثاني توكيلاً وهو قوله: فطلقها بطلاق غير الأول، فصار كأنه قال: طلقها بسبب أن أمرها بيدك.
ولو قال: "طلقها فجعلتُ أمرها بيدك" فطلقها في المجلس طلقت تطليقةً رجعية.
ولو قال: "طلقها وجعلتُ أمرها بيدك" وطلقها في المجلس طلقت تطليقتين.
وفي الجامع إلخ الجامع الصغير، كتاب في فروع الحنفية للإمام الرباني محمد بن الحسن الشيباني له، وهو من كتب ظاهر الرواية. [الشافي: ص ١٧٤] ما سبق من الأمثلة من نظائر دخول الفاء على العلة، وهذه المسائل من نظائر دخول الفاء على حكم العلة، ولهذا يقع الطلاق الواحد إذا طلقها؛ لأن الفاء لبيان حكم العلة، فكان قوله: "فطلقها" أمراً بمباشرة ما فوض إليه من الأمر باليد كذا في "المعدن". بائنةً: لأن المفوض بالأمر باليد هو البائن؛ لأنه كناية ولا يقع بالكناية إلا الواحد البائن كذا في "المعدن". بسبب أن أمرَها بيدك فكان الثاني وهو قوله: "فطلقها" هو الطلاق المفوض ، ولا يكون توكيلاً بالطلاق غير الأول، فلا تقع إلا واحدة، ولو كان للعطف تقع تطليقتان طلقت إلخ أي يقع في هذه الصورة الطلاق الواحد الرجعي؛ لأنه تفويض لصريح الطلاق وهو رجعي، والأمر باليد بيان له تطليقتين لأن قوله: "طلقها" توكيل لصريح الطلاق، وقوله: "جعلت أمرها بيدك تفويض الطلاق إليه، لكنه ليس بحكم الأول؛ لأن الواو لا يحتمل ذلك، فكان الثاني غير الأول فصار المأمور وكيلاً بتطليقتين: أحدهما: بائن وهو الأمر باليد كناية، والأخرى: فإذا قال في المجلس طلقتها، فقد أتى بما وكل به وهو الطلقتان فوقعتا، لكنه يكون كلاهما بائنين؛ لأن الرجعي يصير بائناً مع البائن؛ لأنه إذا وقع البائن فلا رجعة بعده ترجيحاً للمحرم كذا في "المعدن". رجعي؛ لأنه صريح
And in Al-Jami': If a man says, 'The matter of my wife is in your hand, so divorce her,' and he divorces her in the session, one irrevocable divorce occurs, and the second phrase is not an agency... (followed by similar cases and commentary explaining the nuances of fa, waw, and the number/type of divorces).

Commentary
This passage is an istinbat (derivation) of legal rulings about divorce delegation, using the meaning of 'fa'. The author presents cases from Al-Jami' al-Saghir of Imam Muhammad. When the husband says 'Your hand controls my wife's matter, so divorce her,' the 'fa' makes the delegation the reason for the divorce, so one irrevocable (ba'in) divorce occurs. If he says 'Divorce her, and I have put her matter in your hand,' using 'waw' (and), two divorces occur because the phrases are independent. The commentary meticulously explains how the conjunction used affects the number (one vs. two) and type (ba'in vs. raj'i) of divorce, upholding Hanafi principles of interpreting particles.

Key Terms
التفويض (al-tafwīḍ): Delegation of authority to the wife to divorce herself; here, the husband delegates the power to a third party.
البائن (al-bā’in): Irrevocable divorce that ends the marriage immediately without right of return.

A) Two divorces, both raj'i.
B) One irrevocable (ba'in) divorce.
C) One revocable (raj'i) divorce.
D) Two divorces, one ba'in and one raj'i.

Key Takeaway
The particle 'fa' in delegation phrases causes the divorce to be a single irrevocable one, whereas 'waw' yields two divorces, both becoming irrevocable.

The Meaning of 'Thumma': Delay in Pronunciation and Ruling
Usul dispute on the particle 'thumma'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi dispute over 'thumma' and apply the two positions to conditional divorce scenarios.

Core Idea
'Thumma' implies delay (tarakhi) — but is it in both utterance and ruling, or only the ruling?

The conjunction 'thumma' (then) seems simple, but its legal effect in conditional statements divides the Hanafi school. This lesson unpacks the dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two foremost disciples, the Sahibayn, on whether 'thumma' delays only the ruling or also the pronunciation. Understanding this dispute reveals how a small linguistic nuance can change divorce outcomes dramatically.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue
What does 'thumma' mean in terms of delay (tarakhi)? Is it delay in the utterance (lafz) alone, or also in the ruling (hukm)?
The disagreement stems from the nature of the word 'thumma' — does it connect speech or separate it?
2
Analyze the first example: condition before the divorces
Consider the statement: 'If you enter the house, you are divorced, then divorced, then divorced.' Work through Abu Hanifa's view: only the first is conditioned; the second occurs immediately; the third is invalid. Then work through the Sahibayn's view: all three are conditioned, but only one occurs upon entry.
Pay attention to the marital status (non-consummated wife) and how it affects the count.
3
Analyze the second example: condition after the divorces
Now reverse the order: 'You are divorced, then divorced, then divorced if you enter the house.' Apply both views. Under Abu Hanifa, the first occurs immediately (unconditional), and the rest are invalid. Under the Sahibayn, all are conditioned, so only one occurs upon entry.
The position of the condition changes the attachment of the divorces.
4
Consider the case of a consummated wife
When the wife is consummated, the waiting period (idda) allows revocable divorces. See how Abu Hanifa's view adapts: if the condition is presented, the first is conditioned and two occur immediately; if delayed, two occur immediately and the third is conditioned. The Sahibayn hold all are conditioned regardless.
The consummated wife's status allows more divorces to be effective before irrevocability.
5
Derive the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: under Abu Hanifa, fewer divorces are attached to the condition, so the condition triggers fewer actual divorces. Under the Sahibayn, all are attached, but the sequence means only one takes effect because the wife becomes irrevocably divorced after the first.
Both sides reach a similar practical outcome in many cases, but the legal reasoning differs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في ثم: وعندهما يفيد التراخي في الحكم. دون اللفظ. وبيانه: فيما إذا قال لغير المدخول بها: "إن دخلت الدار فأنت طالق ثم طالق ثم طالق"، فعنده يتعلقُ الأولى بالدخول، وتقع الثانية في الحال ولغت الثالثة، وعندهما: يتعلق الكل بالدخول، ثم عند الدخول يظهرُ الترتيب فلا يَقَعُ إلا واحدة. ولو قال: "أنت طالق ثم طالق ثم طالق إن دخلت الدار" فعند أبي حنيفة وقعت الأولى في الحال ولغت الثانية والثالثة، وعندهما يقع الواحدة عند الدخول؛ لما ذكرنا. وإن كانت المرأة مدخولاً بها، فإن قدَّم الشرط تَعَلَّقَت الأولى بالدخول ويقَعُ ثنتان في الحال عند أبي حنيفة، وإن أخر الشرط وقع ثنتان في الحال، وتعلقت الثالثة لبقاء المحل لوجود العدة بالدخول، وعندهما: يَتَعَلَّقُ الكُلُّ بالدخول في الفَصْلَيْن.
Section on 'Thumma': According to the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), it conveys delay in the ruling, not in the pronunciation. Its explanation: In the case where he says to a woman who has not been consummated: 'If you enter the house, you are divorced, then divorced, then divorced,' then according to him (Abu Hanifa) the first is attached to the entry, the second occurs immediately, and the third is invalid. According to the two, all are attached to the entry, then upon entry the order appears, so only one occurs. If he said: 'You are divorced, then divorced, then divorced if you enter the house,' then according to Abu Hanifa the first occurs immediately and the second and third are invalid. According to the two, one occurs upon entry due to what we mentioned. If the woman was consummated, then if he presented the condition, the first is attached to the entry and two occur immediately according to Abu Hanifa. If he delayed the condition, two occur immediately and the third is attached due to the existence of the place (mahl) because of the waiting period with consummation. According to the two, all are attached to entry in both cases.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage is a usul rule explaining the meaning of the word 'thumma' (then) in conditional statements, specifically in the context of divorce (talaq). The author presents the dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn). The core issue is whether 'thumma' implies delay (tarakhi) only in the ruling (hukm) or also in the pronunciation (lafz). Abu Hanifa holds that 'thumma' indicates both delay in pronunciation and in the ruling, meaning that the speaker is understood to pause after each statement. Therefore, when a conditional statement is made with 'thumma', only the first part is attached to the condition; subsequent parts are independent and occur immediately. The Sahibayn hold that 'thumma' only implies delay in the ruling, not in pronunciation, so all parts are spoken together and all are attached to the condition; upon fulfillment of the condition, they occur in sequence. The passage provides two examples: one with the condition before the divorce statements, and one with the condition after. In the first example, a man says to a non-consummated wife: 'If you enter the house, you are divorced, then divorced, then divorced.' According to Abu Hanifa, the first divorce is conditioned on entry; the second and third are unconditional: the second occurs immediately (since she is not yet divorced), and the third is invalid because the wife has become irrevocably divorced (bainuna) by the second. According to the Sahibayn, all three are conditioned on entry; upon entry, they occur in order, but only one is effective because the wife becomes irrevocably divorced after the first, so the second and third are invalid. In the second example, the condition is after the statements: 'You are divorced, then divorced, then divorced if you enter the house.' According to Abu Hanifa, the first occurs immediately (as it is unconditional), and the second and third are invalid because the wife is already irrevocably divorced. According to the Sahibayn, all are attached to the condition, so only one occurs upon entry. The passage also considers a consummated wife: here, Abu Hanifa holds that when the condition is presented, the first is conditioned and two occur immediately; when the condition is delayed, two occur immediately and the third is conditioned (because the wife remains in waiting period and thus is still eligible for revocable divorce). The Sahibayn hold that all are attached to entry in both cases. This dispute illustrates the usul principle of interpreting particles and their effect on the connection of speech.

Key Terms
ثم (thumma): A conjunction meaning 'then', indicating sequential order; in this context, it is debated whether it implies delay in pronunciation and ruling or only in ruling.
تراخي (tarakhi): Delay; the temporal gap between the occurrence of two events. In the context of 'thumma', it can apply to pronunciation (utterance) or ruling (legal effect).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn, when a man says to his non-consummated wife, 'If you enter the house, you are divorced, then divorced, then divorced,' how many divorces occur upon entry?
A) Three divorces.
B) Two divorces.
C) One divorce.
D) No divorce.

Key Takeaway
The meaning of 'thumma' in conditional statements is debated: Abu Hanifa holds it delays both pronunciation and ruling, while the Sahibayn hold it delays only the ruling — a difference that can change the legal outcome in divorce cases.

The Particle 'Bal' in Hanafi Usul: Rectifying Error in Reports, Not Performatives
Defines 'bal' as a particle for rectifying errors with a distinction between ikhbar and insha'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul principle that 'bal' (with negation) rectifies error only in ikhbar (informative statements) and not in insha' (performatives), and identify the rulings for divorce utterances using 'bal' with and without preceding negation.

Core Idea
Bal rectifies error only in reports, not in performatives like divorce.

In Hanafi usul, the particle 'bal' (rather, but rather) serves to correct an error in a preceding statement, but only if the statement is a report (ikhbar) that can be true or false. Performatives (insha'), such as divorce, create legal reality and cannot be rectified. This distinction leads to clear rulings when 'bal' is used with or without the negation 'la'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the function of 'bal'
The passage states that 'bal' is used for rectifying error (tadaruk al-ghalat) by placing the second statement in the position of the first. Recognize this definition.
2
Distinguish between ikhbar and insha'
Only reports can contain error and thus be rectified; performatives create reality and cannot. Apply this distinction to the examples given.
Reports: statements that can be true/false. Performatives: utterances that effect a legal change (e.g., divorce, sale, marriage).
3
Analyze the divorce example with 'la bal'
Trace the ruling: 'You are divorced, no rather twice' — the second part is a separate performative that fails because the wife is already divorced and because rectification is invalid in insha'. Conclude: only one divorce occurs.
4
Analyze the divorce example without 'la'
When 'bal' is used without preceding 'la', it indicates turning away (i'rad) from the first statement, not rectification. Since turning away is invalid in performatives, the first statement stands. Conclude: the wife is divorced once, as per the first utterance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في بل: "بل" لتدارك الغَلَط باقامة الثاني مقام الأول. فإذا قال لغير المدخول بها: "أنت طالق واحدة لا بل ثنتين" وقعت واحدة؛ لأن قوله: "لا بل ثنتين" إنشاء طلاق ثان في المحل وهذا يتصور حقيقة التكلم بهما في المحل من غير فصل يمكن يجريه منعقداً، فإنه لو أراد إيقاع طلاق ثان لم يمكن لأن المحل فات من الأول هذا على أن كلمة "بل" لتدارك الغلط، ولا يصح تدارك الغلط في الإنشاءات وإنما يصح في الإخبارات، لأن الإخبار يحتمل الخطأ والإنشاء لا يحتمله. ولو قال: "أنت طالق بل ثنتين" من غير "لا" فالأصح أنه يرجع إلى واحدة على الأظهر؛ لأن كلمة "بل" إذا لم تسبق بنفي لا تصلح لتدارك الغلط بل للاعراض عن الأول ضرورة، والإعراض لا يصح في الإنشاء فيبقى الأول على حاله.
Section on 'Bal': 'Bal' is for rectifying error by placing the second in the position of the first. So if he says to a non-consummated woman: 'You are divorced once, no, rather twice,' one divorce occurs; because his statement 'no, rather twice' is an initiation of a second divorce in the same place, and this is imagined truly by uttering both in the same place without a separating pause that could be considered, but if he intended to effect a second divorce it is not possible because the place was lost by the first. This is because the word 'bal' is for rectifying error, and rectifying error is not valid in performatives (insha'at); it is only valid in reports (ikhbarat), because reports can contain error while performatives cannot. If he said: 'You are divorced, rather twice' without 'no,' then the most sound is that it refers back to one, because the word 'bal' when not preceded by negation is not suitable for rectifying error but rather for turning away from the first necessarily, and turning away is not valid in performatives, so the first remains as it is.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage is a usul definition explaining the function of the word 'bal' (rather/but rather) in Arabic, particularly in legal utterances like divorce. The author states that 'bal' is used to rectify an error (tadaruk al-ghalat) by substituting the second statement for the first. However, this rectification is only valid in statements that are reports (ikhbar), which can be wrong, not in performatives (insha'), which bring about a legal effect and cannot be retracted. Three examples illustrate this: first, if one says 'divorced once, no, rather twice' to a non-consummated wife, only one divorce occurs because the second part is a separate performative that fails due to loss of the object (the wife becomes irrevocably divorced after the first). Second, the explanation clarifies that 'bal' with a preceding 'la' (no) is for rectification. Third, if one says 'divorced, rather twice' without 'la', the first stands because 'bal' here indicates turning away (i'rad) rather than rectification, and turning away is invalid in performatives. Thus, the lesson highlights the importance of distinguishing between reports and performatives in legal language.

Key Terms
بل (bal): A particle used for rectifying error (tadaruk al-ghalat) by canceling the first statement and replacing it with the second, but only in contexts where error is possible (reports).
إنشاء (insha'): A performative utterance that brings about a legal effect immediately upon utterance, such as divorce, sale, or marriage.
إخبار (ikhbar): An informative statement that describes a reality and can be true or false; it is open to error.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When a person says to his wife (who is not consummated) 'You are divorced, no, rather twice,' how many divorces occur according to Hanafi madhhab?
A) Two divorces, because 'bal' corrects the first to two.
B) One divorce, because the second is invalid as a performative and the first stands.
C) No divorce, because the statement is contradictory.
D) Three divorces, because of the repetition.

Key Takeaway
Bal with negation rectifies error only in reports, not in performatives; without negation, it indicates turning away and leaves the first statement intact.

The Effect of 'Bal' on Insha' and Khabar
Analysis of 'bal' in divorce and acknowledgment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between insha' and khabar and apply the Hanafi rule that 'bal' cannot retract insha' but can correct khabar.

Core Idea
Insha' creates an irreversible legal reality; 'bal' cannot undo it. Khabar admits correction.

The particle 'bal' (بل) seems like a simple retraction, but its legal effect depends entirely on the nature of the speech act. This passage from Usul al-Shashi illustrates the Hanafi distinction between insha' (performative speech, like divorce) and khabar (informative speech, like acknowledgment), showing why 'bal' has different consequences in each. Understanding this distinction is fundamental to applying rules of legal interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two speech acts
Locate the examples of divorce and acknowledgment in the passage. Note that divorce is classified as insha' (creative speech) and acknowledgment as khabar (report).
Insha' brings a new legal reality into existence; khabar describes a pre-existing fact.
2
Apply the rule of 'bal' to each
For divorce: 'bal' is ineffective because the first divorce has already occurred irrevocably. For acknowledgment: 'bal' can correct the amount because error is possible in reports.
The rule is: 'bal' is for error correction (tadāruk al-ghalat). In insha', there is no error to correct because the act itself creates the reality.
3
Compare the outcomes
For a non-entered wife, only one divorce occurs; the second statement is void. For acknowledgment, the second amount replaces the first (Hanafi position), so total liability is the second amount (2,000), not a sum (3,000). Zufar disagrees, holding both effective.
The practical result differs: Hanafi says 2,000; Zufar says 3,000.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute
Zufar's view: 'bal' replaces but the first cannot be invalidated, so both remain (3,000). The majority Hanafi view: 'bal' replaces, so only the second stands (2,000). The passage supports the majority view.
The dispute hinges on whether the first acknowledgment can be cancelled.
5
Derive the underlying principle
The nature of the speech act determines the possibility of retraction. Insha' yields an immediate, irreversible effect; khabar remains open to correction because truth/falsehood is possible.
This principle governs other speech acts like sales, gifts, and oaths.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في بل
لعدم صحة الرجوع عنها
"لا بل ثنتين" رجوع عن الأول بإقامة الثاني مقام الأول، ولم يصح رجوعه فيقع الأول فلا يَبْقى المَحَلُّ عند قوله : "ثنتين"، ولو كانت مدخولاً ما يقعُ الثلاث. وهذا بخلاف ما لو قال: "لفلان عليَّ ألف لا بَلْ ألفان" حيث لا يجب ثلاثة آلاف عندنا، وقال زُفَر له: يجب ثلاثة ألاف؛ لأن حقيقة اللفظ لتدارك الغلط بإثبات الثاني مقام الأول، ولم يصح عنه إبطال الأول، فيجب تصحيح الثاني مع بقاء الأول، وذلك الإقرار بالألف بطريق زيادة الألف على الألف الأول، بخلاف قوله: "أنت طالق واحدة لا بل ثنتين"؛ لأن الكلام إنشاء لا يمكن إبطاله بعد التكلم بدون جعله في حكم المسكوت عنه؛ لأنه قد وجد وصدر منه ما لا مبرر له، ولا يمكنه إعدامه أي: إبقاؤه على عدمه الأصلي؛ لأنه ينسلخ عن أصله بالوجود فلا كأن لم يكن، وأما العدم اللاحق فلا يضر الوجود؛ لأن الوجود يتحقق في زمانه، وإذا تحقق وجب ترتب حكمه عليه وهو وقوع الطلاق، لامتناع تخلف الحكم عن سببه؛ لأنه موجب له بل زمان وجوده عين زمان وجوده كذا في كتب الأصول.
يقَعُ الثلاث: لأنه لا يمكن الرجوع، فيقع الأول والأخيران معه بخلاف ما إذا كانت المرأة غير مدخول بها حيث تقع واحدة؛ لأنه لا يصح الرجوع عنها فوقعت واحدة وتبين بها ولا تبقى محلاً عند قوله: "ثنتين"، فلا يقعان كذا في "الفصول". ولم يصح عنه إلخ لبطلان إنكار بعد الإقرار؛ لما قال علي: المرء يؤخذ بإقراره لكن إقراره بألفين على وجه إقامتهما مقام الأول صحيح لاقتضاء كلمة "بل". فيجب إلخ: أي فيلزمه الألفان مع الألف الأول كما لو قال علي ألف درهم بل ألف دينار، فيلزم المالان لاختلاف الجنس. بخلاف قوله أنت إلخ يعني إذا قال بغير المدخول بها: "أنت طالق واحدة لا بل ثنتين"، تقع الواحدة؛ لأنه إذا قال: "أنت طالق واحدة، وقعت واحدة، ولا يمكن الإعراض عنه، ولما كانت هي غير موطوءة فلا عدة لها، فلم يبق المحل فيلغو ما بعده؛ لأن الطلاق إنشاء، والغلط إنما يكون في الإخبار دون الإنشاء؛ لأنه إيجاد أمر لم يكن، وبعد ما وجد شيء لا يمكن تداركه بأن يجعل باقياً على عدمه، فأما الخبر يحتمل الصدق والكذب فيمكن تداركه بالصدق ونفي الكذب، فأمكن تصحيح الغلط بتدارك الغلط في الإقرار دون الطلاق، حتى لو كان الطلاق بطريق الإخبار يقع ثنتان؛ لما قلنا: إن تدارك الغلط في الإخبار ممكن كذا في "المعدن".
Section on 'Bal' [But Rather]: For the lack of validity of retraction from it. 'No, but two' is a retraction from the first by establishing the second in place of the first, and his retraction is not valid, so the first occurs and the place is not left at his saying 'two'. And if she was [already] entered upon, three [divorces] do not occur. This is contrary to if he said: 'For so-and-so upon me is one thousand, no but two thousand' — three thousand are not obligated according to us, and Zufar said: three thousand are obligated because the reality of the word is to correct error by establishing the second in place of the first, and his invalidation of the first is not valid, so the second must be validated along with the remainder of the first, and that acknowledgment of the thousand is by way of adding the thousand to the first thousand, contrary to his saying: 'You are divorced once, no but twice' — because the speech is a creation [insha'] which cannot be invalidated after speaking without making it in the ruling of silent about; because it has occurred and emanated from him that which has no justification, and he cannot annihilate it i.e. keep it on its original non-existence; because it is stripped from its origin by existence so it is as if it was not. As for subsequent non-existence, it does not harm existence; because existence is realized in its time, and when it is realized, its ruling must be attached to it, which is the occurrence of divorce, because it is impossible for the ruling to be detached from its cause; because it is its consequence, rather the time of its existence is identical to the time of its existence. So it is in the books of usul. 'Three occur': because retraction is not possible, so the first and the last two occur with it, contrary to if the woman was not entered upon, where one occurs; because retraction from her is not valid, so one occurs and she becomes separated by it and no place remains at his saying 'two', so they do not occur. So it is in 'al-Fusul'. 'And it is not valid from him etc.' because denial after acknowledgment is invalid; because Ali said: a person is bound by his acknowledgment. But his acknowledgment of two thousand on the basis of establishing them in place of the first is valid due to the requirement of the word 'bal'. So 'it is obligated etc.' i.e. both thousand and the first thousand are binding, as if he said: upon me a thousand dirhams, bal a thousand dinars — both properties are binding due to difference of genus. Contrary to his saying: 'You are divorced once, no but twice' — meaning if he says to one not entered upon: 'You are divorced once, no but twice', one occurs; because when he says 'you are divorced once', one occurs and he cannot ignore it, and since she is not cohabited, there is no 'iddah, so the place does not remain and what follows is void; because divorce is a creation, and error only occurs in reports not in creations; because it is bringing into existence something that was not, and after something exists it cannot be rectified by making it remain as non-existent. As for a report, it admits truth and falsehood, so it can be rectified by truth and negation of falsehood. Thus correction of error is possible in acknowledgment but not in divorce. Even if the divorce was by way of report, two occur; because we said: correction of error in reports is possible. So it is in 'al-Ma'dan'.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage from al-Shashi's Usul addresses the legal effect of the particle 'bal' (بل, 'but rather' or 'nay') when used in speech acts like divorce (طلاق) and financial acknowledgment (إقرار). The author explains that 'bal' functions as a retraction of the first statement by replacing it with the second. The core distinction is between insha' (إنشاء, performative/legally creative speech) and khabar (إخبار, report/informative speech). Divorce is insha' — once uttered, it creates a legal effect (e.g., one divorce occurs) and cannot be undone by 'bal'. Acknowledgment is khabar — it can be corrected because error is possible. Thus, in 'أنت طالق واحدة لا بل ثنتين' to a non-menstruating woman (غير مدخول بها), only one divorce occurs; the second word is void. In acknowledgment, 'لفلان علي ألف لا بل ألفان' obligates three thousand? Actually, the Hanafi position (and Zufar's dispute) is that it obligates two thousand plus the original? The text says: according to us (at our place, i.e., Abu Hanifa?), the total is two thousand (not three). Zufar said three thousand. The reasoning: 'bal' invalidates the first and establishes the second, but since the first cannot be invalidated in insha', it remains, but in khabar it is abrogated. The correct Hanafi view is that the first thousand is acknowledged, and the second is an addition, so total two thousand. Zufar's reasoning would make it three. The author supports the Hanafi view by noting that 'bal' is for error correction, but does not eliminate the first when the statement is a creation. He also provides an analogy with different currencies. The lesson emphasizes that the nature of the speech act determines whether 'bal' can retract or simply add.

Key Terms
إنشاء (insha'): Performative speech that creates a legal effect immediately upon utterance, e.g., divorce.
إقرار (iqrar): Acknowledgment or confession, considered a report (khabar) about a past fact, thus susceptible to correction by 'bal'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When a man says to his wife (not yet entered upon): 'You are divorced once, no but twice', how many divorces occur according to Hanafi fiqh?
A) One divorce
B) Two divorces
C) Three divorces
D) No divorce

Key Takeaway
The effect of 'bal' depends on whether the speech act is insha' (irreversible) or khabar (correctable).

The Rule of 'Lakin' for Istidrak after Negation
Explaining the use of 'lakin' for istidrak after negation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the function of 'lakin' in Hanafi usul, distinguish it from 'bal', and apply the rule that 'lakin' affirms what follows without negating what precedes.

Core Idea
'Lakin' states a contrast without erasing the first ruling; 'bal' replaces it.

This passage from al-Shashi's Usul defines the particle 'lakin' used for istidrak (rectification/exception). It is a key tool for understanding how contradicting clauses interact in legal texts. By analyzing the conditions and effects of 'lakin', we can interpret texts more precisely and avoid conflating it with 'bal'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition for 'lakin' usage
Note that 'lakin' is for istidrak after negation when conjoining single words. For sentence conjunction, it can be after negation or affirmation.
The type of conjunction (word vs. sentence) determines the rule.
2
Recognize the effect of 'lakin'
Its effect is to affirm what follows; the negation of what precedes remains from the negation particle itself.
'Lakin' does not negate the first part; it only adds an affirmation for the second.
3
Distinguish from 'bal'
'Bal' nullifies the previous ruling, whereas 'lakin' does not. 'Bal' can be used after both affirmation and negation.
This is crucial for legal interpretation: 'bal' abrogates, 'lakin' does not.
4
Understand the requirement of speech coherence
The speech must be coherent (ittisaq al-kalam), meaning it must be connected and not contradictory, requiring two contrasting concepts.
If the speech is disjointed, the conjunction is not valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في لكن
"لكن" للاستدراك بعد النفي فيكون موجبه إثبات ما بعده ، فأَمَّا نَفْي ما قبله فثابت بدليله.
والعطف بهذه الكلمة إنما يتحقق عند اتساق الكلام، فإن كان الكلام متسقاً أي لكن للاستدراك إلخ هذا اصطلاح الخليل، أي: طلب درك السامع بدفع ما عسى أن يتوهم من الكلام السابق، فلا بد له من مفهومين متخالفين، فلو عطف بها مفرد على مفرد وجب وقوعها بعد النفي كما أشار إليه في المتن كما في ما جاءني زيد لكن عمرو أي جاءني عمرو، ولو عطف بها جملة على أخرى جاز الأمران فيها وقوعها بعد النفي وبعد الإثبات، والتخالف أعم من أن يكون بالإيجاب والسلب أو ما يجري محراهما من التخالف بين الثبوتيتين كالزوجية والفردية والإنسانية والفرسية وهي إن كانت مخففة فهي عاطفة، وإن كانت مشددة فهي مشبهة مشاركة للعاطفة في الاستدراك.
من بعد النفي إلخ والله در المصنف الله حيث أشار بهذا الكلام إلى أمرين أحدهما محل استعماله، وثانيهما: بيان موجبه، فأشار إلى الأول بقوله : بعد النفي، وإلى الثاني بقوله: فيكون موجبه إثبات ما بعده، وغرض المصنف الله هذه الإشارة: بيان الفرق بين "لكن" و"بل"، وهو: أن "لكن" لا يستدرك بها بعد الإيجاب، و"بل" يقع بعد الإيجاب والنفي، والثاني أن موجب "لكن" إثبات ما بعده ، وأما لنفي ما قبله فثابت بدليله لا بكلمة "لكن" كما مر ، بخلاف "بل"؛ فإنه يوجب نفي الأول وإثبات الثاني بوضعه وهذا أي: الاستدراك بلكن بعد النفي في عطف المفرد على المفرد فإن كان في الكلام جملتان مختلفان نفياً وإثباتاً جاز الاستدلال بلكن في الإيجاب أيضاً كما جاز في "بل" كذا قيل. فثابت بدليله: أي ثابت بدليل كلمة النفي، أما إذا عطفت بها جملة على جملة؛ فإنها مثل "بل" في النفي والإثبات، إلا أن كلمة "بل" تبطل الحكم السابق وكلمة لكن لا تبطله. [الشافي: ص ١٧٧] عند الساق الكلام أي انتظامه من وسق الشيء إذا جمعه وذلك لشيئين: أحدهما: أن يكون الكلام متصلاً ومرتبطاً بعضه ببعض غير منفصل ليتحقق العطف، والثاني: أن يكون محل الإثبات غير محل النفي ليمكن الجمع بينهما، ولا يناقض آخر الكلام أوله كما في قولك: جاءني زيد لكن عمرواً لم يأت كذا في "مجمع الحواشي".
Section on 'Lakin' [But/However]: 'Lakin' is for istidrak (rectification/exception) after negation, so its effect is to affirm what follows it. As for negating what precedes, that is established by its own evidence. Conjunction with this word is only realized when the speech is coherent. If the speech is coherent — that is, 'lakin' for istidrak etc. — this is the terminology of al-Khalil, i.e., seeking the listener's comprehension by repelling what might be assumed from previous speech. Thus it requires two contrasting concepts. If one conjoins a single word to another single word, it must occur after negation, as indicated in the matn, as in 'Zaid did not come to me, but Amr came' i.e., Amr came. If one conjoins a sentence to another sentence, both possibilities are allowed: its occurrence after negation and after affirmation. The contrast is general, whether by affirmation and negation or analogous to them, like contrast between two positive things such as marital and single, human and horse. If it is lightened (mukhaffafa), it is a conjunction; if it is emphasized (mushaddada), it is a resemblance particle that shares with the conjunction in istidrak. 'After negation etc.' — may Allah reward the author, who by this statement indicated two matters: one is its place of usage, the second is its effect. He indicated the first by his saying 'after negation', and the second by his saying 'so its effect is to affirm what follows'. The author's purpose with this indication is to explain the difference between 'lakin' and 'bal': that 'lakin' is not used for istidrak after affirmation, while 'bal' occurs after affirmation and negation. Second, the effect of 'lakin' is to affirm what follows, while negating what precedes is established by its own evidence, not by the word 'lakin' as mentioned, contrary to 'bal', because it necessitates negation of the first and affirmation of the second by its very nature. This — i.e., istidrak with 'lakin' after negation — is for conjoining a single word to a single word. If there are two sentences different in negation and affirmation, it is permissible to use 'lakin' in affirmation also, just as it is permissible in 'bal'. So it is said. 'Established by its own evidence' i.e., established by the evidence of the negation particle. However, if one conjoins a sentence to a sentence, then it is like 'bal' in negation and affirmation, except that the word 'bal' nullifies the previous ruling, while 'lakin' does not nullify it. [al-Shafi: p. 177] 'When the speech is coherent' i.e., its organization, from wasaqa (to gather). That is due to two things: one, that the speech must be connected and linked part to part, not separate, to realize conjunction; second, that the place of affirmation must be different from the place of negation to allow combining them, and the end of the speech must not contradict its beginning, as in your saying: 'Zaid came to me, but Amr did not come'. So it is in 'Majma' al-Hawashi'.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This section defines the particle 'lakin' (لكن) used for istidrak (الاستدراك, rectification or exceptional correction) after negation. The author explains that 'lakin' is primarily used after a negation to affirm what follows; the negation of the first part is already established by the negation particle itself. The word 'lakin' does not negate the first; it only adds an affirmation for the second. This is contrasted with 'bal' (بل), which in its typical usage negates the first and affirms the second. The passage also distinguishes between the usage when conjoining single words (عطف المفرد) versus sentences (عطف الجملة). For single words, 'lakin' must come after negation; for sentences, it can appear after both negation and affirmation. Additionally, the particle's form matters: 'lakin' with tashdid (مشددة) is a resemblance particle (حرف مشبهة) that shares istidrak with the conjunction, while with tashkil? Actually, the text says مخففة (lightened) is عاطفة (conjunction), and مشددة (emphasized) is مشبهة (resemblance particle). This is a standard usul distinction. The passage cites al-Khalil (the grammarian) for the terminology. It also references al-Shafi (likely al-Shafi'iya?) and 'Majma' al-Hawashi'. The key takeaway is that 'lakin' does not abrogate the previous ruling like 'bal'; it simply adds a contrast. This is important in legal interpretation: when a condition is negated and then an exception is made with 'lakin', the negation stands, and the exception is affirmed separately.

Key Terms
استدراك (istidrak): Rectification or correction by amending a prior understanding; used with 'lakin' and 'bal' to adjust meaning.
اتساق الكلام (ittisaq al-kalam): Coherence and connection of speech, requiring it to be continuous and logically consistent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, which particle can nullify the previous ruling when used for istidrak?
A) 'Lakin' (لكن)
B) 'Bal' (بل)
C) Both
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
'Lakin' affirms its own content without affecting the validity of the preceding negation, making it distinct from 'bal' which replaces the prior ruling.

The Particle 'Lakin' in Legal Speech: Coherence and Ruling
Analyzing how 'lakin' affects coherence of legal statements

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul rule on the particle 'lakin' to determine whether a legal statement is coherent or disconnected, and derive the resulting ruling (e.g., validity of confession or contract).

Core Idea
The particle 'lakin' produces conjunction only when speech is coherent: negation and affirmation must not apply to the same thing.

This lesson explores how a single Arabic particle, 'lakin' (but), can determine the validity of legal statements. The Hanafi usul rule distinguishes between connected (wāṣil) and disconnected (fāṣil) speech based on coherence. By understanding the conditions for coherence, you will be able to analyze complex legal utterances and derive correct rulings in cases of confession (iqrār) and contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the utterance containing 'lakin'
Locate the statement with 'lakin' and note the affirmative and negative parts. Determine if negation and affirmation are both present.
2
Assess connection (waṣl) vs. disconnection (faṣl)
Check if the speech is delivered as one continuous utterance without a significant pause. If there is a break (e.g., by silence or starting a new sentence), it is disconnected.
Connection in utterance is one pillar of coherence.
3
Check for contradiction: negation and affirmation on the same thing
If negation and affirmation apply to the exact same act, object, or right (e.g., both the marriage itself), contradiction arises, breaking coherence. If they apply to different attributes (e.g., different prices or conditions), coherence is preserved.
The second pillar of coherence: negation and affirmation must not attach to the same thing.
4
Apply the ruling
If both pillars of coherence are present (connection and no contradiction), 'lakin' acts as a conjunction, and the entire statement is treated as one coherent utterance with a unified legal effect. If either pillar is missing, 'lakin' indicates a new independent statement, and the legal effect is split accordingly.
Examples: in the slave confession, connected speech gives the slave to the second confirmee; in the marriage authorization, disconnected/contradictory speech voids the contract.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في لكن
فإن وصل الكلام كان العبد للمقرّ له الثاني؛ لأن النفي يَتَعَلَّقُ بالإثبات، وإِن فَصَل كان المقر الأول العبد للمقرّ الأول، فيكون قول المقر له ردًّا للإقرار.
ولو أنْ أمَةً تزوَّجَتْ نفسها بغير إذن مولاها بمائة درهم، فقال المولى: "لا أجيز العقد درهم، ولكن أجيزه بمائة وخمسين" بَطَلَ العقد؛ لأن الكلام غير متَّسَقِ، فَإِن نَفي بالإجازة وإثباتها بعينها لا يتحقق، فكان قوله: "لكن أجيزه" إثباته بعد ردّ العقد.
في كل واحد
فإن وصل إلخ يكون الكلام متسقاً؛ لأن مدار الاتساق على ما قيل مجموع أمرين: الاتصال بالسابق في التكلم، وعدم تعلق النفي والإثبات بشيء بعين حتى لا يبقى التناقض والتدافع ولو بحسب الظاهر فقط، فعند فقدان أحد الأمرين لا يبقى الاتساق بل يعد كلاماً مستأنفاً فتدبر.
للمقر الأول: وهو من في يده العبد؛ لأن المقر الأول إذا فصل وقطع كلامه كان نفياً لملكه مطلقاً أي نفياً عن نفسه أصلاً لا نفياً إلى أحد، بخلاف ما إذا وصل، فإنه وإن كان شهادة الفرد لكنه لما أقر بالملك للغير متصلاً بالنفي عن نفسه صار الكل بمنزلة كلام واحد، فيكون تقديم الإقرار وتأخيره سواء، فيجعل كأنه قدم الإقرار بالملك لفلان صيانة لكلام العاقل عن الإلغاء كذا في "المعدن".
قول المقرّ له وهو قوله: ما كان لي قط في صورة الفصل ردًّا للإقرار وتكذيباً للمقر حملاً للكلام على الظاهر ويكون قوله لكنه لفلان بعد ذلك شهادة بملك الثالث على ذي اليد وشهادة الفرد لا يثبت الملك لاسيما إذا كان بلا دعوى الملك، فبقي العبد ملكاً له كذا في "المعدن".
لا أجيز العقد: يعني أن الأمة إذا تزوجت بغير إذن مولاها بمائة درهم فقال المولى: "لا أجيز النكاح بمائة درهم ولكن أجيزه بمائة وخمسين"، فقوله: "لا أجيزه نفي العقد وفسخ للنكاح، وقوله: "ولكن أجيزه" إلخ إثبات العقد، والإثبات والنفي في محل واحد محال، فجعل "لكن" حينئذ مبتدأ؛ لأن هذا نفي فعل وإثباته بعينه، توضيحه: لما قال المولى:" ولا أجيز العقد" فقد قلع النكاح عن أصله و لم يبق له وجه صحته، ثم لما قال بعده: "ولكن أجيزه بمائة وخمسين" يلزم أن يكون إثبات ذلك الفعل المنفي بعينه؛ لأن المهر في النكاح تابع لا اعتبار له، فيتناقض أول الكلام بآخره، فحملناه على ابتداء النكاح بمهر أخر ، وفسخ النكاح الأول الذي عقدته، فيكون كلمة "لكن" حينئذ للاستيناف لا للعطف، ولو قال المولى في جوابها لا أجيز النكاح بمائة ولكن أجيزه بمائة وخمسين، يكون هذا بعينه مثال الاتساق، فبقي أصل النكاح ويكون النفي راجعاً إلى قيد المائة والإثبات إلى قيد المائة والخمسين، فلا يكون في صورة الوصل نفي فعل وإثباته بعينه فتدبر كذا قيل. بَطَلَ العقدُ: لأن الكلام غير متسق أي: غير مرتبط بما قبله أي من حيث المبنى وإن كان متصلاً صورة.
وكذلك لو قال: "لا أجيزه ولكن أجيزه إن زدتَّنِي خمسين على المائة" يكون فسخـــاً
للنكاح؛ لعدم احتمال البيان؛ لأن من شرطه الأنساق ولا اتساق.
Chapter on 'But'.
If the speech is connected, the slave belongs to the second confirmee; because negation attaches to affirmation. And if it is disconnected, the first confirmer's slave belongs to the first confirmee, so the statement of the confirmee is a rejection of the confirmation.
And if a female slave married herself without her master's permission for one hundred dirhams, and the master said: 'I do not authorize the contract for a dirham, but I authorize it for one hundred and fifty,' the contract becomes void; because the speech is not coherent. For negation of authorization and affirmation of the very same thing cannot be realized. So his saying 'but I authorize it' is an affirmation after rejection of the contract.
In each one...
If he connects, etc., the speech becomes coherent; because the basis of coherence, as has been said, is the combination of two matters: connection with what precedes in speech, and the non-attachment of negation and affirmation to the same thing such that there remains no contradiction or repulsion, even if only apparently. So when one of the two matters is missing, coherence does not remain; rather it is considered independent speech. So reflect.
For the first confirmer: he is the one in whose hand the slave is; because when the first confirmer disconnects and cuts off his speech, it is an absolute negation of his ownership, i.e., a negation from himself entirely, not a negation to someone else. Unlike when he connects, for even though it is the testimony of an individual, since he confessed ownership for another connected to the negation from himself, the whole becomes like one statement. So whether the confession is advanced or delayed is the same; it is made as if he advanced the confession of ownership for so-and-so to preserve the speech of a rational person from being nullified. As in 'al-Ma'din'.
The statement of the confirmee—his saying 'it was never mine' in the case of disconnection—is a rejection of the confession and a denial of the confirmer, interpreting the speech literally. And his saying 'but it is for so-and-so' afterwards is a testimony of ownership of a third party against the possessor, and the testimony of an individual does not establish ownership, especially if without a claim of ownership. So the slave remains his ownership. As in 'al-Ma'din'.
'I do not authorize the contract': meaning that if the female slave married without her master's permission for one hundred dirhams, and the master said: 'I do not authorize the marriage for one hundred dirhams, but I authorize it for one hundred and fifty,' then his saying 'I do not authorize it' is a negation of the contract and an annulment of the marriage, and his saying 'but I authorize it' etc. is an affirmation of the contract. Affirmation and negation regarding the same thing are impossible. So 'but' is made here as a beginning; because this is a negation of an action and an affirmation of the very same. Explanation: when the master said 'I do not authorize the contract', he uprooted the marriage from its foundation and left no aspect for its validity. Then when he said afterwards 'but I authorize it for one hundred and fifty', it would necessitate affirming that very same negated action; because the dower in marriage is subsidiary and has no consideration. So the beginning of the speech contradicts the end. So we interpret it as beginning a new marriage with a different dower, and annulling the first marriage she contracted. Thus the word 'but' here is for starting anew, not for conjunction. And if the master said in response: 'I do not authorize the marriage for one hundred, but I authorize it for one hundred and fifty,' this is exactly the example of coherence. So the basis of the marriage remains, and the negation refers to the restriction of one hundred and the affirmation to the restriction of one hundred and fifty. So in the connected form it is not negation of an action and affirmation of the very same. So reflect, thus it was said. The contract becomes void: because the speech is not coherent, i.e., not connected with what precedes, i.e., in terms of structure, even if it is connected in form.
And likewise if he said: 'I do not authorize it, but I authorize it if you increase me by fifty on the hundred,' it is an annulment of the marriage; because there is no possibility of clarification; for its condition is coherence, and there is no coherence.
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage from Usul al-Shashi is explaining the legal effect of the particle 'lakin' (but) in legal speech. The author analyzes two scenarios: connected speech (mawsul) and disconnected speech (munfasil). The key principle is that coherence (ittsāq) requires both connection in utterance and that negation and affirmation do not attach to the same thing. The first example involves a confession (iqrār) about a slave: if the speaker says 'the slave is not mine but it is Zayd's' in one connected statement, the negation applies to the affirmation, so the slave goes to the second person. If the speaker disconnects by pausing or starting anew, the first part is a negation of ownership and the confession is rejected by the confirmee. The second example involves a master's permission for a female slave's marriage: if the master says 'I do not authorize the marriage for 100 dirhams but I authorize it for 150', the contradiction is clear because the marriage itself is the same; thus 'lakin' here indicates a new start, voiding the original marriage. If he says it in a connected way with conditions, coherence is maintained. The passage emphasizes the importance of textual coherence in legal interpretations.

Key Terms
اتساق (ittsāq): Coherence of speech: connection in utterance and lack of contradiction between negation and affirmation regarding the same thing.
فصل (fasl): Disconnection in speech, either by pause or by starting a new utterance, breaking the coherence.
وصل (wasl): Connection in speech, where the utterance is continuous without interruption and negation and affirmation are not on the same object.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example of the female slave's marriage, why does the master's statement 'I do not authorize it for a dirham but I authorize it for one hundred and fifty' void the contract even though he says 'but'?
A) Because the slave's consent was not obtained.
B) Because negation and affirmation apply to the same act (the marriage), causing contradiction.
C) Because the dower cannot be changed after marriage.
D) Because the master did not specify the new dower clearly.

Key Takeaway
The particle 'lakin' either connects or disconnects speech based on two conditions: utterance continuity and non-contradiction between negation and affirmation; this determines whether a legal statement is treated as a single coherent unit or as separate utterances.

The Particle 'Aw' in Legal Speech: Ambiguity and Explanation
Explaining that 'aw' indicates one of two without specification, requiring clarification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi position on the particle 'aw', distinguish it from the doubt interpretation, and apply the rule to legal statements involving choice or ambiguity.

Core Idea
The particle 'aw' legally designates one of two unspecified alternatives—not doubt—leaving the speaker with the authority to clarify.

This passage from Usul al-Shashi tackles a fundamental linguistic issue: what does 'aw' (or) mean in legal texts? The Hanafi school holds that 'aw' indicates one of two things without specification, not doubt. This distinction matters because it determines who has authority to clarify—the speaker—and how rulings are applied in performative contexts like freeing slaves or appointing agents. Understanding this rule is essential for correctly interpreting Quranic commands and contractual language.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two competing meanings of 'aw'
Note that the main dispute is whether 'aw' literally means 'one of the two without specification' (Hanafi) or 'doubt' (some grammarians/usulis). Identify each position clearly.
The Hanafi position is that doubt is not a conventional meaning; it arises only from context in informatives.
2
Examine the Hanafi proof from linguistic usage
Analyze the examples: freeing a slave, agency, and Quranic verse ('kill yourselves or leave your homes'). Notice that in each case, the ruling applies to one of the two without further specification, requiring the speaker's clarification.
In performatives, 'aw' indicates choice; in informatives, clarification is needed.
3
Follow the dispute and the opposing argument
Learn the opposing school's claim that 'aw' is for doubt, based on everyday usage. Then see the Hanafi rebuttal: doubt is a state of the speaker, not a meaning of the word.
The author calls the opposing view 'not sound' (laysa bi-sadid).
4
Apply the rule to the examples given
Work through the text's examples: a man says 'This is free or that' → he has authority to specify. An agent is told 'sell to Zayd or Bakr' → the agent may choose without further specification because 'aw' in performatives gives choice.
The difference between substitution (freedom case) and generality (agency case) is nuanced.
5
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf
Consider the practical difference: if 'aw' indicated doubt, the speaker's utterance might be considered ambiguous or invalid. Under Hanafi, the speaker retains authority (wilāyat al-bayān) to clarify, making the ruling enforceable.
This principle affects how judges handle ambiguous statements.
